## Page 55

Reading Texts                                                             41

considered to be one of the prisci theologi.9 However, Pico does not mention
the magical texts ascribed to Pythagoras, some of which he could have known
through Marsilio Ficino’s milieu. Moreover, Pico omits the whole prisca theo-
logia tradition prior to Pythagoras, including Zoroaster, Hermes Trismegistus
and Orpheus, all of whom are associated with magical texts and beliefs. To
prove Pythagoras’ anti-astrological position, he instead refers to traditional au­
thors, namely Diogenes of Laertius, Plutarch and Theodoret of Cyrus. From
the beginning of the Disputationes, Pico almost exclusively refers to classical
authors not associated with any contemporary novelties, either philosophical
or astro-magical; this should definitely be considered his major argumenta­
tive principle. Thus, mentioning other ancient opponents of astrology, Pico
refers to Cicero’s De divinatione, Aulus Gellius’ Noctes Atticae and Diogenes’
Lives. He quotes them, usually word for word, in ascribing Panetius, Carneades,
Epicurus and Democritus to the camp of anti-astrologers.10 Pico’s aim seems to
have been dual. On the one hand, he intended to confirm that every influential
school in ancient philosophy rejected astrology. This strategy explains why he
goes as far as to mention Epicurus; Pico argues that those who believe in as­
trology are infinitely insane, because even Epicurus, the most insane amongst
philosophers, had no faith in it.11 On the other hand, he excludes the prisci
theologi from his discourse and generally works through traditional sources.
  He also adopts the approach first developed in the De ente et uno by declar­
ing that neither Plato nor Aristotle ever supported astrological predictions. In
the Disputationes, Pico argues that both Greek philosophers, when they are in­
terpreted independently from the Platonic and Aristotelian schools, transmit­
ted doctrines that were very different from what subsequent traditions made

## Page 56

42                                                   Chapter 3

of them. This principle determines the structure of Book I, in which Pico ex­
plicitly avoids any connection between Plato and Aristotle and their followers
on the problem of astrology. In the case of Plato and Aristotle, Pico’s polemical
strategy is contradictio in contrarium: their total silence on astrology becomes
an argument against it. Pico enumerates Aristotelian treatises and points to
the lack of any mention of astrology.12 He uses the same argument in Plato’s
case.13 In this way, Pico concludes that both Plato and Aristotle totally re­
jected astrology, geomancy, incantations and all other forms of superstition.14
  Pico establishes a strict distinction between the original philosophy of Plato
and Aristotle and that of their interpreters. Rather than focusing on the way
these interpreters analysed the work of their masters, he focuses on their re­
spective treatment of astrology. For instance, when he analyses Alexander of
Aphrodisias’ work, he does not focus on how Alexander interpreted Aristotle,
but rather on how Alexander, seen as an individual author, considered astrol­
ogy. This approach is strikingly different from the previous (and subsequent)
Aristotelian tradition, which treated Alexander as an interpreter of Aristotle, to
be followed or rejected. In his analysis of Alexander’s thought, Pico recognises
that Alexander did not attack astrology in particular, but he nonetheless un­
derlines that Alexander rejected the notion that human actions were governed
by fate, which consequently implied a rejection of judicial astrology.15 Here,
Pico uses the same polemical strategy as in the case of Plato and Aristotle: he

Reading Texts                                                    43

considers that Alexander did not mention astrology throughout his De fato as
an indication that Alexander was against it.
   In Book I of the Disputationes, Pico analyses Plotinus in a similar fashion:
he does not present him as an interpreter of Plato but focuses exclusively on
his personal views on astrology, which are, in turn, considered in the context
of the debate between Porphyry and Firmicus Maternus.16 Considering the
arguments of Porphyry and Firmicus, he takes the side of Porphyry; following
Porphyry, Pico claims that after a careful study of astrology, Plotinus finally
rejected it. Pico also denies Firmicus’ supposition about the astrological pre­
diction of Plotinus’ death.17
  Throughout the Disputationes, Pico’s references to Neoplatonic philoso­
phers are rare. Porphyry is mentioned a number of times, mainly as Plotinus’
biographer or as a proponent of astrological speculation. In Book XII, Pico crit­
icises Porphyry’s fascination with astrology. The name of Iamblichus appears
in the Disputationes only once, although Pico adopts Iamblichus’ arguments in
analysing the problem of celestial light without quoting his source. Pico’s use
of Plotinus is also limited. Thus, his attitude towards the Neoplatonic tradi­
tion in the Disputationes seems to be equivocal: he follows his critical strategy
to show that all major philosophical schools, including the ‘familia platonica’,
rejected astrology, but he does not explore, at least not openly, Neoplatonic
arguments against it. A brief overview of Plotinus’ views becomes just an ep­
isode in the history of astrological debates, whilst the philosophical doctrine
underpinning his views is not analysed.
   In fact, Pico’s goal to prove the agreement amongst the leading auctori-
tates regarding the falsity of astrological predictions leads to a paradox: whilst
questioning the very existence of ‘tradition’ in its various philosophical and

pro-astrological forms, Pico uses the notion of an anti-astrological tradition
to defend the idea that astrological predictions have no validity. In the end, he
constructs a new, inverted tradition, which fits his purpose.
  He consequently refers to two major Eastern authorities, namely Avicenna
and Averroes,18 as well as to Eudoxus of Cnidus, ‘the one who heard Plato’,19
and Origen, the first Christian author in Pico’s anti-astrological list. This enu­
meration suggests that prominent figures from all religions (Christian, Islamic
and pagan) rejected astrology. At least three of these names are reminiscent of
the Conclusiones’ stage of Pico’s career. Connecting Averroes’ and Avicenna’s
positions on astrology, he clearly refers to his project of reconciling the Islamic
philosophers, which he declated in the 900 Conclusiones, but never initiated.
Origen represents a more personal and complicated case. It is known that for
Pico, Origen’s persona was of great importance. In the 900 Conclusiones, he de­
fends Origen, whom the Church had officially accused of heresy. The thesis on
Origen’s innocence was one of the thirteen conclusiones banned by the papal
commission. This caused Pico’s extensive response, which forms the core of
the Apologia. In comparison with all other chapters of the Apologia, for Pico
himself, the one on Origen looks more ambitious and important. It is probable
that by defending the thinker accused of heresy because of his innovative and
radical thought, Pico actually defended his own views and pictured himself
as a new Origen.20 After the failure of his Roman project, Pico only mentions

Origen twice in the Heptaplus: first amongst the disciples of Ammonius
and then as one of the Greek authors who composed a commentary on the
Book of Genesis.21 The passage in question is the sole fragment after the 900
Conclusiones and the Apologia in which Pico labels Origen as a Christian au­
thor who ‘succeeded in philosophy and many other disciplines’, and focuses,
albeit still briefly, on Origen’s views.22
  Going back to the Disputationes, Pico then proceeds to provide an overview
of the ‘modern’ adversaries of astrology. Here, Pico mentions several calcula-
tores from the University of Paris of the fourteenth century: Nicolas Oresme,
Henry of Ghent and other members of the ‘Paris Academy’. What is striking
in the passage in question is that he had criticised these authors in his ear­
lier writings.23 The reason Pico treats these sources differently here, which is
still debated today, is due to the polemical context in which he operates in
the Disputationes. His intention was to focus specifically on the question of
astrology rather than on how to interpret the philosophical doctrines; in doing
so, he could argue for the continuity of the anti-astrological tradition from the
ancient times through medieval scholarship to his own era.
  Pico then turns to the presence of anti-astrological works amongst his
contemporaries. In this section, he mentions Giovanni Marliani, a leading
Milanese natural philosopher, and Paolo del Pozzo Toscanelli.24 Both authors’

names are used in order to show that natural philosophers rejected astrology.
Pico goes on to mention Niccolò Leoniceno, Angelo Poliziano and Marsilio
Ficino.25 Although Leoniceno’s interest in astrology, or more likely in its refu­
tation, was marginal, he played a crucial role in the debates between two
groups of the so-called medical humanists. Controversies on the dominance
of either Latin or Greek tradition in teaching and practicing medicine marked
sixteenth-century Renaissance thought.26 Giovanni Pico himself was amongst
those who applied a similar approach with regard to astrology and questioned
the status of the Greek and Latin-Arabic traditions in the Disputationes. Pico’s
approach determined the deconstruction of the so-called ‘astrological tradi­
tion’, initiated, according to Giovanni Pico, with Ptolemy’s writings and later
misunderstood and distorted by his followers.
  Giovanni Pico’s mention of the two other scholars, Angelo Poliziano
and Marsilio Ficino, represents an important case in the controversies on
astrology.27 Poliziano’s astrological studies still need to be explored in depth.
It is known that in 1490–91, Poliziano lectured on Aristotle’s Ethics and accom­
panied his work with an introduction to the Ethics entitled Panepistemon.28

There, Poliziano intended to present a classification of all arts and knowledge.29
This encyclopedia includes a number of significant passages on divination, as­
trology and other forms of occult knowledge, which Poliziano rejects with the
use of both philosophical and theological arguments.30 In Pico’s own words,
Poliziano’s lectures on Aristotle incited him to compose the De ente et uno.31
Considering Pico’s close friendship with Poliziano, he must have been famil­
iar with his friend’s studies. It is hard to confirm whether Pico used some of
Poliziano’s arguments against astrology; however, by mentioning that he met
with Poliziano to mock astrology,32 Pico clearly reveals that they jointly criti­
cise astrology.
  What is also crucial is that Poliziano was an addressee of Marsilio Ficino’s
famous letter on astrology written in 1494, after the Disputationes started to
circulate amongst the Florentine intellectual elite.33 In his last treatise, Pico
admits that Ficino, despite his critical attitude towards astrology, practiced it
for medical purposes without truly believing in it. He adds that Ficino’s real in­
tention was to help his patients. Thus, Pico obviously alludes to the astrological
techniques described in detail in the De vita; on the other hand, Pico claims that
Ficino fully supported his attempt to refute astrology. This passage, along with
the parallelism between the titles of both writers’ works (Ficino’s Disputatio
contra iudicium astrologorum and Pico’s Disputationes adversus astrologiam
divinatricem), encouraged D.P. Walker to presume that Ficino was a likely

## Page 62

48                                                   Chapter 3

influence on Pico, stimulating him to embark on his anti-astrological treatise.
Chronology and the fact that the Disputationes represents a more focused and
many-sided opposition to astrology contradict Walker’s hypothesis;34 more im­
portantly, there is no evidence that Pico could have read Ficino’s Disputatio,
which was unknown until the twentieth century.35 For some modern inter­
preters of astrological controversies in Renaissance Florence, the passage in
question signifies Pico’s attempt to direct his anti-astrological attack against
Ficinian astral magic expressed in the De vita. However, this interpretation
overlooks the development of both Pico’s and Ficino’s views on astrology and,
more generally, the context of their philosophical outlook in the early 1490s.
   It is probable that the passage on Ficino in the Disputationes should not be
interpreted as a part of the polemics between Ficino and Pico. As is known,
Ficino’s late works on astrology, including the De vita and the De Sole, caused a
disagreement between himself and ecclesiastical authorities, and even forced
Ficino to write two apologies to defend the ‘orthodoxy’ of his position on the
subject.36 Pico’s statement that, despite the use of astrology for medical pur­
poses, Ficino did not believe in it can also be regarded as an attempt to protect
his elder colleague from the opponents’ attacks. To support this interpretation,
it is worth observing Pico’s Latin expression in the passage: according to his
words, he can ‘witness’ in favour of his friend that Ficino’s thought contra­
dicts astrology. In this context, the term ‘witness’ obviously obtains a juridical
dimension.
   In 1494, Ficino wrote a letter to Angelo Poliziano trying to explain his ‘real
views’ on astrology. In secondary literature, this letter is usually considered to
be another apology for his astro-magical views. However, the form of expres­
sion Ficino chose for the letter leaves most of his arguments rather unclear.
He admits the use of astrological images for medical purposes in the De vita,

## Page 63

Reading Texts                                                   49

but he insists that he refuses the very essence of astrological predestination.
In his own words, following Plotinus and other Platonic thinkers, he mocks
astrology.37 In general, with some literary variations, Ficino repeats Pico’s
words taken from the passage of the Disputationes. Ficino’s attempt to clarify
his attitude towards the celestial influence on the sublunar world did not re­
ceive a response from either Poliziano or Pico. A couple of months after Ficino
sent the letter, both Poliziano and Pico suddenly died.
  Thus, Pico constructs the anti-astrological tradition in philosophy, which
consists of three major groups: ancient philosophers, medieval scholastics and
his contemporaries. However, he does not limit his discourse against astrology
to philosophers. The second group of Pico’s anti-astrological sources contains
religious texts. Although throughout the Disputationes, Pico reveals his excel­
lent knowledge of the New Testament, the quotations from the Old Testament
are full of inaccuracies. At the same time, despite failing to provide precise
quotations, Pico never modifies the meaning of biblical passages. A typical
example is the Book of Deuteronomy 18:14.38 In Pico’s text, the passage is as
follows: ‘Gentes augures et divinos audiunt; tu autem a Domino Deo aliter es
institutus’, although the full quotation is ‘Gentes istae, quarum possidebis ter­
ram, augures et divinos audiunt: tu autem a Domino Deo tuo aliter institutus
es’ (These nations, whose land thou shalt possess, hearken to soothsayers and
diviners: but thou art otherwise instructed by the Lord thy God). Apparently,
Pico quoted the fragment from memory without contradicting the meaning
of the biblical narrative. This approach was widespread in Pico’s intellectual
milieu: for instance, in the De religione christiana, Marsilio Ficino refers to a
significant range of biblical allusions and passages, most of which are either
indirect or incomplete.39
  Apart from biblical passages, Giovanni Pico also appeals to the legacy of
the Church Fathers. Amongst the whole range of the Church Fathers, he puts
Augustine first. In Book I of the Disputationes, Augustine is the only Father
granted a careful and detailed analysis as regards his anti-astrological views,
with a list of his treatises against predictions attached. Instead of limiting him­
self to a couple of texts, Pico mentions at least six works in which Augustine
rejects astrology as a dangerous and bad superstition.40 This is no coincidence;

Augustine was the first to argue that pagan religion and Neoplatonic theurgy
were superstitious and evil. All other religious authors enumerated later in the
same chapter are given almost without any particular details, and they merely
serve to confirm the position of the Church against astrology. Pico mentions
but briefly Basil of Caesarea, Tertullian, John Chrysostom, Ambrose of Milan
and Eusebius.41 The names of less known religious writers, including the Syrian
gnostic Bardaisan,42 Didymus the Blind43 and Sidonius Apollinaris, appear
unexpectedly but for the same purpose. Thus, in the section on his religious
sources, Pico singles out Augustine as the main authority in refuting astrol­
ogy. The variety of Augustine’s texts mentioned and analysed in Book I of the
Disputationes clearly demonstrates Pico’s theological orientation towards
the bishop of Hippo. All other authors serve exclusively to prove the conso­
nance amongst major Christian theologians on the question of astrological
predictions. It is also worth noting that in Book I of the Disputationes, Pico

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 65

Reading Texts                                                               51

virtually disregards scholastic philosophy and specifically Thomas Aquinas,
whose authority, along with Augustine’s, Pico used to support his attack on re­
ligious claims of astrology. In the Disputationes, Thomas Aquinas is considered
a crucial authority in the context of both the critique of medieval European
astrologers, who tried to prove Christian dogmas with the use of astrological
calculations and practices, and the refutation of pro-astrological arguments
taken from natural philosophy and Aristotle. What makes the mention of
Thomas Aquinas particularly emblematic in this respect is that beginning in
the thirteenth century, his attitude towards astrology was seen as ambivalent.
On the one hand, he admitted that the stars might influence terrestrial events
and even incline human lives to one direction or the other; on the other hand,
he followed Augustine in refuting predictive practices.44 With the matter left
hanging in the air, Thomas’ position was widely employed to legitimise astrol­
ogy. Supposedly endorsed by the Doctor Angelicus, the greatest scholastic the­
ologian, this discipline could get the green light to become an integral part of
Christian teaching. The uncertainty regarding Thomas’ stance on the value
of astrology incited Pico’s response in the Disputationes, which is in line with
Pico’s two main argumentative strategies. First, he frames Thomas as a major
theological authority within the long-standing anti-astrological tradition; sec­
ond, he argues that over the centuries, astrologers inverted the actual essence
of Thomas’ position to justify their field of knowledge. His aim appears to be a
thorough investigation of Thomas’ legacy and an eventual unveiling of the fact
that all the pro-astrological interpretations of his views are false and should
be firmly dismissed. As shown below, Pico did the same thing with regard to
another principal authority: Aristotle. These details indicate that despite the
significant number of religious writers that Pico claims for his side, Augustine
and Thomas Aquinas are given prominence in the Disputationes.
   Finally, canon and civil law decrees form the third group of anti-astrological
sources in Book I of the Disputationes. This is the only occasion when Pico
reveals his expertise in both laws that he studied, without much success, at
Bologna in 1478.45 Canonical resolutions apparently taken from Gratian’s

       cially 29–35.

     annum ageret, matris iussu, quae sacris eum initiari vehementer optabat, discendi iuris
       pontificii gratia Bononiam se transtulit.’





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 66

52                                                   Chapter 3

Concordia discordantium canonum46 are cited together with the Code of
Justinian, according to which the work of astrologers was considered abomi­
nable, and at the emperor’s court, it was prohibited to consult them under the
threat of torture and death.47
   In summary, in the first book of the Disputationes, Pico intends to prove that
no one amongst the best philosophers or theologians ever supported the very
idea of predictions. The same intention underlies the structure of the whole
treatise. Pico pays special attention to the philosophical tradition of refutation
of astrology, which, according to him, began with Pythagoras and lasted until
his own time. Amongst religious authorities, he mentions two most impor­
tant theologians, Augustine and Thomas Aquinas, who appear in the context
of Giovanni Pico’s criticism of Christian astrology, as well as in the refutation
of pro-astrological arguments taken from natural philosophy and Aristotle.
Finally, the juridical solutions borrowed from both canon and civil law had to
confirm the philosophical and theological arguments.
   In Book I of the Disputationes, Pico develops a number of methodological
approaches he widely uses throughout the whole treatise. In his brief history of
anti-astrology, he reconstructs the ‘anti-astrological tradition’, which consists
of three major groups, namely ancient philosophers, Christian theologians and
his contemporaries. He effectively uses it as a polemical tool to demonstrate
that the foremost thinkers of the past universally acknowledged astrology as

      per astrolabium cuiusdam ecclesiae furto investigando aliquid esset perscrutatus, quam­
     quam, ut idem Pontifex testatur, nullam ibi aut magicam aut aliam superstitionem admi­

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 67

Reading Texts                                                     53

a dangerous superstition. At the same time, nothing prevents him from chal­
lenging the status of traditio when it comes to the philosophical, theological
and mathematical arguments with which astrologers intended to justify their
discipline. In other words, from a methodological point of view, Pico’s ap­
proach seems to be inconsistent; however, the way he questions the validity of
the prisca theologia concept or that of the ‘astrological tradition’ is very much
in line with the method he first applied in exploring the Plato-Aristotle recon­
ciliation in the De ente et uno.
   It should also be noted that the analysis of the texts presented above clearly
indicates that Pico was not operating within the same context as Savonarola
and Gianfrancesco Pico. His aim was not to show that pagan philosophy as
a whole was to be rejected in favour of Christianity, nor was it to prove that
paganism could not lead to true knowledge. In the Disputationes, he metic­
ulously demonstrates that the astrological tradition had become so distorted
that one could no longer trust any of its representatives, and that the funda­
mental principles of astrology were based on wrong mathematical premises.
In doing so, he certainly provided elements that would be later exploited by
Savonarola and Gianfrancesco Pico. However, it is worth emphasising that the
ideological framework within which he developed his argument was markedly
different in that he, unlike Savonarola and his disciples, never rejected pagan
culture in toto.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 68

Chapter 4
Me quoque adolescentem olim fallebat: Giovanni
Pico della Mirandola versus Prisca theologia


1       Introduction

As we have seen above, Pico’s polemical strategy in the Disputationes deals
with the deconstruction of tradition in order to reconsider the legacy of tra-
ditional auctoritates and is similar to the method he uses in the De ente et uno.
His approach remains in force in Book XII of the Disputationes, in which he
dismisses a tradition that seemed central to his earlier works. This chapter is
focused on one of the most intriguing aspects of his attack, one that concerns
the prisca theologia doctrine. The last book of the Disputationes has never
been analysed from this perspective before. The second central topic that this
chapter investigates is the question of the Disputationes’ authorship. I intend
to explore possible intersections between Giovanni Pico’s and Gianfrancesco
Pico’s works as regards the prisca theologia doctrine attacked in Book XII of the
Disputationes adversus astrologiam.
  The last book of the Disputationes, in which Giovanni Pico considers the
origin of astrological speculation, is the most questionable in terms of his orig-
inal authorship. In this book, Giovanni Pico severely criticises the doctrine of
prisca theologia, which he had been loyal to during all his previous itinéraire
philosophique. What reinforces the doubts is that Giovanni Pico’s arguments
against prisca theologia as the origin of astrology would be later repeated in
Gianfrancesco Pico’s De rerum praenotione in an even more radical manner.
According to Gianfrancesco, who closely followed his uncle, curiosity is par-
ticular to human beings, but it might have a negative impact on people who
are far from objective knowledge. These people can easily fall under the ma-
lign influence of astrologers and magicians. Amongst those who are responsi-
ble for the development and diffusion of astrology, Gianfrancesco Pico names
the Egyptians, the Chaldeans and the Jews.1 Whereas Gianfrancesco’s attitude
towards prisca theologia is well known from the rest of his legacy, Giovanni
Pico’s radicalism in this question appears unexpected. The following section


1  Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola, Ioannis Francisci Pici Mirandulae domini et Concordiae
   comitis de rerum praenotione libri novem, p. 16.


© Koninklijke Brill NV, Leiden, 2021 | doi:10.1163/9789004442276_005  Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 69

Me quoque adolescentem olim fallebat                            55

will focus on Giovanni Pico’s critique of prisca theologia within the context of
his anti-astrological polemics.


2       Prisca theologia in Giovanni Pico’s Writings before the
       Disputationes adversus astrologiam divinatricem

First developed in early patristics in order to demonstrate the compatibility
of Christianity with pagan thought, the prisca theologia concept was pop-
ular amongst Renaissance thinkers of the late fifteenth and early sixteenth
centuries, who considerably renewed the meaning and significance of the
term.2 The prisca theologia doctrine that claims that a single, true wisdom
was transmitted over time through various ancient theologians, spreading
from Egypt and Persia to Greece and Rome, owes its renaissance in the West
to Georgius Gemistus, also known as Plethon. He was amongst the most suc-
cessful Byzantine émigrés.3 In his fundamental treatise, the Book of the Laws,
Plethon insists on the restoration of the cult of the ancient Greek gods.4 As
a result, he focuses on the chronology of the pagan period of prisca theolo-
gia, particularly on the legacy of Zoroaster and other prisci theologi. During
the Council of Florence, he attracted the attention of many prominent think-
ers and humanists. According to the idealised account, described in Ficino’s
commentary on Plotinus, it was Plethon who had advised Cosimo de’ Medici

   del Rinascimento italiano e la loro fortuna’, in Il pensiero italiano del Rinascimento e il tempo
   nostro, ed. by Giovannangiola Tarugi (Florence: Olschki, 1970), pp. 211–36; D. P. Walker, The
   Ancient Theology: Studies in Christian Platonism from the Fifteenth to the Eighteenth Century
   (London: Duckworth, 1972); Cesare Vasoli, ‘Dalla pace religiosa alla ‘prisca theologia”, in
   Firenze e il Concilio del 1439, ed. by Paolo Viti (Florence: Olschki, 1994), pp. 3–25; idem, ‘Prisca
   theologia e scienze occulte nell’umanesimo fiorentino’, in Storia d’Italia. Annali 25: Esoterismo,
   ed. by Gian Mario Cazzaniga (Turin: Einaudi, 2010), pp. 175–205; Wilhelm Schmidt-Biggemann,
   Philosophia perennis. Historical Outlines of Western Spirituality in Ancient, Medieval and Early

   The Evolution of Humanist Theology. 1461/2–1498 (Leiden; Boston: Brill, 2008).

to restore the Platonic Academy in Florence.5 Though the authenticity of the
legend and the existence of the Platonic Academy in Florence are subject to
dispute by modern scholarship, the passage clearly indicates that Plethon in-
fluenced Italian humanists and philosophers and that this influence impacted
the younger generation. Marsilio Ficino studied Plethon’s works in a manu-
script now kept in the Riccardiana Library in Florence.6 The most important
difference between Plethon and Ficino is that Ficino’s interpretation of prisca
theologia, which benefited from a direct knowledge of Plethon’s sources, was
primarily centred on Christian elements. Ficino also aimed to prove that upon
centuries of transformation and development, ‘ancient theology’ had in effect
become an integral part of Christianity.
   Marsilio Ficino claims in the introduction to his De religione christiana
that divine knowledge is often found amongst people who have little in com-
mon with Christianity.7 According to Ficino, the ideas expressed by such
prominent  prisci theologi as Zoroaster,8 Hermes Trismegistus, Orpheus,9

## Page 71

Me quoque adolescentem olim fallebat                             57

Aglaophemus and Pythagoras10 are in complete agreement. Ficino ascribed to
Plato, whose writings he believed contained a full description of the whole
prisca theologia doctrine, the central position of ‘mediator’ between pagan
antiquity and Christian theology.11 Ficino asserts that after the advent of
Christianity, Platonic mysteries, the meaning of which had been unclear to his
followers, along with the mysteries of other prisci theologi, were illuminated
through the Christian revelation.12
   Ficino insists that most prisci theologi, including the most significant one,
Plato, were rediscovered by Christians after not being properly understood
during their lifetime and completely forgotten after their death. His principal
intention, therefore, was to revive the legacy of the prisci theologi and sup-
plement it with Christian teaching. Given that most of these texts abound in
magical elements, it opened the door to magical speculation in Ficino’s and
his contemporaries’ works. Thus, when exploring celestial influences upon the
terrestrial world, Ficino generally combines traditional medieval astrological
texts with new, mainly Neoplatonic, sources.13 This novel reading of astrology
formed the basis for late fifteenth-century disputes on the status of astrology,
seen from that moment on not as a ‘handmaid’ of theology, as it had been
in the Middle Ages, but as its predecessor. The prisca theologia concept in its
relation with astrology and magic can be observed throughout all of Ficino’s
major writings. His younger contemporary, Giovanni Pico, became the most
important recipient of the Ficinian doctrine and went on to enrich it with new
Kabbalistic sources.
  The influence of the prisca theologia doctrine is present in the entire body
of work of Giovanni Pico della Mirandola. The only exception seems to be the
Disputationes. Giovanni Pico was largely responsible for the formation of the
Christian Kabbalah during the Renaissance.14 His interest in Kabbalistic texts

## Page 72

58                                                   Chapter 4

and his fame as one of the most prominent intellectuals of his time stimu-
lated the learning of Hebrew in Italy and across Europe. Amongst those who
fell under his influence, Johann Reuchlin,15 Jacques Lefèvre d’Étaples16 and
Francesco Zorzi17 are to be mentioned in primo loco.
   In the Kabbalah and other related ‘ancient’ doctrines, Giovanni Pico saw al-
ternative sources to prove the essence of the Christian religion. The idea of rec-
onciling all possible philosophical and theological schools under the only true
doctrine, that is, Christianity, occupied Giovanni Pico from the Roman dispute
of 1486 until his later theological writings. In Giovanni Pico’s own words, ‘the
Hebrew wisemen’s own principles’, along with other prisci theologi, should be
considered for the benefit of Christian doctrine. One of the most remarkable
examples of this can be found in the tenth Kabbalistic thesis, in which Pico
tries to find theological analogies to the Son of God by combining the views
of at least six prisci theologi.18 Giovanni Pico also justifies some Christian dog-
mas using Kabbalistic astrology. For example, as we have seen above, Pico re-
fers to the Arabic practice of using the horoscopes of Christ,19 whereas in the
eighteenth Kabbalistic thesis, Pico suggests an astrological reason to celebrate
the Sabbath on Sunday, instead of Saturday as it is done in Jewish religious
communities.20

## Page 73

Me quoque adolescentem olim fallebat                           59

  At the beginning of the Oratio de hominis dignitate, in commenting on the
central place of man in the universe, Giovanni Pico states that the Arabs, the
Persians, the biblical prophets and even Hermes Trismegistus are in full ac-
cordance on this subject.21 Unfortunately, the Oratio has become hostage to
scholarly disputes on Renaissance humanism and is often regarded exclusively
from that point of view. The question of Giovanni Pico’s use of sources is cru-
cial for understanding the philosophical significance of the Oratio. Giovanni
Pico remains loyal to the medieval interpretation of microcosm and macro-
cosm and is obviously familiar with the Christian tradition of placing man at
the centre of the universe. Neither the central position of man nor the possi-
bility of defining oneself according to one’s own free will, both characteristic
of Christian theology, can be considered Giovanni Pico’s novel idea. Instead,
the basis of Pico’s thought, at least at the first two stages of his philosophical
career, was formed by the doctrine of the new Adam taken from saint Paul and
the compatibilities of prisci theologi and Kabbalists with the Christian religion.
   After having settled in Florence, Giovanni Pico continued to introduce
the Kabbalistic tradition into Christian discourse. He did not put aside the
prisca theologia model in his later theology-loaded writings. As was men-
tioned above, in the Heptaplus, in which he commented on the Book of
Genesis using Kabbalistic texts rather than drawing upon traditional exegesis,
Giovanni Pico claims that elements of Moses’ doctrine can be found amongst
the Egyptians, from whom they were later transmitted to the most prominent
Greek philosophers.22 Giovanni Pico’s aspiration was to confirm the existence
of the prisca theologia and its transmission from Moses through ancient phi-
losophers to his own time.
  The influence of the Kabbalah on the content and structure of the Heptaplus
becomes evident through two main points. First, Pico’s treatise consists of
forty-nine chapters, which fits with the forty-nine ‘gates of wisdom’ described
in Jewish mystical texts. According to Moses ben Nahman, these ‘gates of wis-
dom’ correspond to the forty-nine levels of knowledge revealed to Moses on
Mount Sinai.23 This idea was primarily diffused within the Spanish Kabbalistic
branch in the thirteenth century and was then transmitted to Italy. Abraham
Abulafia and Mehanem ben Recanati, both well known to Giovanni Pico,
are amongst its most significant supporters. Another important Kabbalistic


21   Idem, Discorso sulla dignità dell’uomo, pp. 2–4.
22   Idem, ‘Heptaplus’, pp. 170, 178–80.
23    Black, Pico’s Heptaplus and Biblical Hermeneutics, pp. 223–30; Brian Ogren, ‘The Forty-
     Nine Gates of Wisdom as Forty-Nine Ways to Christ: Giovanni Pico della Mirandola’s
      Heptaplus and Nahmanidean Kabbalah’, Rinascimento, 49 (2009), 27–43.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 74

60                                                   Chapter 4

feature of the Heptaplus is its analysis of the word bereshit, ‘in the beginning’,
which is the first word of the Torah.24 On the one hand, Giovanni Pico claims
that after having discovered the forty-nine ‘gates of wisdom’, one would be able
to reach God. The square of seven is forty-nine and, following the Kabbalistic
interpretation of numbers, Giovanni Pico associates seven with the Sabbath
or the highest position of contemplation and reunion with God. On the other
hand, the last, forty-ninth chapter of the Heptaplus directly addresses the sig-
nificance of bereshit. Pico’s interpretation reflects an essential theological and
mystical doctrine: the end reveals itself through the beginning. Giovanni Pico
was already familiar with this idea, having used it in the Oratio when present-
ing the concept of the ‘new Adam’.
  Giovanni Pico interprets the word bereshit using magical combinatorial
techniques. In his opinion, it is possible to combine each letter of the word
with another letter and thus form an independent Hebrew word that consists
of two letters taken from bereshit. Through this technique, Giovanni Pico cre-
ates twelve different words united into one phrase: ‘The Father, in the Son and
through the Son, the beginning and end or rest, created the head, the fire, and
the foundation of the great man with a good alliance’.25 The phrase, which
concerns Christ, undoubtedly contradicts the core of Jewish mysticism. For
Giovanni Pico, however, it is of great intellectual importance. Demonstrating
the agreement between Kabbalistic and Christian sources on the same sub-
ject, critical for both traditions, he does not seek to convert Jewish people to
Christianity; his only aim is to substantiate the truth of his own religion. Finally,
the unfinished Expositiones in Psalmos is rich in references to the Talmudic lit-
erature. It is worth noting that the impact of the Kabbalah on the Expositiones
is less evident. Be that as it may, the references to Abraham ibn Ezra and par-
ticularly to David Kimchi reveal that in the Expositiones, Giovanni Pico re-
mains under the influence of Jewish theology. In this context, the position of
Giovanni Pico’s nephew, Gianfrancesco Pico, regarding the Expositiones seems
surprising. He criticised his uncle for his admiration of the Kabbalah, at the
same time pointing out that the Expositiones was a turning point in Giovanni
Pico’s conversion to the true Christianity.26 It is likely that this assessment was
conditioned by the general lack of Kabbalistic or other magical sources in
the Expositiones. After some time, Giovanni Pico radically changed his mind


24   Giovanni Pico della Mirandola, ‘Heptaplus’, pp. 374–82.
25    Ibid., p. 378: ‘Pater in Filio et per Filium principium et finem sive quietem creavit caput
     ignem et fundamentum magni hominis foedere bono.’
26   Gianfrancesco Pico  della Mirandola,  Ioannis Francisci  Pici Mirandulae domini  et
      Concordiae comitis de rerum praenotione libri novem, p. 100.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 75

Me quoque adolescentem olim fallebat                                61

regarding the legacy of Abraham ibn Ezra, and in the Disputationes, he appears
as one of the main authorities in matters of astrology.
  Thus, the prisca theologia played a crucial role in Giovanni Pico’s thought
during the whole period of his intellectual activity, and the transformation of
his attitude towards it in the last book of the Disputationes adversus astrolo-
giam divinatricem seems, therefore, to contradict his earlier position. The de-
cision of Giovanni Pico or of the compiler of the last book of the Disputationes
to disprove the prisca theologia is ambiguous and generally contrasts with his
previous writings. In what follows, I shall provide a closer analysis of the way
in which Pico reconsiders the prisca theologia in the Disputationes. I argue that
in light of the analysis of Book I, we might be now capable of contextualising
Pico’s arguments against the prisca theologia doctrine and consequently put-
ting an end to the debates concerning the treatise’s authorship.


3    On the Origin of Astrology: Giovanni Pico della Mirandola versus
        Prisca theologia

In terms of stylistic coherence, the twelfth book of the Disputationes is far
below the Piconian standards. The Latin expression does not resemble the
language of the Oratio or that of the letter to Ermolao Barbaro.27 The text
contains numerous unnecessary repetitions and overlaps with important pas-
sages from other chapters of the Disputationes. However, a more careful read-
ing uncovers the book’s general structure. Despite the fact that Giovanni Pico
had no time to polish the text in question, his basic idea is clearly outlined.
First, he intends to focus on the reasons why ancient people, specifically the
Egyptians and the Chaldeans, became attached to astrological superstitions.
After examining once more several key terms used in astrological practice, he
observes the ways in which astrological knowledge was transmitted from an-
cient Eastern societies (i.e., the Egyptians and the Chaldeans) to Greece, the
Islamic world and then to the Christian West.


27    This famous letter was published: Prosatori latini del Quattrocento, pp. 804–23. For the full
      correspondence between Barbaro and Pico on this subject, see Quirinus Breen, ‘Giovanni
      Pico della Mirandola on the Conflict of Philosophy and Rhetoric’, Journal of the History of
       Ideas, 13, 3 (1952), 384–412. On the debates and their context, see Hanna Gray, ‘Renaissance
     Humanism: The Pursuit of Eloquence’, Journal of the History of Ideas, 24, 4 (1963), 497–514
      (507–10); Letizia Panizza, ‘Pico della Mirandola et Il Genere Dicendi Philosophorum del
       1485: L’encomio paradossale dei ‘barbari’ e la loro parodia’, I Tatti Studies: Essays in the
      Renaissance, 8 (1999), 69–103.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 76

62                                                   Chapter 4

  According to Giovanni Pico, his intention in the previous books of the
Disputationes was to show the dissent amongst astrologers regarding the ba-
sics of astrology. He claims that the question of its origin is still unclear. At
the same time, his arguments radically differ from his earlier views. Astrology,
in his opinion, inspires ‘us’ in accordance with ‘our merits’.28 This remark ap-
parently echoes Savonarola’s sermons and Gianfrancesco Pico’s writings, in
which contemporary issues were often interpreted through human sins and
faults. Giovanni Pico points to various possible origins of astrological specu-
lation. Apart from his permanent opponents—the Egyptians, the Chaldeans,
Ptolemy and the Arabs—he mentions the Jews. The attribution of astrological
superstition to this tradition, which Giovanni Pico esteemed all his intellectual
life, seems strange. Be that as it may, the author ultimately ascribes the rise of
astrology to the Egyptians and the Chaldeans.
   In the section concerned with the motivations for the ancients’ reliance
on astrology, the author explains that for a Christian, the fascination with as-
trological speculation is undoubtedly provoked by demons.29 The polemical
strategy of stigmatising pagan astrologers as supporters of diabolic knowledge,
which ultimately derives from Augustine, is not typical of Giovanni Pico and
does not agree with the rest of his written legacy. It is, however, characteristic
of his radical followers, namely Gianfrancesco Pico.
  However, there are instances in which the strategy used by the author of
Book XII is very similar to that used in previous books, which is to find con-
tradictions amongst different representatives of a given tradition in order to
undermine the validity of that tradition. For instance, according to the author
of the last book of the Disputationes, astrologers usually refer to a large variety
of stories on the origin of astrology to justify their doctrine. Amongst such sto-
ries, he mentions the legend that astrological knowledge was given to Adam
by God. It was later transferred to Noah, whose fourth son, Jonicus, according
to a certain Methodius, was an expert in astrological predictions and made
many of them.30 The legend about Jonicus, who was sometimes labelled ‘the

     doquidem, ut vidimus, tanta sit in decretis astrologiae dissensio, nec revelatum sit adhuc
     Chaldaeroum ne astrologia, an potius Aegyptiorum, an Iudaeorum, an Ptolemaei, an
     Arabum illa sit, quam munere singulari Dii nobis bene merentibus aspiraverunt.’

       interpretes interdicunt. Si respondeant a daemone, nulla fides daemoni, quem mendacii
     patrem Deus appellavit.’
30    Ibid., pp. 486–88: ‘Ad Ionicum veniamus. Scribit quidem de hoc Methodius, sed idoneus
     parum auctor; neque enim ille est, cuius meminit inter viros illustres divus Hieronymus;





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 77

Me quoque adolescentem olim fallebat                           63

first astronomer’, was relatively popular in the Middle Ages, though, given its
dubious origin, it was not generally accepted.31 Most probably, ‘Jonicus’ was a
personal name that was used by mistake because it is similar to the Latin word
ionicus (‘Ionic’) and was, therefore, used as a synonym for ‘Greek’. Be that as it
may, the Jonicus legend was one of numerous fictional stories about Noah’s dis-
ciples that populated the oikumene after the Deluge. Considering that this type
of literature reached its culmination in Giovanni Nanni’s Libri antiquitatum
variarum published for the first time in 1498,32 Pico’s desire to dismiss what
he considered a spurious biblical pro-astrological narrative is comprehensible.
   Astrologers insist, in Giovanni Pico’s opinion, that after Jonicus, the knowl-
edge of astrological practices was transmitted to Abraham, who then taught it
in Egypt.33 Giovanni Pico obviously disagrees with this chronology; for him,
to accept the Jewish origin of astrology is the same as to oppose the history of
Christianity. To undermine the validity of the testimony of Methodius, whose
interpretation of the history of astrology formed the basis for the supposi-
tion that it was of Jewish origin, he gradually deconstructs the whole tradi-
tion by showing that Methodius was not a reliable source. It is worth noting
that the person of Methodius, who was under such rigorous critique in the
Disputationes, cannot be identified with certainty.
  According to Pico, the Methodius once mentioned by saint Jerome should
not be confused with the Methodius in Petrus Comestor’s writings, the latter
being a person of mediocre intelligence and poor intellectual activity, who
was not even familiar with the biblical passages concerning Noah’s family.
The mention of a fourth son of Noah perfectly demonstrates the unreliability
of Methodius’ opinion regarding astrology’s Jewish origins. In addition, Pico
argues, no one amongst the prominent ‘commentators on the law of Moses’,


      hic Patarensis fuit, ille alius omnino, citatus interdum a Petro Commestore, in quo nemo
      mediocriter eruditus delectum bonorum auctorum non desiderat.’

      Robert D. Hughes (Barcelona: Barcino; Woodbridge: Tamesis, 2008), p. 52.

## Page 78

64                                                   Chapter 4

including Flavius Josephus and Philo, had ever mentioned the existence of a
fourth son of Noah.34 Therefore, Methodius should be dismissed as a valid au-
thority on the subject. On the other hand, Giovanni Pico affirms that Abraham
had indeed taught astrology to the Egyptians, though not its superstitious ver-
sion that deals with divination and other magical practices; instead, Abraham
limited his teaching to mathematical astrology.35 In Pico’s opinion, this was
completely new and useful knowledge for the Egyptians, which allowed them
to learn the position and movement of celestial spheres and bodies in order
to observe the time of their religious ceremonies. Additionally, they were al-
ready familiar with judicial astrological practices through the influence of the
Chaldeans.36 Regarding the problem of astronomical knowledge amongst the
Chaldeans, Giovanni Pico did not miss another opportunity to point out the
controversial nature of astrological writings on the matter. Whilst Ptolemy de-
clares in the Almagest that the Chaldeans were ignorant in practical astronom-
ical calculations, Abu Ma‘shar contradicts him by saying that Noah’s sons had
come to the land of the Chaldeans to introduce them to the science of astrol-
ogy/astronomy, which is, according to Giovanni Pico, an absurd affirmation.37
Thus, once again manipulating various sources and finding crucial contra-
dictions amongst astrologers on the fundamental points of their doctrine,
Giovanni Pico totally rejects the idea that astrology and its further diffusion
went back to the time of Noah and Abraham. He ascribes the interest in as-
trology to ancient idolatrous societies, specifically to the Chaldeans and the
Egyptians, and he reiterates that it has nothing to do with divine knowledge
and the Jewish tradition.
   In order to explain how astrology spread amongst the Chaldeans and the
Egyptians, Giovanni Pico named two key features of their cultures: idolatry

and astronomical studies.38 He also admits that their interest in astronomy,
and by extension in astrology, was determined by geographical specifics; in his
opinion, large valleys and open spaces are convenient for conducting observa-
tions of celestial movements.39 The custom of contemplating nature resulted
in a religious perception of natural phenomena in ancient societies; as an
example, Giovanni Pico names the religious interpretation of the Nile in the
Egyptian tradition.40 He claims that in these ancient societies, astronomy was
readily associated with religious practices. Notably, he did not see any particu-
lar difference between the religious traditions and thought of the Chaldeans
and the Egyptians, uniting them under the shared notion of idolatry. Despite
the ambiguous status of their religious doctrine, Pico continues, both the
Chaldeans and the Egyptians were highly respected by other ancient socie-
ties. Giovanni Pico admits that in his earlier writings, being too young, he was
also under their influence.41 The most prominent philosophers of antiquity—
including Pythagoras, Democritus, Eudoxus and Plato—also succumbed
to this ‘plague’. This assertion radically contradicts Giovanni Pico’s words
in Book I of the Disputationes, in which he works through anti-astrological
sources; by contrast, it agrees with the attack against both astrology and phi-
losophy undertaken by his nephew Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola. It was
also not typical for Giovanni Pico to acknowledge the mistakes of his youth.
This is the first confession of this sort in the whole corpus of Piconian writ-
ings, and its style is reminiscent of some passages concerning Giovanni Pico’s

## Page 80

66                                                   Chapter 4

‘re-conversion’ in the Vita, written by his nephew Gianfrancesco. Consequently,
if we accept Giovanni Pico’s authorship of the Disputationes, then in the last
book of his anti-astrological treatise, he decisively rejects his earlier ideas on
the prisca theologia, as well as the theurgical traditions of Hermetism and the
pseudo-Zoroastrian oracles. Giovanni Pico does not touch upon the topic of
secret divine knowledge that had arguably preceded philosophy and Christian
theology. However, wishing to remain within the aforementioned approach
of reconciling all anti-astrological sources, including Greek philosophers, the
author of the last book of the Disputationes tries to explain the origin of his
predecessors’ error. According to the author, all peoples and philosophers
spontaneously agree that the only true knowledge is religion. The international
fame of the Chaldeans and Egyptians as experts in magical ceremonies and re-
ligious matters attracted the interest of ancient philosophers. Astronomy was
considered another field of their expertise. That is why, according to the author
of the last book of the Disputationes, ancient (specifically Greek) philosophers
recognised the Egyptians and the Chaldeans as famous priests and astrono-
mers, although both nations were, in fact, completely ignorant in philosophi-
cal matters.42 But the author’s attempt to reconcile all anti-astrological sources,
distinguish illicit astrological knowledge from philosophy and in the end avoid
contradictions with the first book of the Disputationes fails. His obvious antip-
athy towards anti-religious philosophy incites him to affirm that despite their
generally negative attitude towards astrology, Greek philosophers sometimes
grounded philosophical systems in Eastern astrology. For example, the author
of the last book of the Disputationes accuses Thales of Miletus of borrowing
elements from Egyptian and Chaldean theurgy to establish the key principles
of his philosophy.43 In a similar way, he asserts that Plato adopted the doc-
trine of the four basic elements that form the structure of the universe from
ancient Eastern religious cults; in Egypt, Pico believes, it was closely related

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 81

Me quoque adolescentem olim fallebat                            67

to astrological practices.44 The author of the last book of the Disputationes
concludes that despite the numerous travels to Egypt intended for exploring
astrology and religious ceremonies, Greek philosophers were not caught up in
astrological superstition. As the most remarkable example, in accordance with
Book I of the treatise, he chose Eudoxus of Cnidus.45 This attempt to avoid
obvious clashes between Book I and Book XII of the Disputationes is unsuc-
cessful. The author’s attitude towards philosophy in its relation to astrology
and other magical practices is extremely negative and drastically contradicts
the content of Book I.
  The same position is replicated with some minor changes in the De rerum
praenotione of Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola.46 However, during the
years of his activity, Gianfrancesco’s uncle, Giovanni Pico was far from this
anti-philosophical radicalism. Giovanni Pico’s statement that astrology can be
convincing only amongst idolaters led him to make conclusions on the nature
of astrological speculation. He admits that the development and diffusion of
astrology might take place in a particular mental and religious environment,
that is, within societies that are unfamiliar with true religion. Giovanni Pico’s
interpretation, however, has nothing to do with sociology or scientific ap-
proaches to this question. His position is more radical: under ‘true religion’, he
understands specifically and exclusively Christianity. For him, astrology is an
important and influential part of idolatrous doctrines that focuses solely on
the study of nature and completely ignores the central place of man, his free
will and ability to influence terrestrial events to the same degree as natural
forces. Giovanni Pico proceeds by insisting that astrology totally contradicts
true religion and refutes it by establishing idols. According to him, the worship
of these idols, originally intended for defining good and evil, inevitably leads
to the Manichean heresy.47 In summary, Giovanni Pico repeats his mantra:
astrology can emerge only in the absence of true religion; the idolatrous

doctrines, dedicated solely to the study of nature, tend to replace true religion
and in the end resemble the most dangerous heresies. Their ultimate aim is to
overcome Christianity.
  The author of the last book of the Disputationes, tracing the history of as-
trology, tries to identify the next recipients of idolatrous doctrines in Greece
and other regions. To avoid contradictions with Book I, he admits that the
most renowned philosophy schools did not accept astrology. In this context,
the figure of Thales appears outlandish because the author accuses him of as-
serting that astrology had a positive impact and of using it in his philosophi-
cal discourse.48 However, his main polemical strategy at this stage is to show
that only unknown or minor philosophers in Greece had adopted astrological
doctrines. The only major author who sought to give a philosophical justifi-
cation for astrological speculation was Ptolemy.49 His authority and fame as
the greatest astronomer legitimised astrology amongst his followers. To prove
this, the author mentions Porphyry, who accepted numerous forms of super-
stitions and believed in demonic cults. The author also claims that Porphyry’s
teacher Plotinus and his disciple Iamblichus denounced Porphyry’s fascina-
tion for superstitious doctrines.50 The passage in question shows that the au-
thor was acquainted with Iamblichus’ magical and philosophical writings and,
more importantly, with the disagreements amongst the Neoplatonic thinkers
on the matter. Moreover, the name of Porphyry for the first time emerges in
relation to pro-astrological views in this passage. Previously, he was men-
tioned in the Disputationes principally as the biographer of Plotinus. If the
last book of the Disputationes was distorted, the radical change in Porphyry’s
status could be related to his position as one of the main theorists of natural
magic, expressed earlier in the Oratio de hominis dignitate and criticised by
Gianfrancesco Pico.51

## Page 83

Me quoque adolescentem olim fallebat                          69

  Then the author of Book XII of the Disputationes discusses the influence of
Ptolemaic astrological writings upon Ptolemy’s Arabic and Persian followers,
with particular emphasis on the leading astrologer Abu Ma‘shar, who, ac-
cording to him, was ignorant in matters of both astronomy/astrology and
philosophy.52 Exploring the ways in which astrological knowledge was trans-
mitted to Europe, the author justly highlights the courts of Alfonso X and
Frederick II as the centres of magical and astrological speculation. The author
acknowledges the contribution of the Spanish king to the development of as-
tronomy and mathematics, but he admits that Alfonso X could not avoid the
destructive influence of astrological superstitions.53 Alfonso’s interest in prac-
tical astrology prompted him to collect many astrological treatises of Greek
and Eastern origins, later translated either in Spain or at the court of Frederick
II in Sicily. The most intriguing figure in the list of these astrological trans-
lators is Michael Scot.54 Born in Scotland, Michael studied at Oxford and in
Paris before moving to Toledo. There, he learned Arabic and started working
on translations of numerous philosophical and astronomical/astrological texts
from Arabic into Latin. At some point, Frederick II invited Michael to join his
court. The fame of Michael as a magician spread across Italy and Europe; he is
even mentioned in Dante’s Inferno.55

     pene tanto peiorem astrologia Ptolemaei quanto bonis artibus peior est astrologia, factus

      penitus insolens, fugillatus a doctissimo quoque astrologorum, multitudini gratus, apud
     quam magnus qui plura dixerit, non qui meliora.’

      Michael Scot (London: Nelson, 1965); Graziella Federici Vescovini, ‘Michel Scot et la
      ‘Theorica Planetarum Gerardi”, Early Modern Science and Medicine, 1, 2 (1996), 272–82;

            ’l gioco.’ On Dante and astrologers: Jean-Patrice Boudet, Entre science et nigromance,





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 84

70                                                   Chapter 4

  As for the anti-astrological sources in Book XII, on the whole, this list co-
incides with the one in Book I. This applies to the biblical prophets, ancient
and medieval thinkers. The only difference is that the author adds some new
names of those who opposed astrology after its successful transmission to the
Latin West.56 Apart from some stylistic inaccuracies, the rest of the book re-
flects Giovanni Pico’s main arguments against astrology and his method of
working with astrological and anti-astrological sources.


4      Conclusion

In summary, Book XII of the Disputationes is the most questionable in terms
of its authorship. Its author severely criticises the doctrine of prisca theolo-
gia, accusing the Egyptians and the Chaldeans of developing and dissemi-
nating astrology. He affirms that astrology can only be accepted in societies
unfamiliar with the true religion that is Christianity. He also attacks those
philosophers who adapted various elements of astrological speculation to
their philosophical systems. This radical criticism of the prisca theologia is
not typical for Giovanni Pico, who remained loyal to it during the previous
years of his intellectual activity. However, this criticism perfectly fits the po-
sition of Giovanni Pico’s nephew, Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola, who
was responsible for the posthumous edition of his uncle’s Opera omnia, in-
cluding the Disputationes adversus astrologiam divinatricem. Because we do
not have enough evidence on whether the last book of the Disputationes was
written by Giovanni Pico himself or distorted by Gianfrancesco Pico, two ex-
planations are possible. Because we do not possess the original manuscript
of the Disputationes, a close examination of Giovanni Pico’s work on the trea-
tise at different stages is not possible. A study of Pico’s activities over the last
years of his life, 1493 and 1494, could have provided a better understanding of
his later philosophical outlook. But this is also impossible due to the lack of
documents. It must be noted that some traces of Pico’s attack on the prisca


      pp. 13–18; Simon Gilson, ‘Tradition and Innovation in Cristoforo Landino’s Glosses on
      Astrology in his Comento sopra la Comedia (1481)’, Italian Studies, 58 (2003), 48–74.
56   Giovanni Pico della Mirandola, Disputationes, pp. 530–32: ‘Ubi vero in Academia paris-
       iensi primitus apparuit, vix impetratum a curiosis quin internitioni data sub ignibus del-
       itesceret, cumque aliquot post annos honestare eam Rogerius Bacchon et alii quidam
      conarentur, restiterunt eis viri doctissimi Guilelmus Alvernius episcopus Parisiensis, et
      post eum Nicolaus Oresmus mathematicus excellens, et Henricus ex Assia, et Ioannem

      plane totam, ut vanam falsamque detestantes.’

## Page 85

Me quoque adolescentem olim fallebat                                  71

theologia doctrine appear in other books of the Disputationes; thus, for in-
stance, in Book II written in 1493, he compares Eastern idolatrous cults and
astrology with the heresy of Manichaeism. This may add weight to the hypoth-
esis that during the two years of his work on the Disputationes, Giovanni Pico
radically altered the principles of his philosophy. Also, the arguments against
the prisca theologia doctrine generally correspond to Pico’s main polemical
strategy of deconstructing the notion of tradition. Finally, Pico’s drastic shift
from Neoplatonism and prisca theologia towards traditional Christian views,
based on a close reading of Thomas Aquinas and Augustine, could have been
conditioned not only by his close ties with Savonarola but also by his grow-
ing interest in the sceptic tradition, especially in Sextus Empiricus. According
to Pearl Kibre and Sebastiano Gentile, Pico possessed the Adversus astrologos
chapter of Sextus’ Adversus mathematicos and possibly used it whilst work-
ing on the Disputationes.57 It appears indicative that Sextus’ central argument
against astrologers—that they are embroiled in discrepancies amongst each
other, which, in turn, undermines their discipline’s credibility—corresponds
to what Pico aimed at doing in the Disputationes. The second possible expla-
nation is far simpler: Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola distorted some of the
passages of Book XII of the Disputationes. However, despite the lack of firm
evidence either pro or contra the last book’s authorship, it is possible to con-
clude that Gianfrancesco Pico’s involvement in the final version of the treatise
could have been only partial. There is no sign of a total and radical distortion
of Pico’s Disputationes, but some details at our disposal suggest that Giovanni
Pico prepared a draft of Book XII that Gianfrancesco Pico might have slightly
revised in order to advance his own intellectual agenda.

       1936), p. 258; Sebastiano Gentile, ‘Pico filologo’, in Giovanni Pico della Mirandola. Convegno

       (479). Gentile argues that Giovanni Pico worked with the text of Sextus Empiricus. In the
      manuscript of Sextus consulted by Angelo Poliziano, there can be found the handwriting
       of a second person. Gentile supposes that these notes could have been made by Giovanni

      Richard G. Bury (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 1971).

## Page 86

Chapter 5
‘Princeps aliorum’ and His Followers:
Giovanni Pico della Mirandola on the
‘Astrological Tradition’ in the Disputationes
adversus astrologiam divinatricem


1       Introduction

One of the main polemical strategies against astrology Pico applied was find-
ing contradictions within astrological writings by analysing in detail all the dif-
ferent sources available to him. This laid the groundwork for Giovanni Pico’s
attack; his analysis of astrological practices came ex principio, that is, from
studying Claudius Ptolemy’s writings on astrology1 and supplementing this
study with a criticism of Ptolemy’s followers, who, he argued, either did not un-
derstand their ‘teacher’ or entirely misrepresented his ideas and calculations.
This chapter is devoted to the question of Pico’s astrological sources, which
usually remains outside of scholarly interest. The main aim of the chapter is to
show how Pico uses his sources to set Ptolemy against other astrologers and to
emphasise the dissonance in the astrological tradition.2
  Pico claims to be familiar with all major ancient and medieval astrological
authorities. His goal is to explain the genesis of a long astrological tradition,
which he considers fundamentally wrong because it is based on a misinterpre-
tation of philosophy, especially of Aristotelian natural philosophy. According
to him, the first to have made this mistake is Ptolemy, whom Pico labels op-
timus malorum, ‘the best of the worst’.3 Pico knew very well all three of the
works written by or attributed to Ptolemy: the Almagest, the Tetrabiblos and the

1  I disagree with Louis Valcke’s notion (expressed in his book Pic de la Mirandole. Un itinéraire
   philosophique’, p. 305) that Pico’s ‘bête noire’ was Pierre d’Ailly. Pico himself stated that all
   astrological speculations, even those of ‘Christianised’ astrologers, were based on Ptolemy’s
   theories. See Giovanni Pico della Mirandola, Disputationes, I, I, p. 70: ‘Porro Ptolemaeum
   principem aliorum facile concedent; est enim doctissimus astrologorum et, quod attinet ad
   mathematica, vir ingeniosissimus.’
2  Some parts of this chapter have been discussed in my essay: ‘Princeps aliorum and His
   Followers: ‘Giovanni Pico della Mirandola on the ‘Astrological Tradition’ in the Disputationes
   adversus astrologiam divinatricem’, Renaissance Studies, 32, 4 (2018), 547–64.
3  Giovanni Pico della Mirandola, Disputationes, I, I, p. 70: ‘Quod autem ad genethliacos, que-
  madmodum dici solet, optimus malorum.’


© Koninklijke Brill NV, Leiden, 2021 | doi:10.1163/9789004442276_006  Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 87

‘Princeps aliorum’ and His Followers                               73

Centiloquium. Pico explains that he singles Ptolemy out because of Ptolemy’s
philosophical studies. Ptolemy is praised for having attempted to establish
the philosophical and scientific foundations for astrology and for having con-
tributed to the development of mathematical astrology, that is, astronomy.
However, Pico argues, Ptolemy’s philosophical premises are fundamentally
wrong. In the introduction to the Almagest,4 Ptolemy ‘following the opinion of
Aristotle’, distinguishes three types of contemplative philosophy: theological,
natural and mathematical. Pico argues that Ptolemy is wrong when he says
that ‘everything that exists consists of three elements: matter, form and move-
ment, which can be divided conceptually, but not in the things themselves’.5
From this idea, Ptolemy concluded that each type of contemplative philoso-
phy corresponded to one of the elements: theology to movement (as the prime
cause of motion is nothing but God), physics to matter, and mathematics to
form. Pico calls this conclusion absurd and contradictory to Aristotle and to
any other philosophy.6 Despite this fundamental error, however, Ptolemy dif-
fers from other astrologers because he attempted to bring astrology closer to
science and philosophy. It is important to note that in the following pages of
the Disputationes, Pico leaves aside the Almagest and generally focuses on
Ptolemy’s two astrological treatises: the Tetrabiblos and the Centiloquium.
   Pico’s focus on Ptolemy and his attempt to question Ptolemy’s auctoritas,
seen as the starting point of a long tradition of textual misinterpretation, was
no coincidence; the status and value of Ptolemy was central to astrological and
philosophical controversies that took place in the fifteenth century. With the
revival of Ptolemy’s Almagest in the mid-fifteenth century thanks to George
of Trebizond’s Latin translation and commentary, a debate ensued about the
place of Ptolemy’s legacy within the commentary tradition. Whereas George
of Trebizond favoured a strict return to Ptolemy’s original texts, Bessarion con-
sidered that Theon of Alexandria’s commentary on the Almagest (composed in
the fourth century AD) was the best source to understand Ptolemy and Greek
astronomy. In 1451, George of Trebizond completed a new translation of the
Almagest from Greek and ‘accompanied his version of Ptolemy’s Almagest with


4  Ptolemy, Syntaxis mathematica, 2 vols, ed. by Johan L. Heiberg (Leipzig: Teubner, 1898–1903),
    vol. 1 (1898), prooemium, pp. 4–7; Giovanni Pico della Mirandola, Disputationes, I, I, pp. 70–72.
5  Giovanni Pico della Mirandola, Disputationes, I, I, p. 70: ‘Exordiens enim libros mathemati-
   cos, partitur philosophiam contemplatricem, ex Aristotelis sententiam, in tria genera, the-
   ologicum, naturale et mathematicum; tum, rationem partitionis exponens, omnia, inquit,
   quae sunt, ex tribus constant: materia, forma et motu, quae separari ab invicem cogitatione
   quidem possunt, re autem non possunt.’
6  Ibid., pp. 70–72.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 88

74                                                   Chapter 5

a commentary of enormous length’.7 He intended to dedicate it to Nicholas V
but had to abandon this idea.8 In his work, George sought to purify Ptolemy’s
original ideas from medieval, both Arabic and Latin, interpretations, as well as
to oppose Bessarion, who had advised him to use Theon of Alexandria’s com-
mentary on the Almagest as an example. The attempt to prove that Theon of
Alexandria’s influence on medieval thinkers was negative and caused misun-
derstanding of Ptolemy’s work incited a response from one of Bessarion’s most
famous disciples, Regiomontanus, who wrote a special treatise against George
known as the Defensio Theonis contra Trapezuntium.9 Other scholars, such as
Georg von Peuerbach and Niccolò Perotti, were also involved in the debate,
which, along with the famous Plato-Aristotle controversy, formed the essence
of the Bessarion-George conflict.10 Later in the sixteenth century, Ptolemy was
at the centre of Girolamo Cardano’s ‘restored’ Greek astrology.11 This trend, trig-
gered by the rise of humanism and further application of humanist methods
in the field of cosmology, put forward another foundational question of how
reliable the Islamic tradition was in interpreting Ptolemy and other Greek as-
tronomical and astrological sources. It appears that the Renaissance witnessed
a gradual decline of confidence in Arabic sciences amongst humanist physi-
cians and astrologers.12 Thus, the idea of reconsidering the position of Ptolemy

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 89

‘Princeps aliorum’ and His Followers                               75

within a larger ‘astrological tradition’ seems to be typical for Renaissance
scholars. Pico’s Disputationes was an important landmark in this respect.
  Despite the apparent existence of such an astrological tradition, Pico con-
tends that Ptolemy and his followers contradicted each other regarding both key
theoretical positions and astrological practice. Taking this approach, Giovanni
Pico establishes a distinction between Ptolemy, an astrologer of a higher level,
albeit fundamentally wrong in his interpretation of Aristotle, and his follow-
ers, who, Pico argues, distorted his legacy. In order to confirm his idea of the
fundamental rupture within the astrological tradition, Pico uses two modes of
argumentation. On the one hand, he shows that Abu Ma‘shar and others often
made mistakes in interpreting Ptolemaic astrological terms and, as a result,
used them in a completely different sense. On the other hand, commenting
on the Centiloquium (now considered spurious), Giovanni Pico constantly dis-
putes the points that contradict Ptolemy’s other texts whilst agreeing with the
writings of more recent astrologers.13 Without trying to question the author-
ship of the Centiloquium, he chose another method of attack. In his opinion, in-
stead of using the original text of the Centiloquium, astrologers relied on a bad
translation, in which Ptolemy’s ideas were distorted. However, he continues,
the original Greek text does not, in fact, contradict the rest of Ptolemy’s writ-
ings. Pico claims to have compared the Latin translation of the Centiloquium
with the original text, and he claims he found more than six hundred errors
in the translation.14 As Eugenio Garin and Sebastiano Gentile have shown,
Giovanni Pico most probably used the translation of the Centiloquium made
by Giovanni Pontano and completed between 1477 and 1490, rather than one
of the numerous medieval translations.15 These two approaches to the recep-
tion of Ptolemy and his followers in the Disputationes determine the structure
of the current chapter: its first part deals with astrological practices, modified
or misunderstood in the astrological tradition, whereas the second section fo-
cuses on the problem of the Centiloquium.




       Press, 2016), especially pp. 248–92 on the dichotomy between Ptolemy and his Islamic
      commentators.

       declararetur.’

## Page 90

2     The Use of Astrological Techniques and Its Controversies

As stated above, Pico’s first strategy is to explore the contradictions amongst
astrologers in practical matters. The three examples below illustrate how Pico
identifies the errors in the usage of astrological terms. The first one deals with
the solar eclipse discussed in the Tetrabiblos. According to Ptolemy, someone
watching the solar eclipse should note the place where it occurs and calcu­
late the angle from this place.16 If the eclipse occurred in the ninth region of
the sky, then the angle of the tenth is calculated, and so on. However, Pico
claims that Ptolemy’s followers incorrectly translated the term τοῦ ἑπομένου
κέντρου (‘the angle of the following area’) as anguli praecedentis, that is, the
angle of the previous area, which, he argues, completely changed the very prin-
ciple of this calculation.17 In Pico’s opinion, therefore, on the basis of this error
in translation, Ptolemy’s followers misinterpreted the way the eclipse should
be calculated.18 Continuing the examination of the misinterpretation of this
passage, Giovanni Pico gives another example. Speaking about calculating the
eclipse angle, Ptolemy wrote that the angle might refer to both the eastern
and the middle parts of the skyline. ‘The Greek commentator’, the so-called
pseudo-Proclus,19 interpreted Ptolemy as saying nothing regarding the attitude
of the angle to the western part of the horizon, because in accordance with

     quo deficit Sol et angulum qui cum locum proxime sequitur, ut si, gratia exempli, in nona
       caeli regione defectus obtigerit, decimi loco angulus observetur; si in undecima vel duo-
      decima, angulus orientis.’ See also Ptolemy, Le previsioni astrologiche (Tetrabiblos), II, 6.

       guli praecedentis, unde contrarius omnino sensus elicitur.’

‘Princeps aliorum’ and His Followers                                77

the aforementioned theory, in this position, the angle could not be located
after the place of the solar eclipse, and the eclipse itself would have no effect.20
According to Pico, the only reason why the Greek commentator makes this
argument is that he misunderstood τοῦ ἑπομένου κέντρου as referring to anguli
praecedentis.21 Consequently, he too failed to grasp the significance of Ptolemy’s
doctrine. What is particularly important for Pico is that these misinterpreta-
tions could lead to the incorrect prediction of the future and, therefore, under-
mine the validity of astrology.
  The role of ‘the Greek commentator’ is worth attention also in another con-
text. At the beginning of the fifteenth century, an excerpt of four chapters from
Geminus’ Isasoge was ascribed to the Neoplatonic philosopher Proclus under
the title of Sphaera. The reason for the attribution of the previously unknown
astrological/astronomical treatise to Proclus remains unclear. Since the begin-
ning of the fifteenth century, the Sphaera by pseudo-Proclus was diffused in
manuscripts across Europe. The Sphaera remained a very popular text in the
sixteenth century, with over sixty editions published. It was also translated
into English by the famous English humanist Thomas Linacre. The popularity
of the Sphaera by ‘Proclus’ made it the second most important astrological/
astronomical ‘manual’ in the West, behind only the Sphaera by Johannes de
Sacrobosco. Some scholars even consider the pseudo-Proclean Sphaera as the
humanist response to Sacrobosco’s ‘medieval’ treatise of the same name.22

    cum accidit, vim futurorum effectricem habere non putant.’ See also Pseudo-Proclus, In
