## Page 131

Back to Aristotle?                                                           117

theory according to which all the planets have an identical nature and thus
produce identical influences. In order to reject this supposition, Pico decon-
structs the pro-astrological reading of Aristotle’s On the Heavens, claiming that,
although the Stagirite confirms that by their own nature the planets and stars
can all produce certain influences, it does not mean that their influences are
identical.38 Commenting on the same passages from Aristotle’s treatise, Pico
reinterprets them in a non-astrological manner. He declares that the planets
per se can have identical powers, but, in fact, their productivity varies due to
additional causes. He distinguishes three primary causes: size, density and
proximity to Earth.39 Depending on these factors, the influence of each celes-
tial body becomes stronger or weaker. Pico adds that, due to the rotation of
planets or stars, the angle, and therefore the intensity, of their radiation can
change too. Considering the variety of factors that determine the power of ce-
lestial radiation, Pico concludes that only the Sun and the Moon are in a posi-
tion to produce effective and strong influences on the physical world; although
other planets and stars are much bigger than the Sun and the Moon, they are
too far away from Earth, which reduces their power.40 Thus, Pico points to an-
other mistake of astrologers who, in his opinion, misunderstood Aristotle; by
deconstructing previous interpretations of this passage from Aristotle’s On
the Heavens and reinterpreting it in accordance with his primary polemical
strategy, Pico achieves his purpose of purifying Aristotle from subsequent

## Page 132

118                                                   Chapter 6

astrological speculations and of demonstrating that there is no philosophical
basis for a belief in astrology.
  These features—a detailed description of the mechanism of celestial influ-
ences through the combination of ‘perfect’ light with matter, a clear orientation
towards Aristotelian physics and an attack on astrology from both theological
and natural philosophical perspectives—form the basis of Pico’s natural philo-
sophical arguments against astrology. They correspond to Pico’s main polemi-
cal strategy to return ad fontes, as expressed in other parts of the Disputationes.
He intends to restore what he sees as the true meaning of the philosophical
and theological texts, which were distorted by astrologers in order to give their
discipline more authority. In Pico’s view, the case of Aristotle conspicuously
exemplifies the astrologers’ attempts to deceive people and make of their sub-
ject something that it is not: a credible field of philosophical investigation. Pico
concludes that astrology has nothing in common with a proper knowledge of
nature and is based exclusively on astrologers’ ‘false’ theories and calculations.
  Apart from the general philosophical perspective, to support his argument,
Pico refers to a number of practical examples. He claims that astrologers falsely
attribute several natural effects to the influence of the Sun and the Moon. He
refutes their opinion by using two polemical methods: again, he reminds us
of the discrepancies, contradictions and inconsistencies within the astrologi-
cal tradition concerning all major theoretical problems; he also indicates that
astrologers often ascribe astrological causes to natural phenomena, whereas,
according to Pico, most of the astrological concepts are at variance with nat-
ural philosophy and experimental knowledge. These cases include seasonal
illnesses41 and the changing of the seasons.42 He firmly rejects the idea that
the traditional fields of human activity connected to the observations of the
Sun—navigation, medicine and agriculture—can be subject to astrological
speculation.43 In a similar way, he opposes the so-called lunar astrology claim-
ing that all the influences provoked by the Moon are of accidental nature.44
He purifies the legacy of Hesiod, in whose writings astrologers sought to find
some ground for their predictions.45 As for Galen, Pico accuses the ancient
physician of acknowledging some elements of lunar astrology, but at the
same time, Pico points at the misunderstanding of Galen’s teaching amongst



41    Ibid., I, III, XIII, pp. 278–80.

43    Ibid., pp. 282–84.

## Page 133

Back to Aristotle?                                                         119

subsequent medico-astrologers.46 He also adds that other prominent phy-
sicians, namely Hippocrates and Avicenna, disagreed with Galen and never
accepted astrological speculation.47 Finally, the most significant practical ex-
ample with which Pico intends to demonstrate the fallacy of astrology deals
with the problem of tides.48
  As is well known, two of the most famous early modern interpretations of
tides are by Galileo Galilei and Isaac Newton, but the origin of this natural phe-
nomenon was widely debated before the publication of Galileo’s Dialogo.49
We still do not have enough evidence about ancient representations of tides;
at least, none of the theories achieved consensus. According to some stud-
ies, the primary source of the ancient theories of tides is Pliny the Elder, who
strongly influenced all medieval authors concerned with natural history. In his
Naturalis Historia, he states that an interaction between the Sun and the Moon
on the basis of the fortnightly cycles causes the tides, but he does not go into
much detail and gives no explicit explanation for the fortnightly cycle.50
   In the Middle Ages, many explanations for tidal motions were put forward.
Some of them had a clear astrological background and were introduced in the
twelfth and thirteenth centuries due to the transmission of Islamic knowledge

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 134

120                                                  Chapter 6

in the West. As Lucio Russo and Federico Bonelli have pointed out, there were
three main tidal theories in the Middle Ages.51 The first admitted the exist-
ence of underwater chasms, which periodically drew in and forced out the
water, and thus, in analogy with breathing in an animal, caused the movement
of the tides due to certain natural rules. The second set of theories, also of a
more physical nature, attributed tides to, amongst other things, the action of
rivers and winds, the salinity of the water or the natural heat of deep water.
Finally, the third, astronomical/astrological theory was based on a recognition
of the correlation amongst tides, diurnal rotation and the lunar cycle. It dated
back to Abu Ma‘shar’s Liber introductorii maioris ad scientiam iudiciorum ast-
rorum, the most influential astrological ‘manual’ in medieval Europe, and thus
obtained a strong astrological dimension.52 In addition, Abu Ma‘shar contrib-
uted to an idea that besides the Moon, other heavenly bodies, including the
Sun and the planets, also influenced the tides, which gave additional weight
to pro-astrological explanations of the phenomenon. The theory was widely
accepted in medieval Europe and gave rise to new interpretations of tidal
motions.53 At the same time, Abu Ma‘shar himself confirms that the tides can
have dual origins. After a lengthy exposition on the astronomical/astrological
origin of tides, he adds, as another cause of the phenomenon, the action of
the winds. As we shall see, such ambivalence in Abu Ma‘shar’s work is crucial
for Giovanni Pico’s arguments against the astrological interpretations of tides.
  The revival of Platonism in fifteenth-century Florence had no impact on
the debates on tides. Although Marsilio Ficino examined the problem of
fluxus/refluxus and discussed whether heavenly bodies influenced the terres-
trial world permanently,54 the only text in which the tidal motions are inter-
preted through the lens of Neoplatonic teaching is Laonikos Chalkokondyles’
Histories, unknown to both Ficino and Pico.55 Thus, whilst considering the ori-
gin of tides, Pico remained entirely within the medieval tradition.

  Pico shows a good knowledge of the debates on the problem in question, re-
jecting step by step various tidal theories.56 His main aim, however, is to prove
that the tides have nothing to do with ‘lunar astrology’. In opposition to the
astrological interpretation of which he accuses Abu Ma‘shar and his followers,
he proposes another explanation, namely that the winds determine the tidal
motions.57 However, because we have seen that both theories go back to Abu
Ma‘shar Liber introductorii maioris, relying on Abu Ma‘shar would contra-
dict Pico’s strategy of stigmatising the Persian astrologer as one of his main
opponents. That is why he attributes the theory he accepts to a certain
‘Adelandus Arabus’.
   ‘Adelandus’ seems to have been identified with Adelard of Bath, a twelfth-
century translator, philosopher and astrologer, who was, amongst other things,
responsible for the first translation of Abu Ma‘shar into Latin.58 Adelard sig-


        last 150 years of the Byzantine Empire. There, whilst describing the ebb and flow in
      England, Laonikos proposes his own interpretation of the tides evidently influenced by
      the Neoplatonic tradition: Laonikos Chalkokondyles, The Histories, 2 vols, ed. by Anthony
       Kaldellis (Cambridge, MA: Harvard University Press, 2014), I, 1.88.18–1.90.8: ‘When the
    Moon reaches the middle of the sky, coming to both our horizon and the one beneath the
       earth, the waters turn and flow in the opposite direction. Those who observe the Moon
     need to consider this motion of the waters. For I believe that the Moon has been assigned
     by God to govern the nature of the waters. This would not be inconsistent with its nature
     and mixture of elements, which it received in the beginning from God the Great King.
    When the Moon rises in the sky it draws the waters after it until it reaches the highest
      point in the sky; then, as it descends, the waters go back, no longer following it in its as-
       cent. And when, in turn, the Moon has made its descent and begins to go back up again,
      the tide turns about and starts to rise again. It happens that the winds contribute to this
      process too and move the waters still more, from wherever the latter originally receive
       their motion. This movement of the waters may, then, feature a dual motion that goes
      against the motion of the totality of the sky, becoming both spontaneous and violent, so
      that if this motion does not attain a harmonious unison, it becomes extremely varied.
      This is most pleasant to contemplate, view, and hear, and is in accordance with one of
      the rules of the Soul of this Universe, namely that this Soul takes pleasure in perceiving
     how different motions may be conveyed and borne along with each other into a certain
      uniform harmony.’ The fragment in question clearly reflects the Neoplatonic notion of the
      world soul seen as the motor of all natural effects, in Laonikos’ view, also responsible for
       tidal motions. I would like to thank my colleague Sergei Fadeev for bringing my attention
       to this passage.

     Yamamoto (Leiden: Brill, 1994). ‘Adelandus Arabus’ seems to have been identified with
      Adelard of Bath in the 900 Conclusiones: Farmer, Syncretism in the West, pp. 13–14. In the
      edition of the Disputationes, Eugenio Garin translated the original Latin form ‘Adelandus’
      as ‘Adelardo’ in Italian: Giovanni Pico della Mirandola, Disputationes, I, III, XV, pp. 308–9.



                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 136

122                                                  Chapter 6

nificantly contributed to the transmission of previously unknown texts to the
Latin West, including the Elements of Euclid. Apart from that, Adelard com-
posed several original treatises, mostly on natural philosophy.59 Pico’s decision
to ascribe Adelard to the Islamic tradition might have been explained by the
influence Islamic thought had on the twelfth-century translator, but the text
of the Disputationes shows no traces that Pico was, in fact, familiar with the
Christian origin of Adelard. The same mistake can be observed in Pico’s earlier
text, the Conclusiones (1486), in which Pico extracts eight theses from the views
of ‘Adelandus’.60
  Although Adelard simply adopted the Persian astrologer’s doctrine on tidal
motions influenced by the winds, ascribing the only correct (in Pico’s view)
tidal theory to him, instead of Abu Ma‘shar, allows Pico to underline that as-
trologers are unable to provide precise calculations and contradict each other
on almost every major concept of their discipline.61 Supplemented by other
examples of a misinterpretation and misunderstanding of the theory over the
centuries, Pico’s solution perfectly corresponds to what he claims about the
‘astrological tradition’ in general: in Pico’s opinion, the existence of a disso-
nance within the tradition proves the falsity of the whole discipline.
  To summarise, Giovanni Pico’s natural philosophical arguments against
predictions form a significant part of his general attack on astrology in the
Disputationes adversus astrologiam divinatricem. On the one hand, Book III of
the Disputationes marks the transformation of Pico’s views on the question
of celestial light. As previously shown, he puts forward the problem of the
communication of light with matter in the Heptaplus and the Expositiones in
Psalmos, but at that stage, he does not succeed in combining the notion of
celestial light interpreted within a Neoplatonic framework with Aristotelian
physics. In the Disputationes, however, probably due to the influence of the De
mysteriis Aegyptiorum and Marsilio Ficino’s paraphrase of Iamblichus’ treatise,
Giovanni Pico proposes a compromise between the Platonic and Aristotelian
theories. On the other hand, Pico’s polemical strategies in Book III correspond
to the arguments developed in the rest of the treatise. He insists that the leg-
acy of Aristotle was distorted by subsequent pro-astrological interpretations
and needs to be restored to its fullest purity. The whole natural philosophical
discourse in Book III remains strongly within an Aristotelian framework. This


59   On Adelard of Bath’s activities in general, see Charles Burnett, Adelard of Bath: An English
       Scientist and Arabist of the Early Twelfth Century (London: The Warburg Institute, 1987).
60    ‘Conclusiones secundum Adelandum Arabem numero VIII’ (Farmer, Syncretism in the
      West, pp. 302–4).
61   Giovanni Pico della Mirandola, Disputationes, I, III, XV, pp. 310–16.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 137

Back to Aristotle?                                                    123

also confirms Pico’s intention to avoid, at least openly, some contemporary in-
tellectual currents, which is characteristic of the Disputationes on the whole.
As we have seen, in most cases, he refers to classical authors not associated
with any philosophical novelties, like the prisca theologia and Neoplatonism,
and, therefore, comes back to the origins of philosophy and theology person-
ified in the Disputationes by Aristotle and by Augustine and Thomas Aquinas,
respectively. In the meantime, the idiosyncratic manner in which Pico cleanses
Aristotle of erroneous and misleading readings and intends to reconstruct the
Stagirite’s ‘original’ teaching about nature clearly resonates with an issue that
dominated humanist scholarship and natural philosophical and scientific
thinking in the sixteenth century. Whereas some questioned Aristotle’s author-
ity on the basis of humanist critiques of his works or stressed the incompat-
ibility of Aristotelian natural philosophy with new discoveries, others sought
to advocate for his reputation and adapt his teaching to the shifting world of
early modern science.62 This reveals an overarching trend behind Pico’s take
on Aristotle’s legacy and suggests that the Count of Mirandola was highly en-
gaged with the problems that constituted the hot spots of Renaissance intel-
lectual life. Thus, the analysis of Book III of the Disputationes sheds new light
on the development of Pico’s philosophical views over his intellectual itiner-
ary, the polemical strategies he applied in the last anti-astrological treatise and
his contribution to the problem of philosophical authority that received new
input in the subsequent century.

62   The last decades have witnessed a soaring interest in the role of the Aristotelian tradi-
      tion in shaping early modern philosophical and scientific ideas. These studies have suc-
       cessfully challenged two outdated positions: that after its revival in the fifteenth century,
      Platonism was by far the prevailing philosophical school in the Renaissance; and that
     new science emerged strictly outside Aristotelianism. To name but a few studies that
      allow for a more nuanced understanding of this subject: Charles B. Schmitt, Aristotle and
      the Renaissance (Cambridge, MA; London: Harvard University Press, 1983); Craig Martin,
      Renaissance Meteorology: Pomponazzi to Descartes (Baltimore: Johns Hopkins University
       Press, 2011); idem, Subverting Aristotle: Religion, History, and Philosophy in Early Modern
      Science (Baltimore: Johns Hopkins University Press, 2014); Paul Richard Blum, Studies on
      Early Modern Aristotelianism (Leiden; Boston: Brill, 2012).

## Page 138

Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 139

Part 3
The Disputationes: Pro et contra

        ∵





                                                        Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 140

The posthumous publication of the Disputationes adversus astrologiam divina­
tricem aroused heated discussions on the place of astrology within Renaissance
culture. As we have seen above, in his work, Giovanni Pico challenges astrology
as a discipline able to predict the future. On the one hand, considering how
popular astrological knowledge was at that time, it comes as no surprise that
the Disputationes faced severe opposition. On the other hand, Pico’s fame; the
way he uncovered the issues with the mathematical calculations and theoret-
ical grounds on which astrology was based, which required reconsideration
and fixing; and the religious and intellectual developments of the late fifteenth
century associated with the circle of Girolamo Savonarola all contributed to
the success of the Disputationes. In the following chapters, my main goal is
to analyse the reception of Giovanni Pico’s treatise. The research will focus
on the immediate reaction of the Disputationes amongst those who supported
or, on the contrary, opposed Giovanni Pico’s anti-astrological account. Most of
the thinkers mentioned below either knew Giovanni Pico in person or were
amongst the first readers of the Disputationes, sometimes even before its pub-
lication. The first three chapters cover three case studies on the ‘positive’ re-
ception of the Disputationes, after which I intend to turn to Giovanni Pico’s
opponents.





© Koninklijke Brill NV, Leiden, 2021 | doi:10.1163/9789004442276_008  Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 141

Chapter 7
Ideological Appropriation of Giovanni Pico’s
Disputationes: Girolamo Savonarola and His
Contro gli astrologi

In 1494, Giovanni Pico della Mirandola suddenly died under mysterious cir-
cumstances. From that moment on, one version of his death became par-
ticularly popular: notorious Pope Alexander VI had ordered Giovanni Pico’s
assassination. This version originated from the fact that until 1493, Giovanni
Pico had  still been  officially considered a heretic. The decree of Pope
Alexander’s predecessor, Pope Innocent VIII, who planned on hosting the 1486
Roman dispute, was not cancelled. Right after his election, the new Pope had
solemnly forgiven Giovanni Pico. The text of his decree was then published
by Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola at the beginning of his uncle’s Opera
omnia.1 Nevertheless, Alexander VI quickly changed his opinion on Pico, as the
latter, alongside almost all his friends and colleagues, accepted the new polit-
ical regime under Savonarola that was established in Florence after the death
of Lorenzo de’ Medici in 1492 and Piero de’ Medici’s exile in 1494.2 According to
those who accused Alexander VI of Pico’s murder, the Count of Mirandola’s fa-
vourable attitude towards Savonarola was one of the reasons that led the Pope
to orchestrate Pico’s death. Curiously, Pico’s close friend Angelo Poliziano, who
was also amongst Savonarola’s supporters, had died a month earlier. Although
some studies and additional evidence seem to support the theory of murder,3
Pico’s death remains an enigma.

© Koninklijke Brill NV, Leiden, 2021 | doi:10.1163/9789004442276_009  Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 142

128                                                  Chapter 7

  Be that as it may, Giovanni Pico’s sudden death played a significant part in
the construction of his myth. During his lifetime, he was a living legend whose
knowledge was believed to encompass an entire spectrum of philosophical
and theological disciplines and who also mastered rare and previously un-
known languages, such as Hebrew. In the early 1490s, it was quite common
to travel to Florence for an audience with Giovanni Pico. Amongst the young
and gifted scholars from European countries who admired Giovanni Pico was
Jacques Lefèvre d’Étaples, who later became a famous humanist. Undoubtedly,
Giovanni Pico and his Kabbalistic studies inspired several humanists through-
out Europe to learn Hebrew.4 But his fame attracted negative elements as well.
One of them was related to the posthumous publication of the Disputationes.
  The controversies regarding the authorship of the Disputationes cast shad-
ows on Giovanni Pico’s legacy as a whole. The core of his attack was distorted
in essence; from the late fifteenth century on, the Disputationes was often
considered not a comprehensive and multi-faceted critique of astrology but
a religiously motivated invective. There were two reasons for this view. The
first is the image of Giovanni Pico his nephew crafted in the Vita. This text
created a narrative about a talented heretic who became disillusioned with
his own earlier ideas and eventually returned under the wing of the Church.
Thus, Gianfrancesco’s account, written in line with his personal position,
stressed and undoubtedly exaggerated the religious aspects of Giovanni Pico’s
views.5 Second, in 1497, Savonarola himself published a text against astrol-
ogy, which he presented as a simplified vernacular version of Pico’s complex
philosophical doctrines; through the use of simple expressions, he tried to
make Pico’s ideas accessible to common people.6 Savonarola’s Contro gli
astrologi came to be seen as the most influential continuation of Giovanni
Pico’s anti-astrological attack.7 Apart from philosophical and scientific re-
sponses to the Disputationes, in many respects, it was Savonarola’s Contro gli
astrologi that shaped the religious discourse on this subject. Some later works
concerned with the refutation of astrology largely drew upon both Giovanni

   Aragno, 2006); Busi, L’enigma dell’ebraico nel Rinascimento.
5  Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola, Ioannis Pici Mirandulae viri omni disciplinarum genere
   consumatissimi vita.

   pp. 34–35.

    Pico’, in Nello specchio del cielo, pp. 117–37.

## Page 143

Ideological Appropriation of Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes        129

Pico’s and Savonarola’s treatises and included, amongst others, the writings
by the famous Swiss theologian Thomas Erastus and the leading Jesuit exegete
Benedict Pereira.8
  Savonarola is thought to have heavily influenced Giovanni Pico in the
early 1490s. There are strong arguments for accepting this assumption. First,
Savonarola probably came to Florence in 1490 at Pico’s invitation.9 From
that moment until Pico’s death in 1494, they remained friends. In 1492,
Giovanni Pico visited an assembly of the Dominican order that took place
near Ferrara. Savonarola was participating in the event, and Giovanni Pico,
inspired by his speeches, decided to write the so-called ‘moral letters’ to his
nephew Gianfrancesco Pico.10 Although there are now some reservations
about their authenticity,11 at that time, the letters were believed to constitute
a summary of Giovanni Pico’s religious views. Along with the Vita authored
by Gianfrancesco, they formed the basis for an important side of Giovanni
Pico’s international reputation. For instance, some years later, Thomas More
translated the body of Giovanni Pico’s late religious opuscula and the Vita
into English.12
   Savonarola’s influence was reflected in some other instances. Thus, it is
assumed that he consulted Pico on the books the Count of Mirandola ought
to read and the areas of philosophy and theology in which he needed to
deepen his expertise. Savonarola insisted that Pico broadened and devel-
oped his knowledge of the Scriptures and carefully read Thomas Aquinas and

130                                                  Chapter 7

other ‘traditional authors’.13 Finally, Pico’s ‘re-conversion’ was finalised by his
solemn burial at the San Marco monastery in the Dominican habit.14 Although
Gianfrancesco Pico in all probability overstated Savonarola’s ‘positive’ influ-
ence on Giovanni Pico, one cannot disregard some facts that might support
the picture of his uncle created in the Vita. The main question concerns the
extent of this influence. On the one hand, it seems evident that in the early
1490s, Pico’s philosophical views developed from earlier positions. Because
his keen interest in religious texts coincided with Savonarola’s sermons in
Florence, one can presume that the evolution of Pico’s thought was, at least
in part, determined by the intellectual context of contemporary Florence.
Besides, we know that the ‘Ferrarese prophet’ impressed other intellectuals
from the former Medici entourage to the point that some of them turned into
his admirers. Angelo Poliziano was buried next to Giovanni Pico at San Marco,
whilst Girolamo and Antonio Benivieni were amongst Savonarola’s most fer-
vent followers. Even Marsilio Ficino supported the new Florentine regime for
a while.15 This suggests that Pico could have been subject to a similar impact.
  On the other hand, the evidence at our disposal does not seem to be suf-
ficient to affirm that the Disputationes was written under Savonarola’s spell,
nor did his ideological agenda define the content and polemical strategies
of Pico’s last treatise. As we have seen, Pico’s central argument entails distin-
guishing Aristotle, Plato and, to some extent, Ptolemy from later distortions of
their thought and owes much more to the humanist method of textual criti-
cism than to Savonarola’s teachings on religion and the moral corruption of
contemporary society. One could also argue that there could be an influence
in the opposite direction, that is, from Giovanni Pico to Savonarola. Although
Savonarola paid particular attention to dismissing pagan theories on prophecy

## Page 145

Ideological Appropriation of Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes         131

and demons and accused in his sermons all forms of predictions, including
astrology, before 1493–94, he did not develop this general condemnation into a
substantial critique.16 Similarly, around the early 1480s, Savonarola denounced
those who supported chiromancy, again without elaborating on the subject.17
His first detailed attempt to disprove occult sciences as such appears to be the
Contro gli astrologi. Later, he would repeat his main arguments against occult-
ism and superstitions in the foundational Il Trionfo della Croce,18 with the Contro
gli astrologi remaining the only treatise directed specifically against astrology.
  Savonarola does not deny that Pico’s Disputationes prompted him to under-
take his own attack on astrology. In the introduction to the Contro gli astrologi,
he claims that he has an ambiguous attitude towards the Disputationes. On the
one hand, he finds it very useful for the benefit of religion and the Christian
flock because it helps them avoid being enslaved by various dangerous super-
stitions. On the other hand, he regrets that Pico had died too suddenly and did
not get a chance to polish his text.19 This justifies Savonarola’s own motivation
for taking up Pico’s fight against astrology by translating Pico’s main arguments
into Italian and consequently adapting them as his own.
  The idea of translatio studii is reflected in the way Savonarola shaped both
the structure of the Contro gli astrologi and the essential elements of his argu-
ment. He copies the introduction to the Disputationes, declaring that no phi-
losopher, theologian or ruler has ever endorsed astrology and other predictive
techniques as valid and trustworthy. To this question, he devotes the entire
first book of his treatise, which content-wise echoes the ‘historiographic’ first
book of Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes. The sole difference is that Savonarola
changes the order of the anti-astrological arguments. Whereas Giovanni Pico
starts with philosophy, Savonarola first insists on the theological contradictions
between astrology and other occult sciences, on the one hand, and religion, on

## Page 146

132                                                  Chapter 7

the other. Savonarola’s claims are, however, standard. In line with what most
of the opponents of astrology had already stated over the centuries, he asserts
that astrology contradicts one of the central premises of Christian teaching—
the doctrine of free will—and undermines God’s omnipotence.
  To prove this notion, Savonarola mostly refers to the Bible. However, in prin-
ciple, he does not quote from the New Testament; with the exception of one
case, most quotations are from the Old Testament. Other works seem to follow
this tendency. As Riccardo Fubini has suggested, the fact that references to Old
Testament books are predominant in Savonarola’s writings has to do with the
way he intended to represent himself. Savonarola considered himself a suc-
cessor to the Old Testament prophets, who had sought to turn people from
superstitions and sins back to God and were the direct intermediaries between
God and the people.20 Hence, Savonarola’s own prophetic intentions gained
another dimension. In order to confirm the idea that astrology was always on
the margins of culture and religion, Savonarola chose several quotations from
the Old Testament claiming that the Jews who deviated from God had done
so because they had fallen victim to false prophets and doctrines. As we shall
see below, Gianfrancesco Pico adheres to the same anti-Jewish rhetoric in dis-
missing astrology as a field incompatible with true religion in the De rerum
praenotione.
   In the second section of Book I of his treatise, Savonarola argues that ‘good’
theologians have consistently adopted an anti-astrological sentiment. He il-
lustrates this by referencing Augustine and Thomas Aquinas, both quoted
on numerous occasions throughout the Contro gli astrologi, in contrast to
singular references to other thinkers, such as Jerome, Pseudo-Dionysius the
Areopagite21 and Gregory I.22 In this context, Savonarola repeats word for
word what Pico says in the Disputationes about a unanimous rejection of astrol-
ogy amongst theological authorities. Basil of Caesarea,23 John Chrysostom,24

        territoriale al Machiavelli (Florence: EDIFIR, 2009), pp. 249–71; idem, ‘Profezia e riforma
      nel pensiero di Girolamo Savonarola’, Studi slavistici, 7 (2010), 299–311.

      falsam, ridiculam.’





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 147

Ideological Appropriation of Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes        133

Ambrose25 and Origen26 all reappear to prove Savonarola’s point. He also
translates an entire section of Pico’s Disputationes related to the prohibition of
magical rituals and studies with the use of canon and civil law, but he goes on
to expand the fragment by offering an interpretation of the Decretum Gratiani
and the decree of the Council of Toledo.27 In another paraphrase, Savonarola
translates the passage on the Code of Justinian in which, to use his words,
‘mathematical art, that is, divinatory astrology, was condemned’.28
  The analysis of the first book of the Contro gli astrologi reveals, therefore,
that despite being apparently faithful to Pico’s text, Savonarola carefully
re-orders Pico’s arguments and thereby significantly changes the perspective
of the anti-astrological attack. The attack is no longer considered as a means to
reflect on the ways errors and misinterpretations had crept into the tradition
over the centuries through corrupt translations and commentaries; rather it is
viewed as a fundamental and consistent position amongst religious authori-
ties. His aim is to demonstrate that biblical prophets and theologians rejected
astrology and that true religion can only be attained through the Scriptures.
   Savonarola’s main strategy  is developed in the second book. Whereas
Giovanni Pico had provided a detailed overview of anti-astrological literature—
from ancient Greece to French calculatores and Florentine thinkers, human-
ists and scientists—Savonarola only mentions in passing some of the ancient
philosophers already quoted in the Disputationes, including Plato, Pythagoras,
Democritus, Plotinus, Averroes, Avicenna, Favorinus and Euxodus of Cnidus.29
What he wished to achieve here was different.
  From the beginning of the ‘philosophical’ Book II, Savonarola highlights
Aristotle as the greatest authority of the ancient pagan tradition. This point
is crucial because, as is known, Savonarola charged Gianfrancesco Pico with
the job of translating and commenting on various fragments from Sextus
Empiricus’ Adversus mathematicos in order to use the arguments they contained

      impossibilem.’

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 148

134                                                  Chapter 7

against ancient philosophy.30 This caused the rise of anti-philosophical writ-
ings in Savonarola’s circle; it is worth recalling in primis Gianfrancesco Pico’s
harsh attack on Aristotle and the Aristotelian tradition.31 However, we know
that Savonarola’s attitude towards philosophy was not straightforward, as one
could imagine. He carefully studied Plato’s and Aristotle’s works and compiled
for his purposes some compendia that contained extensive excerpts from their
texts. Savonarola later used these passages in his sermons and treatises.32 These
details prove that Savonarola still regarded Plato and Aristotle as authorities in
philosophical and intellectual matters, despite his fundamental message that
no pagan philosophy should be part of religious experience. What at a glance
could seem an ambiguous and contradictory stance was, in fact, an elaborate
and learned adoption of the knowledge he aimed at dismissing. On the one
hand, the Stagirite remained the principal ancient author, ‘the best of the worst’
(to use Pico’s definition), widely criticised within Savonarola’s circle. On the
other hand, as is shown in the Contro gli astrologi, Savonarola did not deny that
Aristotle, whose influence on scholasticism and Thomas Aquinas in particular
he acknowledged, could be of some help to the Christian religion. This is why
Savonarola included philosophical positions in his anti-astrological argument.
  Thus, following Pico in general terms, Savonarola ties together two significant
theories, that of the possibility of foreknowledge and that of ens per accidens,33
both regarded from the standpoint of the philosophy of Thomas Aquinas. The
first question he poses is on the method of cognition. Here, Savonarola fol-
lows Aristotle as interpreted by Thomas Aquinas, pointing out three forms
of cognition: sensation, imagination and intellect.34 According to this view,

      Sextus Empiricus. With a Facing Text of Pico’s Quotations from Sextus’, Bruniana &

## Page 149

Ideological Appropriation of Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes        135

feelings and imagination might help one acknowledge past or present things,
but they cannot be useful with regard to predicting what will happen in the
future. The only way to foresee the future is through intellect, but it also has
limitations. However, without knowing the actual and the present, cognised
through the first two forms of knowledge, the intellect would be unable to pre-
dict the future. Thus, mathematical astrology could be considered a science
inasmuch as it explores real, present causes of natural elements and events.35
This idea, taken from Ptolemy’s Tetrabiblos, had already been put forward in
the introduction to the Disputationes, in which Giovanni Pico advances the
opposition of two forms of astrology. The same notion was transferred to the
Contro gli astrologi. Savonarola’s personal contribution consists of the follow-
ing: knowledge based on the idea of ens per accidens is not a science because
it deals with fortune, which is not predictable. The predictions contradict the
boundlessness of the unknowable divine will, which illuminates human rea-
son and makes divine secrets accessible to ordinary people. Savonarola insists
that heaven or God is a universal rather than a particular cause that governs all
natural effects in the world but leaves some room for individual initiatives.36
It is obvious that this position resembles Augustine’s doctrine of free will and
divine predestination, expounded first in the De civitate Dei and then in the
writings directed against Pelagius. Furthermore, as a theoretical basis for his
conclusion, Savonarola draws upon Aristotle and Thomas Aquinas; thus, he
borrows from the three thinkers to develop a single concept of his own. This
combination of the three major and traditional authorities permeates the rest
of Savonarola’s arguments because it enables the discrediting of astrology in
both philosophical and theological terms. Nevertheless, such a ‘Christianised’
interpretation of Aristotle was later scrutinised by Pietro Pomponazzi in the De
incantationibus and the De fato, libero arbitrio et predestinatione. Pomponazzi
made just one short step to a more radical reading of Aristotle, completely
reconsidered the whole concept and, as a result, advocated for predictions
by suggesting that natural causes (which, according to the first two forms of

       zia perché cerca di conoscere li effetti per le vere cause, come sono li eclissi e le coniunzi-
      oni de’ pianeti e simili altri effetti che procedono di necessità e sempre dalle sua cause; e
      similmente quella che cerca di conoscere certi effetti naturali che procedono quasi sem-
      pre dalla allongazione o appropinquazione del sole o dalla coniunzione e opposizione e
      moti della luna si può dimandare arte o scienzia.’
36    Ibid., p. 61. Cf. Giovanni Pico della Mirandola, Disputationes, p. 188.

## Page 150

136                                                  Chapter 7

cognition, are responsible for the knowledge of past or present events only)
might also be used to understand the future.37
   Finally, in the third book of the Contro gli astrologi, Savonarola briefly
touched upon some technical aspects of astrology. In comparison to Savonarola,
for Giovanni Pico, the practical part of astrological speculation was far more
important; two thirds of the Disputationes are devoted to refuting particular
astrological theories and their possible application. In contrast, Savonarola did
not pay much attention to this question; it evidently lay beyond his primary
interest. Nevertheless, in line with what Pico does in his treatise, Savonarola
mentions Ptolemy as the most important author who had ever written on as-
trology; at the same time, Savonarola refrains from working through the con-
tradictions within the astrological tradition. To discredit Ptolemy’s authority,
Savonarola adopts Giovanni Pico’s strategy. He claims that Ptolemy is to be
considered the prince of astrologers not only because of his foundational trea-
tises but also because of his status as a philosopher. He repeats, word for word,
Pico’s refutation of Ptolemy’s idea that philosophy should be divided into three
parts, theology, mathematics and natural philosophy, each guiding its respec-
tive field of knowledge.38 Savonarola states that Ptolemy was engaged with
mathematical astrology, whereas other, less gifted astrologers, such as Abu
Ma‘shar and Haly ibn Riḍwān, were embroiled in vanity and superstitions.39
Savonarola recalls that during the Middle Ages, astrology was in part legiti-
mised due to some works falsely attributed to prominent philosophers, such
as Albert the Great or Aristotle.40 Unfortunately, it is not possible to determine

## Page 151

Ideological Appropriation of Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes        137

whether, when referring to Albert, Savonarola had the Speculum astronomiae
in mind. Furthermore, Savonarola briefly examines the most important astro-
logical techniques, such as horoscopes,41 especially the horoscope of Christ42
and horoscopes of religions,43 as well as the astrological speculations on the
number of the celestial spheres and the signs of the Zodiac.44 Savonarola con-
cludes that such superstitious disciplines as astrology are characteristic of so-
cieties that lack true religious faith. As an example, he refers to the ancient
history of the Egyptians and Chaldeans, which perfectly correlates with the
last, twelfth book of the Disputationes.45
  Thus, in the main points, Savonarola essentially reproduced Pico’s attack on
astrology, albeit with one exception: the Ferrarese monk did it in the vernacu-
lar. Savonarola’s purpose was to point out the superstitious character of astro-
logical speculation and to claim that it had nothing in common with religious
beliefs. His motivation caused a re-orientation towards a more theological
discourse, which became evident in the first book of the Contro gli astrologi.
His appropriation of philosophical arguments was motivated by the idea of
uniting Aristotle’s theory of knowledge with the scholastic doctrine of natural
causes, as well as with Augustine’s notion of free will. These, as well as the
rest of Savonarola’s claims, relied on the narrative previously developed in the
Disputationes. Hence, a question arises: did Savonarola influence the content
of Giovanni Pico’s treatise or, on the contrary, would Savonarola’s Contro gli
astrologi never have been written without the Disputationes?
  Both Savonarola and Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola were instrumental
in spreading an image of Pico as an ardent young man who had abandoned
paganism in favour of Savonarolan spirituality centred upon the Scriptures.
When it comes to astrology, however, Savonarola went even further: he gave an
abridged interpretation of Pico’s Disputationes, which served his own ideologi-
cal campaign against the Florentine intellectuals (like Ficino). From that point
on, therefore, Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes was sometimes, and mistakenly,


41    Ibid., pp. 96–98.
42    Ibid., pp. 105–6.
43    Ibid., p. 107.
44    Ibid., p. 89–95.

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 152

138                                                  Chapter 7

seen as a vehicle for Savonarolan ideas that intellectuals had to follow or reject
depending on their political allegiances.
   It is difficult to form a clear view of Savonarola’s influence on Pico. We
still do not know the extent to which Pico’s late writings were motivated by
Savonarola’s teachings. But historical evidence suggests that Savonarola—who
was interested in broadening his knowledge of astrology and pagan doctrines
on prophecy, and who, whilst in Florence, read and studied Aristotle and
Plato—appropriated the treatise as expressing his own views. From the anal-
ysis of the Contro gli astrologi, it becomes evident that Savonarola’s treatise is
a careful manipulation of Pico’s thought. It purports to present an authentic,
vernacular and polished version of Pico’s work, but it is, in fact, an ideological
appropriation of Pico’s ideas intended to serve a very different agenda.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 153

Chapter 8
Praenotio, Prisca haeresis and Astrology:
Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola between
Savonarola and Giovanni Pico

1       Introduction

Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola, the nephew of Giovanni Pico della
Mirandola,  is known as one of the most ardent followers of Girolamo
Savonarola.1 He also authored both his uncle’s and Savonarola’s biographies.
However, as an independent thinker, Gianfrancesco Pico still remains in the
shadow of his famous mentors, although his writings are crucial for under-
standing the developments through which religious and philosophical ideas
went in the early sixteenth century.2 Promoting Savonarola’s memory, he ad-
vanced his own position, which was based on the notion that philosophy could
only serve as a handmaid to theology.
  Gianfrancesco was born in 1470 to Giovanni Pico’s elder brother Galeotto
and Bianca Maria d’Este.3 Little is known about Gianfrancesco’s formation
and education, except that he was influenced by the intellectual milieu of the
d’Este court. In contrast to his famous uncle, who had refused the inheritance
of the Mirandola counts’ estate in order to dedicate himself to philosophical
studies, Gianfrancesco became the sole heir to his ancestors’ domain in 1499.
In the following years, he was involved in a prolonged and fierce war against
his own relatives who wanted to take hold of the county of Mirandola. The
several attempts to overthrow Gianfrancesco were supported by his mother,

1  This chapter is a slightly developed version of my essay published under the same title in
   Renaissance and Reformation / Renaissance et Réforme, 42, 4 (special issue: Gianfrancesco Pico
   della Mirandola (1469–1533): Faith, Antiquity, and the Witch Hunt, ed. by Marco Antonio Piana)
   (2019), 135–58.
2  On Gianfrancesco’s thought within a broader context of religious and intellectual devel-
   opments in sixteenth-century Italy and the challenges that newly revived currents, such
   as Neoplatonism, posed to the integrity of Christian teaching, see Claudio Moreschini,
   Rinascimento cristiano. Innovazioni e riforma religiosa nell’Italia del quindicesimo e sedicesimo
   secolo (Rome: Edizioni di Storia e Letteratura, 2017).
3  For the biographical information on Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola, see Schmitt,
   Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola, pp. 11–30; Elena Schisto, ‘Introduzione’, in Gianfrancesco
   Pico della Mirandola, Vita Hieronymi Savonarolae, ed. by Elena Schisto (Florence: Olschki,
   1999), pp. 13–17.


© Koninklijke Brill NV, Leiden, 2021 | doi:10.1163/9789004442276_010   Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 154

140                                                  Chapter 8

Bianca Maria d’Este. In 1502, Mirandola was finally taken, and Gianfrancesco
was forced to leave. For nine years, he travelled across Italy and several
European countries, including Germany, where he presumably fell under the
influence of pre-Reformation ideas.4 This journey, together with his friend-
ship with Savonarola and the spreading of eschatological ideas in Italy, is
believed to have determined Gianfrancesco’s religious outlook. In 1511, Pope
Julius II helped Gianfrancesco return to Mirandola, but it was only a tempo-
rary success. During the next two decades, Gianfrancesco had to oppose the
Trivulzio family.5 In 1511, when Mirandola was captured by French troops,
Gianfrancesco Pico left for Rome, where he came into contact with Pietro
Bembo and wrote his famous treatise De imitatione.6 In 1514, he reconciled
with the Trivulzio family, but he had to concede the principality of Concordia.
This truce, which allowed Gianfrancesco Pico to return to Mirandola, proved
to be fragile. In 1533, he was killed by his own nephew Galeotto, the son of
Lodovico Pico and Francesca Trivulzio.
  The philosophical path of Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola was also
marked by significant episodes. His philosophical views were decisively influ-
enced by Giovanni Pico and Savonarola, whom he met in 1492. The impact
these two mentors had on Gianfrancesco led him to write two Vitae glorify-
ing both Giovanni Pico and Savonarola using several topoi taken from the
Christian hagiographical tradition. An illustrative example of his style is the
description of Giovanni Pico’s birth, which, the author states, was accompa-
nied by the appearance of a flame in the room; in the hagiographic tradition
this signifies the birth of a sage or a saint.7

   against the rest of his family: Schmitt, Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola, pp. 18–19.
5  By that year, his mother Bianca Maria had passed away (she died in 1506); Federico Pico died
   in 1502 due to an illness, and Lodovico Pico was killed in a battle in 1509.

   pp. 16–125.

  vum ante ipsius ortum apparuit. Visa enim circularis flamma est supra parientis matris astare
   cubiculum, moxque evancescere, fortasse nobis insinuans orbiculari figurae intellectus per-
   fectione simillimum eum futurum, qui inter mortales eadem hora proderetur universoque
   terrae globo excellentia nominis circumquaque celebrandum, cuius mens semper coelestia

   […] Legimus quippe doctissimorum sanctissimorumque hominum ortus insolita quandoque

   coetu divino nutu segregantia, summisque rebus gerendis natos indicantia.’





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 155

Praenotio, Prisca haeresis and Astrology                              141

  Gianfrancesco was only six years younger than his uncle, but, unlike him,
at the time when Savonarola came to Florence in the early 1490s, he appeared
not to have yet fully developed his own philosophical position. Evidence sug-
gests that he was, like many of his contemporaries, profoundly impressed
by Savonarola’s sermons. After 1498, that  is, after Savonarola’s execution,
Gianfrancesco did not turn his back on the Ferrarese monk’s teaching; on
the contrary, the restoration of Savonarola’s reputation and the cult around
his persona contributed, at least in part, to influencing the future direction
of Gianfrancesco’s thought. The relations between the two were friendly.
Gianfrancesco dedicated some of his early writings to Savonarola, whereas the
latter was in correspondence not only with his disciple but also with other
members of Gianfrancesco’s family.8 Furthermore, Gianfrancesco Pico insisted
that their families had been tied in the past. Savonarola’s grandfather, the fa-
mous physician and natural philosopher Michele Savonarola, was a court phy-
sician to the d’Este family and treated Gianfrancesco’s mother, Bianca Maria
d’Este.9 During Savonarola’s trial, Gianfrancesco was amongst the most fervent
defenders of the Dominican friar and even testified against the accusations of
Savonarola’s follower Pietro Bernardino.10
  Owing to the contributions of Gian Maria Cao, Gianfrancesco is today
considered the first Renaissance sceptic.11 His Examen vanitatis doctrinae
gentium, published in 1520, is the first attempt to disseminate scepticism
within Renaissance philosophy. His contribution to the Renaissance intellec-
tual culture also includes several treatises on demonology, witchcraft and fe-
male prophecy, as manifested in the Strix and the biography of Caterina Mattei
Racconigi.12 In these texts, Gianfrancesco Pico establishes a strict dichotomy

      Savonarola to Bayle (Oxford; New York: Oxford University Press, 2003).

## Page 156

142                                                  Chapter 8

between pagan inspiration, which can only lead to demonic possession and
witchcraft, and Christian inspiration, which is the only path to ecstasy and
prophecy.
  For the purpose of this book, however, the most interesting aspect of his
intellectual activity is concerned with the De rerum praenotione of 1507,
one of the most significant texts against magical and astrological specula-
tion in the Italian Renaissance that was written in the context of the early
sixteenth-century astrological controversies, which largely drew upon his
uncle’s Disputationes adversus astrologiam divinatricem.


2     The De rerum praenotione and the Quaestio de falsitate astrologiae:
       Praenotio versus Prophetia

Gianfrancesco Pico’s major contribution to the revival of Sextus Empiricus
is usually seen to be his most famous Examen vanitatis doctrinae gentium of
1520.13 However, it is worth noting that he started working on the Examen
around 1510, that is, a couple of years after the completion of the De rerum

      Routledge & Kegan Paul, 1977), pp. 32–48. Some excerpts from the text: Witchcraft
       in Europe, 400–1700: A Documentary History, ed. by Alan C. Kors and Edward Peters
       (Philadelphia: University of Pennsylvania Press, 2001), pp. 239–44.
13   The impact of Gianfrancesco Pico’s revival of sceptical philosophy was significant. He
       extensively influenced subsequent thinkers, including in particular Heinrich Cornelius
      Agrippa von Nettesheim, who published a similar treatise entitled De incertitudine et
       vanitate scientiarum atque artium declamatio invectiva. In this text, printed in 1527,
      Cornelius Agrippa criticised all forms of knowledge and sciences. Amongst the disci-
       plines Agrippa intended to disprove were magic and astrology. Remarkably, several years
        later, he would publish his legendary De occulta philosophia, containing the ideas he had
      previously criticised in a radical manner in the De incertitudine et vanitate. The main
     body of the De occulta philosophia was ready for publication before 1527, but Cornelius
      Agrippa did not proceed with its publication. The reason for his hesitation remains ob-
       scure. This, in turn, makes the publication order of Agrippa’s books one of the most in-
       triguing questions in the history of Renaissance magic. The Italian edition of the text:
      Heinrich Cornelius Agrippa von Nettesheim, Dell’incertitudine e della vanità delle scienze,
       ed. by Tiziana Provvidera (Turin: Aragno, 2004). On Agrippa’s use of Gianfrancesco, see
      Zambelli, White Magic, Black Magic in the European Renaissance, p. 134. On Agrippa’s
      scepticism, see Vittoria Perrone Compagni, ‘Tutius ignorare quam scire: Cornelius Agrippa
     and Scepticism’, in Renaissance Scepticisms, pp. 91–110. A critical edition of the De occulta
      philosophia is Cornelius Agrippa, De occulta philosophia libri tres, ed. by Vittoria Perrone
     Compagni (Leiden; New York: Brill, 1992). The first version of the De occulta philosophia
     was ready by 1510, but the final edition came to light only in 1533. On the history of the
        text, its context and intellectual impact, see Zambelli, White Magic, Black Magic in the
      European Renaissance, pp. 138–82.

## Page 157

Praenotio, Prisca haeresis and Astrology                           143

praenotione. Meanwhile, his fascination with the sceptical tradition can be
traced back to as early as the very beginning of the 1500s, that is, a long time
before the publication of the Examen vanitatis doctrinae gentium.
  These details suggest that although Gianfrancesco Pico did not mention
Sextus in the De rerum praenotione, his anti-magical radicalism might have been
caused by a close reading of Sextus’ sceptical writings during that period.14
Overall, three main sources are central to Gianfrancesco’s anti-astrological
attack: the texts of Giovanni Pico and Girolamo Savonarola, mentioned by
Gianfrancesco himself, and the philosophical tradition of scepticism, which he
helped revive. Here, the influence of Sextus Empiricus is clear but implicit; in
his later works directed against magic and astrology, Gianfrancesco mentions
Sextus much more openly and reuses his ideas to justify his anti-astrological
positions.
   In the opening pages of the De rerum praenotione, Gianfrancesco introduces
the reasons behind composing such a treatise and offers a critique of all the
ancient authors that have believed in prescience. Under the term at hand, he
understands knowledge of the future, encompassed by the terms praenotio,
prognosis as its Greek equivalent, divinatio and some others.15 His aim is to
establish a distinction between illicit and licit forms of prophetic knowledge,
the same distinction he applied to contrast the pagan Strix and the ‘living saint’
Caterina Mattei.
  He tries to show that praenotio is composed of two words (prae and notio)
and is synonymous with cognitio.16 Without limiting himself to the linguis-
tic analysis of the term praenotio, Gianfrancesco works through an impressive
number of philosophical texts, which, in turn, allows him to address his main
concern: that ancient thinkers favoured this form of knowledge. The very word
praenotio provides a reason to put forward an important distinction between
the ‘licit’ and ‘illicit’ types of prophetic knowledge. In this regard, Gianfrancesco
clearly relies on a long-standing notion of contrasting two extremes present
in every discipline—that is what Ptolemy does in the Tetrabiblos with regard
to astrology and what Giovanni Pico investigates in the Oratio with regard to
magic. In the meantime, it seems that Gianfrancesco Pico’s take on praenotio
and false prophecies received the support of several thinkers in subsequent
generations.

14   On Gianfrancesco’s readings of scepticism before 1520 when he started working on the
     Examen vanitatis doctrinae gentium, see Gian Mario Cao, ‘Inter alias philosophorum gen-
      tium sectas, et humani, et mites: Gianfrancesco Pico and the Sceptics’, pp. 127–28.
15   Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola, De rerum praenotione libri novem, I, II, pp. 6–8.
16    Ibid., I, I, p. 5: ‘Praenotionis nomen compositum est ex praepositione ipsa prae et notione
     quae idem est atque cognitio.’





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 158

144                                                  Chapter 8

   Gianfrancesco’s  anti-occult  attacks were  directed  first  of  all  against
Aristotle. He explicitly rejects Aristotle’s notion of praeexistens cognitio,17
which Cicero drew on to create the new Latin concepts of praesumptio and
notitia communis.18 Criticising these concepts as well, Gianfrancesco did not,
however, change his generally positive attitude towards Cicero. This was pre-
sumably conditioned by Cicero’s open criticism of these forms of predictions
in his De divinatione. Aristotle, whose writings Gianfrancesco considered the
ultimate expression of pagan (that is, non-Christian) philosophy, did not do so
in such an open manner. Thus, Gianfrancesco Pico’s attitude towards Aristotle
in particular and pagan philosophy in general is different from the position
of his uncle.
  Another important target of Gianfrancesco’s attacks on the notion of prae-
notio was Plato and the Platonists. The praenotio in its Platonic context was de-
veloped by Boethius. The author of the De consolatione translated the central
element of Platonic philosophy, the term idea, as praenotio or praecognitio. For
Boethius, emanation contains in se foreknowledge because it descends from
God. As an important notion, which concerns the problem of free will and the
divine predestination in Augustine’s terminology, this concept was adopted by
scholastics of the twelfth and thirteenth centuries.19
   Finally, praenotio and its variations became a central point in the philoso-
phy of Epicurus. The original term prolepsis, regarded as one of the criteria for
true knowledge in Epicurean philosophy, was often translated as praenotio and
anticipatio in its Latin interpretations. The most significant example of this
terminological transformation, however, took place after the De rerum prae-
notione, namely in the works of Pierre Gassendi, who opposed the Aristotelian
and scholastic views on the praenotio. It is quite suggestive that those thinkers
who elaborated on the notion in question after Gianfrancesco, including such
prominent scholars as Francis Bacon and Gassendi, operated within the same
set of philosophical arguments regarding the validity of praenotio.20

        intelligibilis. From Perception to Knowledge. Vol.  2. Renaissance Controversies, Later





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 159

Praenotio, Prisca haeresis and Astrology                           145

   Gianfrancesco’s attack on the praenotio has the fundamental purpose of re-
jecting all possible sorts of pagan divination. Though Gianfrancesco does not
conceal his intentions to follow Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes and especially
Savonarola, he also expands on their anti-astrological and anti-magical dis-
course. He says that his uncle Giovanni Pico had dedicated his twelve books to
the refutation of astrology, which he summarises in the fifth book, and he states
that he has nothing to add to it.21 However, Gianfrancesco insists that he will
broaden his project by refusing and rejecting all superstitions. Thus, he dedi-
cates specific chapters to various types of occultism, for example, the seventh
book to magic, the sixth to physiognomy, etc. At the same time, he opposes
to these praenotiones the unique capacity to obtain foreknowledge, namely
(in his terms) prophetia.22 According to Gianfrancesco, prophetia differs from
praenotio by its very nature. In contrast with prophecy, which is conditioned
by the divine intellect, the praenotio is considered just as a philosophical or, in
Gianfrancesco’s terms, an illicit phenomenon. To prove his idea, Gianfrancesco
adds that philosophers, specifically Aristotle and his followers, tried to recon-
cile these two forms of foreknowledge to raise the status of praenotio.23 The
reason why praenotio and its forms are so widespread is human curiosity.
Tracing back the origins of this superstition, Gianfrancesco supposes that orig-
inally every form of divination was created in ancient Eastern societies, which
had been deprived of true religion—that is, of Christianity.24 In this long pas-
sage on the religious falsity of Eastern and theurgical doctrines, Gianfrancesco
clearly argues against the idea of prisca theologia, which had become popular
in Italy and Europe thanks to Marsilio Ficino. It is also worth noting that an
almost identical passage on the gap between true religion and divination can
be found in the twelfth book of Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes.
  The De rerum praenotione was not Gianfrancesco’s sole text directed against
astrology. In 1510, he completed a short piece entitled Quaestio de falsitate

       Scholasticism, and the Elimination of the Intelligible Species in Modern Philosophy (Leiden;

         I, CIX, pp. 85–86.

     modo possunt quae praenota non fuerint.’

       dici tamen sub ipsa praenotione perinde ac ipsa prophetia concluditur.’
24    Ibid., I, VII, pp. 16–18.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 160

146                                                  Chapter 8

astrologiae.25 It had not been published during Gianfrancesco Pico’s life and
did not circulate in manuscript form. The only surviving copy of the Quaestio is
in a seventeenth-century Ferrarese codex, originally kept in the Strozzi Library
and now in the Folger Shakespeare Library in Washington, D.C.26 The Italian
scholar Walter Cavini discovered the text and published it in 1973, convincingly
arguing that the Quaestio was a synopsis of the fifth book of the De rerum prae-
notione concerned with astrology. Hence, Gianfrancesco Pico’s Quaestio was
also composed in response to the astrological debates stimulated by his uncle’s
stance. Additionally, in this text of 1510, Gianfrancesco for the first time refers
to the works of Sextus Empiricus. This makes the Quaestio the forerunner of
the Examen vanitatis doctrinae gentium and of the Renaissance sceptical tra-
dition in general.27
  The Quaestio is dedicated to one of Gianfrancesco Pico’s mentors, the hu-
manist and physician Giovanni Mainardi.28 The latter participated in the
publication of Giovanni Pico’s Opera omnia, including the Disputationes ad-
versus astrologiam divinatricem. Mainardi was an accomplished scholar who
was known as one of the most radical opponents of astrology. It comes as no
surprise, therefore, that Gianfrancesco addresses his short anti-astrological
text to Mainardi; he intended to demonstrate that the publication of the
Disputationes was not the final step in their joint dispute with astrologers. In
Gianfrancesco Pico’s opinion, their task was to coalesce in the efforts to oppose
and ultimately abolish all forms of occult knowledge.


3     The Controversial Use of (Anti-)Astrological Authorities in the
      De rerum praenotione

Gianfrancesco’s polemical strategy is clearly exposed in the sixth chapter of
Book V, which is focused on those prominent thinkers who opposed astrology,
and in which he finds himself in a rather complicated position. On the one
hand, he seeks to reproduce his uncle’s anti-astrological and historiographical
arguments, maintaining as his main source the first book of the Disputationes,
dedicated specifically to the history of astrology. On the other hand, this at-
tempt came into conflict with the idea that the philosophical tradition, in


25   Walter Cavini, ‘Un inedito di Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola. La ‘Quaestio de falsitate
       astrologiae”, Rinascimento, 13 (1973), 133–71.
26    Ibid., 134.
27    Ibid., 135–36.
28    Zambelli, ‘Giovanni Mainardi e la polemica sull’astrologia’.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 161

Praenotio, Prisca haeresis and Astrology                           147

particular that of ancient pagan philosophy personified in Aristotle and Plato,
was responsible for sowing the seeds of astrological superstitions. This visible
contradiction between his personal views and the loyalty to his uncle’s posi-
tion explains the peculiar and ambivalent nature of the De rerum praenotione.
  Gianfrancesco Pico’s wish to establish a strong opposition between pagan
philosophy and Christian religion was a response to contemporary intellectual
developments. Although Ficino is never explicitly named, Gianfrancesco Pico’s
aim appears to be to dismantle the ideal of prisca theologia and pia philoso-
phia that Ficino had developed in the previous decades, which was based on
the notion that philosophy and religion could be in fundamental harmony for
Christian piety to be restored. Gianfrancesco Pico develops the completely op-
posite view: only the strict separation between philosophy and religion, and an
exclusive focus on Christian religion, can bring faith. His use of scepticism al-
lows him to claim that pagan philosophy defies true knowledge. In this respect,
Gianfrancesco Pico uses Giovanni Pico’s deconstruction of the prisca theologia
concept in a totally different context, and astrology, which, he believed, is an
integral part of the impious ancient knowledge, comes under particular fire.
  At the beginning of his examination, Gianfrancesco Pico remains gener-
ally faithful to the Disputationes. Citing word for word his uncle, he enumer-
ates the main opponents of astrology amongst the ancient philosophers. He
lists Pythagoras, Diogenes Laertius, Plutarch and Theodoret of Cyrus, whose
anti-astrological views are mentioned in the Disputationes.29 He reproduces
the same sentence on Democritus, which describes the philosopher’s stance
on astrology, first stated in the Disputationes and then in Savonarola’s Contro
gli astrologi.30 The Stoic philosopher Panetius also reappears. The only signif-
icant difference between Gianfrancesco Pico’s and his uncle’s texts is that the
name of Seneca is not mentioned either in Giovanni Pico’s or in Savonarola’s
writings.31

         ‘Alii nec dignam putaverunt de qua verbum aliquando facerent silentio eam magis quam
     non nulli verbis condemnantes Pythagoram Astrologiae fidem non praestasse ex Diogene
      Laertio et Plutarcho et Theodereto compertum est.’ Cf.: Giovanni Pico della Mirandola,
       Disputationes, I, I, p. 46.
30   Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola, De rerum praenotione libri novem, V, VI, p. 122:
      ‘Democriti illud vulgatum est: quod ante pedes nemo spectat coeli scrutantur plagas.’ Cf.:
      Giovanni Pico della Mirandola, Disputationes, I, I, pp. 46–48; Savonarola, Contro gli as-
        trologi, p. 54.

      Stoicus et illam ipsam incessuit ex eadem porticu Seneca cuius paulo ante fecimus
      mentionem.’





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 162

148                                                  Chapter 8

  The general aim of his work, to reject all forms of paganism, leads him to
some difficulties when turning to Plato and Aristotle. According to his text
(which in some ways contradicts his preceding radical statements against phi-
losophy), Gianfrancesco admits that Plato and Aristotle did not overtly sup-
port astrology.32 He suggests that his readers thoroughly study their writings to
discover that both philosophers did not write any texts specifically dedicated
to astrology. Thus, according to Gianfrancesco Pico, Plato separated necessity
from fate, whereas Aristotle’s interest was not in fate but rather in exploring
natural phenomena, laws and causes of the universal design. Gianfrancesco
adds that even during his travels to the East, Plato escaped the malign influ-
ence of astrologers and magicians, though he would have had the opportu-
nity to learn the fundamental astrological techniques there. It is impossible to
determine whether Gianfrancesco had assumed that the works of Plato and
Aristotle had subsequently been made to legitimise astrology by their disci-
ples. In any case, his attitude towards the ancient Greek philosophers seems
to be not as straightforward as one could assume after reading the opening
passages of the treatise.
  Upon exploring the positions of Plato and Aristotle, Gianfrancesco rather
unexpectedly turns to the works of Boethius.33 In Giovanni Pico’s Disputa-
tiones, Boethius, sometimes labelled as ‘the last of the Romans’, is never listed
amongst the other ancient opponents of astrology. By placing him in this se-
quence of philosophical authorities, Gianfrancesco reveals his assessment of
Boethius: Gianfrancesco undoubtedly regards the author of the De consola-
tione philosophiae as a pagan writer.
  Following his uncle, Gianfrancesco proceeds by stressing that no influential
philosophical school ever justified astrology. He mentions Porphyry, who in-
sisted in the biography of Plotinus on the critical reaction of his teacher towards
any form of predictions. However, Gianfrancesco does not forget to mention
that there was a disagreement amongst ancient philosophers on this matter,
and Firmicus Maternus opposed Plotinus and dismissed Porphyry’s interpre-
tation of Plotinus’ vision of astrology.34 A significant difference from Giovanni
Pico is that, unlike his uncle, Gianfrancesco attributes a rejection of astrology

## Page 163

Praenotio, Prisca haeresis and Astrology                          149

to another famous Neoplatonic philosopher, namely Proclus.35 At the same
time, Gianfrancesco’s decision to include this author in his narrative seems
strange, first considering his own pro-religious and anti-philosophical stance,
and second considering Proclus’ radical position against Christians, which
caused certain difficulties for Renaissance scholars in quoting his writings or
commenting on him. In any case, Gianfrancesco repeats, usually word for word,
his uncle’s text on Carneades, Cicero and Epicurus (whose status amongst phi-
losophers remains extremely low, despite his alleged anti-astrological views).
With the same aim in mind, Gianfrancesco recalls Alexander of Aphrodisias.36
These names, generally taken from the Disputationes, allow Gianfrancesco to
conclude that all major philosophical schools of antiquity opposed astrology.
  Gianfrancesco consequently turns his attention to other important philoso-
phers, both Eastern and Western. He refers to Averroes, ‘the famous commen-
tator of Aristotle and the first amongst the Arabs who criticised, condemned
and denounced astrology’,37 and censured divination in several of his trea-
tises, sometimes using the authority of Avicenna.38 The names of Origen39
and Eudoxus of Cnidus40 are mentioned as well. The latter, already praised
in the Disputationes and the Contro gli astrologi as an expert in astronomical
studies, obtains the same status of authority in Gianfrancesco’s text. The mod-
erni are also cited in accordance with the Disputationes: Nicolas Oresme, ‘the
acutest philosopher and the most skilful mathematician’, is presented side by
side with Henry of Hesse, William of Auvergne, also known as William of Paris,
and others.41 Gianfrancesco does not omit his uncle’s contemporaries, including

## Page 164

150                                                  Chapter 8

Paolo Toscanelli, Giovanni Marliani and even the unidentified Luchinus, al-
ready mentioned in the Disputationes.42 At the same time, he leaves out two
of the most important thinkers of Giovanni Pico’s milieu: Marsilio Ficino and
Angelo Poliziano. The omission of these two scholars remains unexplained,
but it is possible that Gianfrancesco’s attitude towards Ficino and Poliziano
was determined by the ‘bad’ influence they had on his uncle during his ‘hereti-
cal’ period.
  Turning to the main supporters of astrological speculation, Gianfrancesco
adds no new names to Giovanni Pico’s list. After his uncle, he presents Claudius
Ptolemy as the most influential and competent astrologer, whose legacy was
later misunderstood and distorted by his followers. He adopts the same frame-
work as his uncle, quoting the same passage from the beginning of the Almagest
in which Ptolemy commented on Aristotle.43 A separate passage addresses
Abu Ma‘shar’s false identification of Ptolemy as belonging to the Egyptian
royal family.44 The level of Gianfrancesco’s dependence on his uncle’s text is
proven by the fact that he even spells the name of Manilius as Mallius, ex-
actly the same way as in the Disputationes.45 In addition, Gianfrancesco
falsely attributes some astrological writings to prominent philosophers and
theologians, as his uncle did. A simple enumeration of philosophical texts
reveals Gianfrancesco’s main source. Thus, his polemical strategy comprises
both Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes against astrology and his own views on the
subject. At times, the mixture causes some controversial positions because
Gianfrancesco Pico is not entirely successful in the attempt to combine the
arguments borrowed from the uncle’s treatise with his radical anti-astrological
and anti-philosophical ideas.
   In the next chapter of the De rerum praenotione, entirely devoted to the
rejection of astrology by means of theology and law, both ecclesiastical and

civil, Gianfrancesco manages to avoid such contradictions.46 Although he still
follows the arguments of the Disputationes, he obviously feels much more
comfortable engaging in the discussion through his own approach. He is more
confident in working with the sources quoted throughout the passage and
does not limit himself to retelling his uncle’s stance; rather, he adds original
thoughts to expand upon biblical quotations. After having demonstrated the
religious grounds for opposing astrology, Gianfrancesco does not lose an op-
portunity to criticise those Christian writers who shrugged off what he saw as
the fundamental contradictions between Christianity and astrology.


4       Prisca theologia as prisca haeresis

Book VII of the De rerum praenotione is devoted to magic, and here Gian­
francesco Pico refers specifically to Pierre d’Ailly and Roger Bacon. In this
part of the treatise, Gianfrancesco Pico is not only drawing on his uncle’s
work—which, as we have seen above, devoted a substantial section to the two
authors—but also to Marsilio Ficino’s De religione christiana, a work that is
alluded to numerous times in the De rerum praenotione. In defining magic, Gi-
anfrancesco Pico asserts that for him, magic is a dangerous form of idolatry
related to incantations and demons.47 In his view, like other occult sciences,
magic first appeared in Persia, and it was later disseminated in Egypt, Babylon
and Greece.48 Whilst explaining the significance of magic in ancient cultures,
he uses the same quote from Porphyry that his uncle uses in the Oratio de homi-
nis dignitate to legitimise magical speculation.49 Gianfrancesco points out that
in all ancient societies, magicians possessed important status in social and cul-
tural hierarchies. Their official titles could differ, but their functions remained

      pro noscendis occultis ex aliis compluribus aere, aqua quae supra cum divinandi vanitate
      reprobavimus.’

      dicatur sicuti Horatiano placuit intepreti non a magis particula deducitur non Graecum,
     non Chaldaeum, non Aegyptium sed Persicum est. Magorum nomine apud Persas auc-
       tore Porphyrio divinorum interpretes et cultores indicabantur: apud alios scriptum inven-
     imus eos a Persis magos appellari qui elementis numen tribuerent.’
