                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 166

152                                                  Chapter 8

the same. In this passage, he implicitly refers to the De religione christiana of
Marsilio Ficino. Unlike the Florentine philosopher, however, Gianfrancesco
does not praise the similarities in magic doctrines across various ancient soci-
eties. On the contrary, he focuses on their common fallacies.50
   In contrast to his contemporaries and medieval scholars, who often under-
lined the dual nature of magic,51 Gianfrancesco distinguishes three forms of
magic. Two of them had originated in Persia. The first, created by prominent
Persian magicians, remained within Persia without being disseminated out-
side its borders, whereas the second listed by Gianfrancesco Pico dealt with
incantations and necromancy. Finally, the third form, known as ‘natural magic’,
eventually spread across Eastern countries and was later transferred to the
West.52 His understanding of ‘natural magic’ presumes that this form of magic
was consequently supplemented with other doctrines outside of Persia. Hence,
‘natural magic’ received an ‘international’ status. Gianfrancesco assumes that
the Greeks were introduced to magic during their wars against the Persians,
whereas the Romans adopted magic from the Gauls and their magicians (dru-
ids) and often sought various means of prediction.53 As Gianfrancesco states,
this explains why augurs, haruspices and dream interpreters became espe-
cially influential in Roman society.
  Gianfrancesco Pico’s main interest here obviously lay in countering natural
magic, which had its supporters in various intellectual circles in Renaissance
Italy. He was undoubtedly familiar with his uncle’s contribution to this prob-
lem and with the idea of natural magic being a ‘servant’ to theology that was

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 167

Praenotio, Prisca haeresis and Astrology                           153

widespread in medieval and Renaissance texts. By outlining a gradual develop-
ment of magic from Near Eastern societies to the Greece of Plato and his dis-
ciples, Gianfrancesco reformulates the myth of prisca theologia in a negative
light. He admits that magic took root within the European philosophical and
religious discourse, though several Christian writers, such as Origen, Augustine
and John Chrysostom, had warned of its destructive character. Referring again
to Giovanni Pico, he does not criticise his uncle’s favourable views regarding
natural magic, ascribing to him the important role of being a ‘historiographer’
of magic.54 Thus, he accurately analyses the magical views of three prominent
medieval thinkers: al-Kindi, Roger Bacon and William of Paris. This approach
echoes Giovanni Pico’s Oratio de hominis dignitate,55 but Gianfrancesco devel-
ops a completely opposite argument. In the two chapters directed specifically
against al-Kindi and Roger Bacon,56 Gianfrancesco reconsiders their status
within the medieval tradition, disproving their arguments to unite magic with
philosophy and, in the case of Roger Bacon, with Christian theology.
  Focusing on the origin of astrology, Gianfrancesco insists that curiosity is
particular to human beings, but it may have a negative impact on people who
are far from objective knowledge. These people can easily fall under the malign
influence of astrologers and other magicians. Thus, Gianfrancesco Pico repeats
the main arguments formulated in Book XII of the Disputationes, which, as we
have seen, can be regarded as the most doubtful in terms of Giovanni Pico’s
authorship. In addition, Gianfrancesco reframes the geographical and cultural
boundaries of the prisca theologia. Under the banner of pagan antiquity, which
he opposes as a concept, Gianfrancesco Pico unites several ancient doctrines
widely known in Florence and in the rest of Italy during the late fifteenth cen-
tury, citing the Assyrians, along with the Egyptians, Chaldeans and Arabs.57
The Assyrians were considered to be very close to the Chaldeans, and Marsilio
Ficino translated Iamblichus’ Reply to Porphyry’s Letter to Anebo the Egyptian
with the title On the Mysteries of the Egyptians, Chaldeans, and Assyrians. This
reference to the Assyrians proves that already in the early sixteenth century the
doctrine of prisca theologia was subject to modification. During the sixteenth
century, Renaissance historiographers typically made additions to the general

## Page 168

154                                                  Chapter 8

list of prisci theologi. Thus, for example, in his De perenni philosophia, Agostino
Steuco da Gubbio includes the Armenians in the list of the prisci theologi.58
Gianfrancesco, who, contrary to Agostino Steuco, did not support the doctrine
of prisca theologia, includes the Jews in the list of ancient theologians and in-
tends to revise the Jewish legacy, along with a critique of the Brahmans and
gymnosophists.59 He states that instead of listening to their prophets, the Jews
got embroiled in magical speculation.60 Gianfrancesco Pico’s opposition to the
Jews appears to be radical. There are two possible explanations for his attitude.
On the one hand, he clearly refers to the ancient tradition of Jewish mysti-
cism, which Giovanni Pico supports in his early writings and which, accord-
ing to Gianfrancesco Pico, caused his uncle’s break with the Church. On the
other hand, Gianfrancesco might have been suggesting that the Jews included
some preaching practices in their mysticism. The passage in question is sim-
ilar in essence to Savonarola’s preaching strategies, which contrasted super-
stitious people with those who listened to and followed true prophets. In any
case, Gianfrancesco Pico’s negative attitude towards Jewish philosophy and
Kabbalistic mysticism finds its firm confirmation in this fragment. His main
objective remains, however, to reject the intellectual validations of astrology. It
is worth noting that at the beginning of the De rerum praenotione, he mentions
two thinkers who criticised various forms of superstition. First, he refers to
Tatian the Assyrian, who reproached the Romans for being loyal to divination.
It is difficult to understand how Gianfrancesco overlooked Tatian’s heretical
status and focused solely on his anti-astrological views. Along with Tatian,
he also mentions a Christian writer, the ‘blessed saint Jerome from Florence’.
Under that name, he evidently meant his teacher Girolamo Savonarola.61
  Gianfrancesco Pico insists that his intention is to rid true religion of pagan
superstitions, divinations and other dangerous heretical elements. Declaring
that his treatise is based on Giovanni Pico’s arguments, he does not fail to
indicate his uncle’s significant errors. It is difficult to understand whether
Gianfrancesco Pico ‘forgave’ his relative for the interest in occult sciences, but
he, nonetheless, remained faithful to Giovanni Pico’s legacy, in which he con-
sidered there was no place for the magical 900 Conclusiones and the Kabbalistic
Heptaplus. Thus, meticulously deconstructing the prisca theologia as prisca

      oracula consulenda placuerit. Sed divinationem aliunde sibi multifariam procuravere.’

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 169

Praenotio, Prisca haeresis and Astrology                            155

haeresis, Gianfrancesco finds himself in an ambiguous position between
Savonarola’s anti-philosophical scepticism and Giovanni’s anti-astrology. This
ambivalence becomes even more evident in Gianfrancesco’s reconstruction of
the natural philosophical arguments against astrology.


5       Aristotle and Natural Philosophical Arguments against Astrology

The general structure of Gianfrancesco Pico’s arguments differs from that
of the Disputationes. Unlike his uncle, Gianfrancesco does not focus on nat-
ural arguments against astrology in any specific chapter. He first presents
pro-astrological natural arguments, which he then proceeds to reject. From the
beginning of his examination, Gianfrancesco Pico makes Aristotle responsible
for the philosophical foundations of astrology. This makes for the most sig-
nificant difference between Gianfrancesco Pico and his uncle. Whereas in the
Disputationes, Giovanni Pico della Mirandola chooses Aristotle over Plato as his
major authority in eliminating the possibility of all astral influences, his neph-
ew’s attitude towards Aristotle seems to be far more negative. Gianfrancesco
considers Aristotle to be the main vehicle for the dissemination of astrological
superstitions. This is because, as mentioned above, Gianfrancesco Pico’s aim
is to establish a strict separation between paganism, which is considered the
cause of either superstitions or philosophy, and the Christian religion. In his
opinion, no one can reach divine truth by relying solely on pagan philosophy.
  Thus, Gianfrancesco states that Aristotle legitimised astrological spec-
ulation after determining the close links between its superior and inferior
effects.62 Such a dependence on celestial influences and their impact on the
terrestrial world opened the door to a philosophical justification of astrology.
Aristotle thus had a significant influence on the subsequent philosophical tra-
dition, causing the dissemination of astrology, which then gained a high posi-
tion amongst the other sciences. Gianfrancesco also accuses the subsequent
philosophical tradition of the diffusion of Aristotle’s ideas, but his main attack
is directed against the entire body of Aristotle’s works; it is also worth noting
that he does not distinguish between the Stagirite’s original writings and those
falsely attributed to him.
  Gianfrancesco Pico additionally enumerates the primary fields in which
astrology could be applied. Agriculture, medicine and navigation were quite

62    Ibid., V, II, p. 101: ‘Peripateticorum princeps Aristoteles in primo meteororum libro scribat
      necesse esse mundum hunc inferiorem superioribus lationibus esse contiguum ut omnis
       eius virtus inde gubernetur.’





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 170

156                                                  Chapter 8

common areas of application. He also adds several natural phenomena that
could be predicted on the basis of astrological calculations. If Aristotle created
a philosophical basis for astrological speculation, then Ptolemy adapted and
adopted his ideas and introduced further practical astrological techniques.
In this passage, Gianfrancesco reiterates his uncle’s idea that Ptolemy had in-
tended to comment on Aristotle and had tried to reconcile his philosophy with
astrology.63 However, rather than considering Ptolemy an erroneous inter-
preter of Aristotle, as Giovanni Pico does in the Disputationes, Gianfrancesco
Pico states that Aristotle’s philosophy was responsible for sowing the seeds of
superstition in Ptolemy’s system.
   In order to reject all attempts by astrologers to vindicate their doctrine,
Gianfrancesco Pico borrows from his uncle’s arguments and, in some cases,
uses ideas from Savonarola. He decisively rejects the main astrological question
of celestial causality, repeating the notion that heaven must be considered a
universal cause, which cannot produce particular effects. Here, Gianfrancesco
Pico remains loyal to his mentors and to the long-standing anti-astrological
tradition, which includes the writings of medieval scholastics. In addition,
he reproduces Giovanni Pico’s central polemical strategy of highlighting that
astrologers are not competent in their subject because they contradict each
other in their description of the main astrological techniques. Such contra-
dictions, according to Gianfrancesco Pico, do not allow astrologers to make
accurate horoscopes or predict any sort of future event.64
   Referring to the second point, which deals with the inconsistencies
within the astrological tradition, Gianfrancesco alludes to Book III of the
Disputationes. He distinguishes between astrological speculation and real nat-
ural events, which can be predicted through mathematical calculations. From
Giovanni Pico’s text, he derives the notion that the Sun and the Moon produce
the only significant influence on the sublunary world. Any other potential im-
pact, such as that of Zodiac signs or celestial bodies and planets, is nothing
but the product of speculative assumption. Gianfrancesco argues that natural
events, such as the changing seasons, are not defined by astrological means but
depend exclusively on natural causes.65
  To prove this, Gianfrancesco Pico follows Giovanni Pico and Girolamo
Savonarola and works through the central notions of natural philosophy,
namely light, motion and heat. Using his uncle’s arguments, he rejects the pos-
sible lunar influence on the tides. Borrowing from Giovanni Pico, he mentions

63    Ibid., p. 102.
64    Ibid., V, III, p. 103.
65    Ibid., V, IV, pp. 106–13.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 171

Praenotio, Prisca haeresis and Astrology                            157

amongst the opponents of the ‘astrological’ theory of tides a certain Adelandus,
who is also referred to as the first disciple and follower of Ammonius.66 The
identities of both Ammonius and Adelandus remain unknown. Giovanni Pico
uses the name ‘Adelandus’ to denote the famous medieval astrologer and
translator Adelard(us) of Bath. It is not unlikely that Gianfrancesco Pico re-
peats his relative’s error. Trying to prove his acquaintance with the astrolog-
ical tradition, Gianfrancesco argues against its other significant proponents.
Thus, he criticises Alpetragius (al-Bitruji) for his explanation of celestial mo-
tion and rejects Roger Bacon’s interpretation of the influence of the lunar light.
However, despite all these attempts to demonstrate his expertise in the sub-
ject, Gianfrancesco Pico clearly draws upon the information provided in his
uncle’s writings.67
  Gianfrancesco also claims that astrologers falsely attributed a considerable
power to the Moon. As an example, he refers to the ancient and medieval phy-
sicians (Galen, Pierre d’Ailly and others) who determined the critical days of
an illness by the position of the Moon.68 Gianfrancesco concludes that as-
trologers are completely unable to predict the future. Their calculations are
far from being precise, and the disagreements amongst astrologers in practical
matters only reinforce his doubts.
   In addition, Gianfrancesco rejects the doctrine of animated spheres, as
well as the practice of creating Zodiac signs and giving them personalised
characteristics.69 He severely opposes the attempts to correlate astrology with
the four seasons or with the four types of bile. He claims that the geometrical
figures of celestial bodies are not substantiated either. Gianfrancesco states
that all these astrological practices are speculative and cannot be proven with
exact calculations; therefore, they should not be considered scientific. The

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 172

158                                                  Chapter 8

application of this kind of false mathematical data is extremely dangerous
in all the three main domains related to astrology: medicine, agriculture and
travel. It is worth noting that, apart from astrologers, Gianfrancesco also places
a responsibility on philosophers whose teachings gave rise to further astrolog-
ical speculations. Thus, in marked contrast to his uncle, he repeatedly empha-
sises the negative role of Plato, Aristotle and their disciples in the development
and dissemination of astrology.
  Whilst focusing on the theoretical and practical elements of astrology,
Gianfrancesco consistently discusses these questions with clear references to
the relevant chapters of the Disputationes adversus astrologiam divinatricem.
He refers to various debates on the number of spheres, uncertain properties of
celestial bodies and other theoretical questions that had not been solved by as-
trologers over the centuries in order to question the possibility for astrology to
formulate reliable predictions about future events. In the end, Gianfrancesco
comes to the conclusion (already articulated in the Disputationes) that human
life is not an appropriate subject for celestial influences or astrological calcula-
tions and that it is in reality conditioned only by natural phenomena. Life is not
strictly determined and leaves space for individual freedom. Gianfrancesco re-
jects fate as a philosophical concept, denies the Platonic notion of fortune and
other interpretations put forward by ancient philosophers and consequently
dismisses astrological speculation as such.


6      Conclusion

To summarise Gianfrancesco Pico’s views on astrology, it is worth looking
through the Quaestio de falsitate astrologiae, a compendium from the De rerum
praenotione that reproduces the main polemical strategies, as well as the pri-
mary questions, posed in his major treatise on the subject. Gianfrancesco re-
peats his arguments about the Eastern origin of astrology and its close relation
to non-Christian practices that oppose the Scriptures and the Church Fathers.70
His arguments against practical matters remain the same: in the application of
astrological knowledge, astrologers usually contradict each other and are una-
ble to determine the number and the motion of celestial spheres, nor can they
attribute any specific properties or characteristics to planets, Zodiac signs and



70   Walter Cavini, ‘Un inedito di Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola. La ‘Quaestio de falsitate
       astrologiae”, 138–40.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 173

Praenotio, Prisca haeresis and Astrology                           159

other celestial bodies.71 He severely criticises astrologers for their persistent
attempts to predict important historical events using the astrological theory
of great conjunctions.72 His primary aim is to refute the same pro-astrological
authorities—that is, ancient philosophers, Ptolemy and Eastern magicians—
pitting them against the traditional set of writers, including Augustine and
Thomas Aquinas.
  However, whilst considering anti-astrological texts in detail, Gianfrancesco
faces the same difficulties as in the De rerum praenotione. His position be-
comes controversial when he supports the authors against whom he had re-
cently fought. He repeats the structure of Giovanni Pico’s historiographic first
book of the Disputationes, consecutively enumerating philosophers, theolo-
gians and legislators who opposed astrology. At the same time, in his analy-
sis of Plato and Aristotle, Gianfrancesco finds himself in a deadlock trying to
reconcile Giovanni Pico’s attitude with his own religious radicalism. All pagan
philosophy is to be rejected, and so is every possibility of applying astrology in
some domain.
   Gianfrancesco’s attitude towards astrology and magic was unequivocal: he
was a severe critic of every form of occult knowledge. However, the way he
stood against it was not free from ambivalence. On the one hand, he insisted
that philosophical arguments are a good tool for the development of astrol-
ogy and magic. His opposition to philosophy was obviously conditioned by
his interest in scepticism and the position of one of his teachers, Girolamo
Savonarola, who had thoroughly studied and consequently opposed philo-
sophical texts. On the other hand, however, a complete rejection of philosophy
seemed to enter into conflict with the approach of his famous and beloved
relative, Giovanni Pico della Mirandola. This ambiguity provoked his rather
unconvincing attempt to reconcile these two approaches. This inconsistency
overreached particular treatises against magic and astrology and caused the
ambivalence in Gianfrancesco Pico’s texts. Being under the influence of the
two bright figures equally important to him, Savonarola and Giovanni Pico,
Gianfrancesco Pico had to make a final choice either to refrain from glorifying
his uncle or to counterbalance Savonarolan religious radicalism with Piconian
thought. It seems that in the De rerum praenotione and the Quaestio de falsi-
tate astrologiae, Gianfrancesco does not manage to find a reliable solution to
this issue.

## Page 174

Chapter 9
With ‘Latins’ Against ‘Latin Vice’: Maximus
the Greek on Astrology


As is widely known, the Disputationes provoked a great deal of discussion on
astrology not only in Italy but also across Europe. The diffusion of Giovanni
Pico’s work was accompanied by the transmission of other forms of knowledge
from Italy, such as Neoplatonic philosophy and Kabbalistic literature, both of
which were often envisaged in the context of the prisca theologia. This deter-
mined the multi-layered nature of the reception of Giovanni Pico’s views, in-
cluding his attack on astrology and other occult sciences. Recent studies have
traced responses to the Disputationes in several parts of Europe.1 However, the
spread of such debates to sixteenth-century Muscovy seems rather unexpected.
  The reception of Western influences on early modern Russian thought re-
mains an unexplored field of research. The pioneering studies by William Ryan
shed light on the destiny of the Secretum Secretorum and its use for medical
and magical purposes in the Russian lands during the late fifteenth-sixteenth
centuries. The translation of the Secretum from Hebrew was connected with
the so-called ‘Judaizer movement’ that arose in Novgorod and Moscow in the
late fifteenth century.2 The location of Novgorod and its permanent trade

1  Cf.: vanden Broecke, The Limits of Influence; Westman, The Copernican Question.
2  In the late fifteenth and early sixteenth century, intellectual elites of Moscow and of
   Novgorod, a large commercial city in northwest Russia, were involved in religious and po-
    litical debates. Although, at the time, the controversies at the court were centred on the
   question of ecclesiastical land ownership, for the benefit of their polemical strategies, the
   opposite parties applied religious argumentation. Thus, apart from discussing the economic
   and political issues, they accused their opponents of doctrinal errors and even heresy. A new
   group, which consisted of several courtiers, clergymen and other senior officials, quickly
   gained influence at the court of Grand Princes Ivan III and Vassily III. According to their op-
   ponents, who sought to discredit them in the eyes of the Grand Princes and church authori-
    ties, the characteristic feature of the group was their adherence to Judaism. That is why, since
   the late fifteenth century, they have often been called the ‘Judaizers’. At two church councils,
   in 1490 and later in 1503, the ‘Judaizer movement’ was officially prohibited as a heresy, but
   the word was widely used after 1503 and appears in numerous polemical texts composed
   in the sixteenth century. Some studies have described the ‘Judaizers’ as either a movement,
   which supported a pro-European development of Muscovy through the transmission of
   Renaissance learning, or a group of religious freethinkers. However, the very existence of an
   established group, the coherence of its teaching and the religious character of the ‘heresy’
   are highly doubtful. It is also worth noting that despite its name, which at first glance refers

With ‘Latins’ Against ‘Latin Vice’                                         161

contacts with the Hanseatic League opened the door for the diffusion of some
elements of European culture.
  The Secretum, often called in Old Russian manuscripts ‘The Gates of
Aristotle’, gave rise to speculations on magic in Muscovy, but it was banned in
the sixteenth century, together with the group of several clergymen and senior
officials labelled as the ‘Judaizers’.3 The destiny of the Secretum also contrib-
uted to the negative attitude towards Aristotle in the Old Russian sources.4
Another text composed within the Novgorod context was the Laodicean
Epistle by Feodor Kuritsyn.5 Under the patronage of the Novgorod-Muscovite
intellectual  circles, some other magical and astrological  texts, including

   to the Jewish tradition, in fact, the etymology of the word had nothing to do with Judaism
   per se. After the great schism, the term was frequently used in Byzantine sources to stig-
   matise the ‘Latins’ or the pro-Latin/pro-Catholic opposition, whose thought, according to
   the Byzantine tradition, found its grounds in Jewish books, that is, in the Old Testament.
   Afterwards, the term migrated into Russia, which hosted numerous Greek émigrés after the
    fall of Constantinople, and became an important political tool. With the Judaizer label, its
   opponents simply tried to get rid of the influential group, first in Novgorod and later at the

Sacrobosco’s De sphaera and Maimonides’ texts, were translated for the first
time into Russian.6
  Some scholarly articles have explored specific episodes in the history of
the diffusion of European magical texts and practices in early modern Russia,
though these works reveal the diffusion’s singular and sparse nature.7 It ap-
pears that until the middle of the seventeenth century, Russian intellectuals
did not adopt Western knowledge in a systematic way. Only starting with
Alexei I and then Peter the Great did the role and use of European sources in
Russian intellectual culture significantly increase.8
  There were similar trends in art. Some remarkable examples of European
influence can be found in the architecture of the late fifteenth and sixteenth
centuries, especially in the construction of the Kremlin cathedrals.9 This ten-
dency, however, was not prolonged. The tradition, largely based on Byzantine
and post-Byzantine artistic models, went through significant transformations
in the late seventeenth century.

## Page 177

With ‘Latins’ Against ‘Latin Vice’                                     163

   In this context, Maximus the Greek, a central figure in Russian intellec-
tual culture in the first half of the sixteenth century and the first intellectual
to begin a more systematic reception of European Renaissance culture in
Muscovy, attracts particular attention. Almost thirty years after the publica-
tion of the Disputationes, Maximus, who had lived in Italy in the 1490s and
witnessed the culmination of the astrological controversies caused by the
Disputationes’ publication, wrote in Muscovy his own three Epistles Against
Astrology. He directed these texts against Catholic preachers in Moscow, call-
ing astrology a ‘Latin vice’.10 Despite his close ties with Savonarola and Pico’s
family, he does not mention any ‘Western’ sources in the writings. This chapter
will show that Maximus used Giovanni Pico’s and Savonarola’s treatises, whilst
simultaneously rejecting Italian Renaissance culture as such. Although at first
sight, Maximus’ motivation seems to have nothing in common with the astro-
logical polemics taking place in Italy, I argue that his Epistles Against Astrology
draw upon the astrological disputes in Italy and, therefore, are an integral part
of them. Moreover, this case study on the diffusion of Giovanni Pico’s and
Savonarola’s anti-astrological ideas allows us to expand the boundaries, both
geographical and intellectual, of their success.11 This chapter will also explore
Maximus’ strategies of describing and presenting the European Renaissance to
the Muscovite audience and his fierce anti-Catholic position.
  Born in 1470 or 1475 in the small town of Arta in Greece, Maximus—or more
precisely Michael Trivolis, since at the end of the fifteenth century he had not
yet become a monk—came to Italy in 1492.12 Over a period of twelve years,
he had travelled to various Italian cities, where he had met many prominent

10   These three texts were published in Maximus the Greek, Сочинения, vol. 1, ed. by Nina V.
       Sinitsïna (Moscow: Indrik, 2008), pp. 256–94; 311–34; 359–72.

      Европейское Возрождение и русская культура XV—середины XVII вв.: контакты и
     взаимное восприятие, ed. by Oleg F. Kudryavtsev (Moscow: ROSSPEN, 2014), pp. 92–102;
      idem, ‘“Latin Vice” and “Hellenic Charm”: Maximus the Greek and Renaissance Debates
     on Astrology in Sixteenth-Century Muscovy’, in Cultural Encounters: Cross-disciplinary
       Studies from the Late Middle Ages to the Enlightenment, ed. by Hanna Gentili et al.
      (Wilmington, DE; Malaga: Vernon Press, 2018), pp. 59–72. I would like to thank Vernon
      Press for allowing me to use some parts of my essay in this publication.

     итальянском периоде жизни преподобного Максима Грека’, Вестник церковной

      pp. 21–89. See also Vladimir S. Ikonnikov, Максим Грек и его время. Историческое
      исследование (Kiev: Tipografiya imperatorskogo universiteta Svyatogo Vladimira, 1915);

       et philosophique de Michel Trivolis (Paris; Louvain: Desclée et de Brouwer, 1943).

## Page 178

164                                                  Chapter 9

Italian scholars and familiarised himself with Italian Renaissance culture. After
an initial period in the circle of the noted Greek Renaissance scholar Janus
Lascaris, he spent some time in Florence. From his own writings, we know that
in Florence, he became a close follower of Savonarola. After Savonarola’s ex-
ecution, Michael did not break the ties with the Ferrarese friar’s disciples; in
1498, he became the secretary of Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola and lost
his position only after his patron’s exile. Michael also spent some months in
Venice, where he worked for Aldus Manutius.13 Some preserved letters, which
are signed as being sent from Mirandola and ‘the house of dominus Aldus
Venetianus’, shed new light on the place of Michael amongst Greek émigrés in
late fifteenth-century Venice.14 Years later, when finally in Muscovy, he would
compose a short text on Aldus Manutius and his typography.15
   After the short period in Venice, Michael returned to Florence to become
a member of the Dominican order at San Marco, the monastery that owed its
success in fifteenth-century Florence to Savonarola’s presence.16 Although for
a long time Michael was believed to have taken monastic vows at San Marco,
some newly discovered evidence suggests that for almost ten months, he lived
at the monastery only as a novice and had, in fact, never become a monk.17
A brief overview of Michael’s activities reveals that his career was typical for
a Greek émigré in Renaissance Italy. At different stages, he served as a scribe,
secretary and probably a translator to different patrons, whilst simultaneously
maintaining strong links with the Greek diaspora. Even the fact that he almost
joined the Dominican order corresponds to the ways in which Byzantine émi-
grés often assimilated into Italian society; examples of such conversions from
Orthodoxy to Catholicism were not rare.18
  However, a couple of years later, Michael drastically changed his life’s path.
In 1504, he returned to Greece, to Mount Athos, where he took Orthodox
monastic vows at the monastery of Vatopedi and had his name changed to
Maximus. In 1516, he was invited to Muscovy by Grand Prince Vassily III to
translate Greek prayer books into the Church Slavonic language.19 However,

## Page 179

With ‘Latins’ Against ‘Latin Vice’                                     165

at the time when Maximus finally settled in Moscow, he did not know Church
Slavonic and initially had to work in cooperation with his Russian associates.
However, after learning the language, he went beyond his initial duty of trans-
lating prayer books; numerous texts, including those on politics, social life
and theology, are the result of his activities during the thirty years he spent in
Muscovy. Most of them had a clear anti-Catholic bias.
  Maximus knew Angelo Poliziano and other Italian humanists, whose leg-
acy he severely criticises in the writings directed against the ‘Western mode of
thinking’. Describing the diffusion of astrology and pagan philosophy in Italian
society, he respectively applies two main terms: ‘Latin vice’ and ‘Hellenic charm’.
Maximus expresses this negative attitude towards Renaissance thought in the
Words Against Juan Luis Vives.20 Completed around 1530, this work presents a
severe critique of Juan Luis Vives’ interpretation of Augustine’s De civitate Dei.
It is evident from the text itself that Maximus had not actually read Vives’ com-
mentary, which was published in 1522, that is, already after Maximus’ move
to Moscow; meanwhile, Maximus’ real aim was to persuade his Muscovite
readers that the Catholic Church was corrupted. He rejects the very idea of in-
terpreting religious texts through the use of alternative methods and sources.
In opposition to the attempts to reconcile a ‘humanist’ reading of Scripture
with theology, he presents his Florentine mentor Girolamo Savonarola as an
example of a true religious reformer, one whose teaching did not deal with text
but with spirit.21 But the most interesting example of Maximus’ anti-Latin and
anti-Catholic position can be found in the Epistles Against Astrology.
  The dating of these three texts is uncertain. Two of them, those of a more
theoretical nature, were composed before 1524, whereas the third one rejects
the possibility of a new Deluge, which was predicted to take place in 1524.
Since my primary task is to show the links between Italian anti-astrological
treatises and Maximus’ works, I shall devote less attention to the third epistle,
which has already been studied by Robert Collis.22
   All these writings are dedicated to Maximus’ friend and patron Feodor
Karpov, one of the leading Russian diplomats of that time and a well-known

## Page 180

166                                                  Chapter 9

intellectual, and are directed against Nicholas Bulev or Bülow, also known as
‘Nemchin’ (Бюлев and Немчин in Russian). Little is known about Nicholas,
who was an astrologer and physician at the court of Grand Prince Vassily III.23
In Russian sources, Nicholas is mentioned as Lübchanin (Любчанин); this
means that he was from Lübeck, Germany. This also explains his other nick-
name, Nemchin, which derives from ‘nemets’ (немец) in modern Russian.
This word, meaning ‘dumb’, is still applied to Germans and formerly referred
to people from most Western nations; even a Frenchman or an Englishman
could be called a ‘nemets’. Nicholas studied in Rostock and then, after a short
stay in Novgorod, spent some years in Rome. On the invitation of Vassily’s
ambassador to the Holy See, Nicholas arrived in Russia, where, as his oppo-
nents claimed, he sought to popularise Western thought, including astrology.
His attempts in this direction included a translation into Russian of Johannes
Stöffler’s prediction about the Deluge of 1524. He was also responsible for
translating from German and disseminating the first Russian medical ency-
clopedia, the Blagoprokhladniy vertograd of 1534. Nicholas was a Catholic and,
according to his adversaries, tried to convince the Grand Prince and his milieu
of the closeness of the Western and Eastern branches of Christianity. Naturally,
this activity was identified by Nicholas’ Orthodox opponents as proselytism.
One of the most severe antagonists of Nicholas—regarding not only his as-
trological speculation but, in particular, his attempt to unite the Catholic and
Orthodox Churches—was the former novice of the Dominican order Maximus
the Greek. Thus, the three Epistles Against Astrology were intrinsically linked to
Maximus’ attacks against the Catholic Church and its doctrine. But as we shall
see, he uses in this work some arguments against astrology allegedly taken
from Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes and Savonarola’s Contro gli astrologi.
   In his anti-astrological Epistles, whilst focusing on the political application of
astrology, Maximus clearly addresses the Grand Prince. Accordingly, Maximus
uses some examples to demonstrate the history of the spiritual degradation of
Western Christians. He mentions that it was a Roman custom to appeal to var-
ious magicians, especially during wars.24 To these erroneous attempts to pre-
dict the future he opposes the ‘great’ examples of the past, with Constantine
the Great, Moses and others attributing their destiny and deeds to God instead
of astrology and magical ceremonies.25 He also recalls Russian recent history,

      Petersburg: Borei art, 2002), pp. 5–14.
24   Maximus the Greek, Сочинения, vol. 1, pp. 318–19.

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 181

With ‘Latins’ Against ‘Latin Vice’                                      167

claiming that Mamai, the leader of the Golden Horde’s army in the legendary
battle in Kulikovo, appealed to the astrologers for advice, whereas the Russian
Grand Prince Dmitry Donskoy did not partake at all in magical speculations
and remained loyal to the Christian religion. The first victory over the Mongols
in Kulikovo in 1380 had had a great spiritual effect, of which Maximus and his
readers were aware.26 In addition, Maximus notes that the court astrologer
of Duke Lodovico Moro announced to his patron that he would win the war
against Charles VIII of France, which started in 1494 and, as it is known, ended
in the total defeat of the Milanese ruler.27 Maximus admits that the cause of
the diffusion of magical beliefs was related to the religious crisis in Western
Europe and its radical shift from ‘true’ Christianity to pagan philosophy. This
idea flows through the whole corpus of Maximus’ writings.
  Many texts written at that time represent astrology as a ‘Latin vice’, a theme
that became the central point of the famous Letter Against Astrologers by
Philotheus of Pskov, which significantly contributed to the political and reli-
gious claims of the Muscovite grand princes to become the leaders of Eastern
Europe and the Orthodox world in general. Although the famous concept
of ‘Moscow the Third Rome’ became extremely popular much later, it was
originally coined in Philotheus’ anti-astrological writing. In it, whilst oppos-
ing the Latins’ superstitious belief in astrology and divination, he suggests
the Muscovite grand duchy would succeed Rome and Constantinople as the
third Christian major centre after both cities had been destroyed because of
their religious and moral corruption.28 Philotheus’ letter against astrologers
is addressed to Mikhail Munekhin and is directed against the same Nicholas
Bülow, whose influence upon Vassily III Maximus the Greek tried to eliminate
with his Epistles. Thus, from the sixteenth century onwards, anti-astrology in
Russia had clear political implications due to the position held by Maximus

## Page 182

168                                                  Chapter 9

and Philotheus. In their works, astrology as an erroneous tool for predictions
was strongly related to the question of fate. Maximus claims that by exploring
the wheel of fortune, astrologers limit divine omnipotence.29 The idea of dis-
couraging the rulers’ interest in astrology as a reliable method of predicting the
future and in Western intellectual culture in general, became widespread in
early modern Russian anti-Catholic writings. It received support even though
in the sixteenth and seventeen centuries it was common for Russian tsars to
have personal astrologers. Maximus’ and Philotheus’ anti-Catholicism was
considered to be an effective strategy in political polemics with other courti-
ers, whilst astrology became an important political tool at the court of the first
Romanovs.30
  That being said, in a number of his arguments, Maximus clearly refers to the
tradition he openly opposes. First of all, attention should be paid to the term div-
inatrix, used by Pico, Savonarola and Maximus.31 From the time of Ptolemy’s
Almagest and Tetrabiblos, there was a clear distinction between judicial as-
trology, mired in superstitions, and mathematical astrology, considered to be
a significant and reliable part of natural philosophy. This distinction between
the two branches of astrological knowledge is emphasised in various medieval
texts; Giovanni Pico also refers to it in the Proemium to the Disputationes.32

     and by human self-will’). As regards Savonarola, his criticism seems to be more general,

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 183

With ‘Latins’ Against ‘Latin Vice’                                    169

Savonarola uses the same idea underpinning the opposition between astro-
logical speculations and the study of real (natural) causes of terrestrial events,
which is possible only through divine inspiration,33 though he does not men-
tion the distinction between the two kinds of astrology. Meanwhile, Maximus’
attempt to point out the divinatory character of astrology in his Epistles seems
rather strange if not useless. In sixteenth-century Russia, Ptolemy’s astrolog-
ical or astronomical texts were scarcely known, and the legacy of Western
medieval thinkers remained virtually unknown. The cosmological views were
based on religious sources, primarily Byzantine, often modified after they be-
came popular in Russia. In this regard, Maximus’ motivation to stress the word
предрицательный (equivalent of divinatrix) remains unclear; most probably,
he did so unintentionally in replicating Pico’s title.
  Furthermore, it is interesting to compare the structure of all the afore-
mentioned anti-astrological works. As we have seen above, the Disputationes
overviews all possible arguments against astrology, in the fields of philosophy,
theology, as well as history and law, and is thus structured accordingly. In his
vernacular paraphrase of Giovanni Pico’s treatise, Savonarola modifies this se-
quence in order to adapt the arguments to his own purpose. However, unlike
Pico and Savonarola, Maximus decided to limit himself to religious matters.
This is understandable enough: in Russia, no one knew the names of Abraham
ibn Ezra, Thomas Aquinas or Roger Bacon, let alone their original writings. For
Maximus, who spent many years in Italy, these names might not have been a
complete terra incognita, but he should have done a lot of preparatory work
had he decided to introduce his Muscovite audience to Western thought.
  Conditioned by his focus on religious context, Maximus’ main argument
is that astrological predictions are at variance with the Christian doctrine of
free will. For him, the astrologers’ claim to be able to predict the future ques-
tions God’s omnipotence. Moreover, since, according to him, astrology was im-
ported to Russia by Nicholas ‘Nemchin’,34 this danger acquired a confessional
character. Using numerous quotations from the Bible and religious literature,
Maximus tries to prove the demonic nature of divination. In this respect, his po-
lemical treatises seem to be rather conventional, and I cannot agree with Nina


33    In particular: Savonarola, Contro gli astrologi, pp. 37–38: ‘In molti luoghi della sacra
       Scrittura sono detestati quelli che vogliono predire le cose future senza illuminazione
      divina […] Perrocché non si chiamano divinatori quelli chi prenunziano le cose le quali
      ordinatamente procedono dalle cause naturale o sempre o quasi sempre, perché questo
      è concesso a l’uomo e è cosa umana; ma quelli che senza speciale illuminazione divina
     presumano di prenunziare le cose future.’
34   Maximus the Greek, Сочинения, vol. 1, pp. 268, 359.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 184

170                                                  Chapter 9

Sinitsïna and Robert Collis, who has largely supported Sinitsïna’s supposition,35
that in his anti-astrological reasoning Maximus follows Giovanni Pico and crit-
icises astrology with the use of anthropological arguments. Moreover, Sinitsïna
sought to find some parallel motifs related to the subject of human autonomy
in Western and Eastern thought: in her opinion, Maximus’ attempt to reject
astrology echoes not only Pico’s Disputationes and the Oratio de hominis dig-
nitate but also Erasmus’ De libero arbitrio diatribe sive collatio of 1524.36 My
analysis suggests the opposite: the very character of Maximus’ epistles, their
polemical orientation and sources are quite traditional, whereas his central
premise is to demonstrate how corrupt the Catholic Church had become.
   In elaborating on the history of the spiritual degradation of Western
Christians, Maximus refers to some examples. In his opinion, the best proof
of ‘dechristianisation’ was the work of three contemporary Italian thinkers:
Niccolò Lelio Cosmico da Ferrara, Agostino Nifo and Angelo Poliziano.37
Against the background of this accusation, some remarks about ‘Latin’ sources
in Maximus’ treatises seem to be especially curious.
  Unlike Pico and Savonarola, Maximus uses only a few sources in his Epistles.
In his three short texts, he generally quotes the Bible and the works of the Holy
Fathers. Moreover, because he was Orthodox and an opponent to the Latins,
Maximus preferred to quote the Greek Fathers instead of the Latin ones, which
was also strongly linked to the problem of his readership, since Maximus’ au-
dience was more familiar with Eastern Christian sources. He made only two
exceptions to this strategy, and I think that these two cases unveil the influence
of Savonarola and, probably, of Giovanni Pico upon Maximus’ argument.
  The first case is related to a quotation from Augustine, the only Latin Father
mentioned in the Epistles. In the first Epistle, Maximus refers to the De Genesi
ad litteram, a text that was amongst Augustine’s least known treatises in
sixteenth-century Muscovy. In the passage in question, Maximus says:

     That astrological doctrine is produced by diabolical instigation is attested
    by saint Augustine of Hippo, who said in the first chapter of the De Genesi
    ad litteram: And if astrologers often correctly predict future events, it is
     not made through divine inspiration, but through devil’s hidden insti-
      gation, because human minds that are not sufficiently prepared for this,



35    Collis, ‘Maxim the Greek, Astrology and the Great Conjunction of 1524’, 610–12.
36    Sinitsïna, Максим Грек, p. 151.
37   Maximus the Greek, Сочинения, vol. 1, pp. 362–63.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 185

With ‘Latins’ Against ‘Latin Vice’                                           171

    become subdued; and thus, through such predictions they find them-
      selves in the devil’s hands.38

Curiously, an almost identical quotation can be found Savonarola’s Contro gli
astrologi:

      Li sacri teologi ancora tutti detestano questa astrologia divinatoria di-
    mostrando che è falsa e perniciosa, massimamente santo Augustino in
     molti luoghi, e tra li altri nel secondo libro sopra el Genesi ad litteram
      dice: ‘Quando li matematici, cioè gli astrologi, predicono qualche cosa
      vera, dovemo dire che la predicano per istinto occultissimo di demonii,
      el quale istinto patiscono le mente umane che non se ne avvengano.’39

It should be emphasised that Savonarola quotes this passage at the beginning
of the second book of his treatise. In Russia, Maximus had access to neither a
manuscript of the De Genesi ad litteram nor a copy of Savonarola’s work. He
had to quote from memory, and it is easier to keep in mind a phrase from the
beginning of the text one tries to memorise, as it was in the case of the Contro
gli astrologi. If he had used Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes, which abounds with
quotations from a number of Augustine’s works, it would have been strange
that he had memorised one passage only and eliminated all other possible
sources for his own argumentation. Additionally, in his treatise, Pico does not
specifically quote this passage from the De Genesi ad litteram. The fact that
Maximus quotes it from memory is attested by his own mistake; he was sure
that this passage originated from the first book of the De Genesi, whereas
Savonarola indicated the right place: the beginning of the second book. Thus,
it seems quite plausible that in his Epistle Against Astrology, Maximus quotes
not Augustine himself but Augustine in Savonarola’s interpretation.

   In the second instance that refers to a Latin source, Maximus recalls earlier
philosophical disputes on astrology. It is the only passage in which Maximus
goes beyond the theological limits of his anti-astrological polemic.40 In the
First Epistle to Feodor Karpov, he says:

     Indeed, astrologers are vain and ignorant and full of every lie and false-
     hood, because they have lost God and their souls are enslaved by demons;
     they are not the prophets of God, inspired by the Holy Spirit, witnessing
     piety and salvation, but those of the servants of the Pythian spirit, bad
    men, supporters of every evil, Chaldeans, Babylonians, Egyptians, Arabs
    and Phoenicians, who were converted to the evil ab origine, and after that
     everybody followed the lie of Epicurus and Diagoras,41 who was named
     impious for this. Because neither Socrates,42 nor Plato, nor Aristotle, who
    were considered the most honest amongst the Greek philosophers and
     those who loved the truth most of all, as it becomes clear from their writ-
      ings, agreed with astrological falsehood. And Aristotle, who was aware of
       its falsity and condemned it, despite all the useless efforts of astrology to
     look like art, mentioned it nowhere in his works.43

It seems interesting that in this quotation, Maximus, unlike both Giovanni Pico
and Savonarola, divides major Greek philosophers into two groups. In the first

## Page 187

With ‘Latins’ Against ‘Latin Vice’                                      173

book of the Disputationes, Giovanni Pico, on the contrary, tries to show that all
the outstanding philosophers had been opponents of astrology; he starts with
Pythagoras, saying:

     That Pythagoras did not believe in astrology was attested by Theodoret
      [of Cyrus], Diogenes Laertius and Plutarch.44

We can find almost the same text, with the similar expression praestasse fidem,
in Savonarola’s Contro gli astrologi, though without any reference to sources.45
The case of Epicurus seems to be more complicated. Savonarola does not men-
tion him at all, whereas Giovanni Pico, according to his ‘historiographic’ idea
that all major philosophical schools rejected astrology, mentions Epicurus
amongst those philosophers who had not tolerated astrology. However, he does
not miss the opportunity to condemn the Greek philosopher’s views, saying:

   How great the astrological madness should have been, if even Epicurus,
    who affirmed so many delusions, did not support it!46

As regards Plato and Aristotle as opponents of astrology, Maximus, Giovanni
Pico and Savonarola were unanimous. Probably refuting the philosophical
foundations of astrological speculations, Maximus disagreed with the idea
that ancient philosophy and religion might be reconciled. If so, it means that in
dismissing the prisca theologia, he remained a loyal follower of Savonarola and
Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola. It is also quite possible that the Orthodox
monk, who had quoted this passage from memory already in Russia, simply
kept in mind the principal piece of information, namely the fact that the lead-
ing Greek philosophers, Plato and Aristotle, were inclined against astrology.
Maximus was probably not a good expert in philosophical matters; this can
be confirmed by the way he perceived Socrates and Plato as two separate and
independent philosophers. By recalling the names of ‘Diagoras’ and Epicurus,
he intended to show to his Russian readers the degree to which astrological
ideas had diffused in Europe.

Obviously, Savonarola adopted Pico’s passage on Aristotle’s De caelo, and it is
possible that Maximus recalled this passage from the Contro gli astrologi and,
for this reason, did not mention Plato.
  To sum up, the three Epistles Against Astrology written by Maximus the
Greek at some point around 1524 are a perfect example of anti-Latin polem-
ics in sixteenth-century Russia. Their author, who had earlier intended to be-
come a Catholic monk, turned into an ardent opponent of Catholicism and,
in general, of the Western mode of thinking. Having based his attack on as-
trology, considered a ‘Latin vice’, on the Bible and the works of the Greek Holy
Fathers, he tries to underline the gap between the true Christian religion and
a corrupted Catholic Church embroiled in superstitions, false faith and here-
sies. To show his orientation towards the Orthodox theological tradition and to
preserve the doctrines of human free will and God’s omnipotence, which, ac-
cording to him, are undermined by the astrological speculations, he quotes the
Greek Fathers (Basil the Great, Gregory Nazianzus, John Chrysostom, John of
Damascus and many others), whereas amongst the Latin Fathers, he mentions
exclusively Augustine. Unlike his Italian predecessors, Maximus the Greek
limits his anti-astrological remarks to theological aspects, all but omitting





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 189

With ‘Latins’ Against ‘Latin Vice’                                      175

philosophical and natural arguments against predictions. Nevertheless, in
some cases, when he needs to quote the Latin Fathers or ancient philosophers
like Aristotle, he references the works of Savonarola and Giovanni Pico della
Mirandola without mentioning their names. Thus, Maximus’ wish to discredit
the Latin/Catholic astrological ideas in orthodox Russia forced him to appeal
to the very legacy against which he, in fact, fought.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 190

Chapter 10
Lucio Bellanti and the Return to
‘Christian Astrology’


As discussed in the previous chapters, Giovanni Pico’s attack on astrology
found support amongst several well-known thinkers of the late fifteenth and
early sixteenth centuries. Their motivations to reject astrology were mostly re-
lated to religious intentions. The rise of anti-astrological literature was building
on previous traditions, but it received additional impetus after the publication
of Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes. However, Pico’s influence and mythical sta-
tus as the greatest intellectual of his day did not prevent some intellectuals
from opposing his ideas. The list of Giovanni Pico’s critics includes prominent
Renaissance philosophers and humanists. In what follows, I shall focus on the
three most important figures who did not share Giovanni Pico’s opinion on
astrology: Lucio Bellanti, Giovanni Gioviano Pontano and Francesco Zorzi. The
first two, Bellanti and Pontano, read the Disputationes before its publication
in 1496. Thus, their reaction to Giovanni Pico’s treatise was immediate, which
allows us to place their writings into the context of anti-astrological polemics
without taking into consideration the subsequent political, philosophical or
scientific interpretations. The case of the third one, Francesco Zorzi, perfectly
illustrates how Savonarola’s and Gianfrancesco Pico’s appropriation of the
Disputationes’ arguments shaped Pico’s image amongst early sixteenth-century
Italian scholars. A fervent admirer of Pico’s earlier Kabbalistic texts, in several
passages of his enormous and ambitious De harmonia mundi, Zorzi gradually
distances himself from the author of the Disputationes. As we shall see, al-
beit from different perspectives, having various, and sometimes contrasting,
sources in mind, all three authors discussed in the following part sought to
revive the same version of astrology, the foundations of which they believed
had been shaken by Pico’s attack and its subsequent pro-Savonarolan inter-
pretation. To begin, it is worth having a closer look at Lucio Bellanti, whose
response to the Disputationes, chronologically first, determined some of the
central arguments within the anti-Piconian polemics.
   It is hard to reconstruct Bellanti’s biography.1 We know that he descended
from a noble Sienese family. The exact date of his birth is unknown, as is his

1  On Bellanti’s biography, see Cesare Vasoli, ‘Lucio Bellanti’, in Dizionario biografico degli ital-
    iani, vol. 7 (Rome: Istituto della Enciclopedia Italiana, 1965), pp. 597–99.


© Koninklijke Brill NV, Leiden, 2021 | doi:10.1163/9789004442276_012   Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 191

Lucio Bellanti and the Return to ‘Christian Astrology’             177

university and intellectual background. The only evidence at our disposal
about the latter is that Bellanti called himself a magister artium et medicinae,
although the place where he obtained his degree is uncertain. In 1483, his name
comes to light in the sources describing political conflicts in Siena. Bellanti
took an active part in those political controversies, but the party to which
he belonged was defeated, and Bellanti was forced to leave his hometown. It
seems that his exile did not last long; already in 1487, Bellanti had reappeared
in Siena. During those four years, the political situation changed, and the new
political regime was favourable to Bellanti’s family. In the subsequent period,
Bellanti played an important role in Sienese politics; he was involved in the
war against Florence, as well as in the political clashes between the previous
government and Pandolfo Petrucci, the future ruler of Siena. After Petrucci’s
victory over Bellanti’s party, the latter had to leave Siena. Although Bellanti’s
brother begged Petrucci to show mercy, and Petrucci seemed to be inclined to
do so, Bellanti refused to yield to Petrucci and never returned to Siena.
   In 1495, he appeared in Florence for a brief period of time. There, he sup-
posedly learned about the upcoming publication of the Disputationes adversus
astrologiam divinatricem, which started disseminating amongst Florentine in-
tellectuals and immediately caused some debates. By then, Bellanti had be-
come a recognised astrologer. What contributed to his fame in particular was
that he made Giovanni Pico’s horoscope and correctly predicted that Giovanni
Pico della Mirandola’s life would only last thirty-one years. The accuracy of
the prediction astonished his contemporaries and granted Bellanti prestige
amongst fellow astrologers. Thus, when he was informed that Gianfrancesco
Pico della Mirandola and other thinkers from the circle of Savonarola intended
to publish Giovanni Pico’s anti-astrological text, Bellanti expressed his ex-
tremely negative attitude towards this initiative. It is probable that the motive
to compose Pico’s horoscope in the first place was that Bellanti highly praised
the Count of Mirandola. In this regard, a prospective publication of a text in
which a person whom he greatly admired denounces any validity of his pro-
fession might be the main premise of Bellanti’s response to the Disputationes.
Nevertheless, it is still unclear whether Bellanti initially intended to picture
Giovanni Pico as a pro-astrology scholar. What he aimed to do instead was to
oppose the attack on astrology as something initiated, in Bellanti’s opinion,
due to the malign influence of Savonarola.
  His appearance in Florence in 1495 caused a mistake in subsequent
scholarship.2 The idea that Bellanti completed his Responsiones in disputationes

2  Pompeo Faracovi, ‘In difesa dell’astrologia: risposte a Pico in Bellanti e Pontano’, in Nello
   specchio del cielo, p. 47.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 192

178                                                  Chapter 10

Ioannis Pici adversus astrologos during his short stay in Florence was first
promulgated in the eighteenth century; it was believed that he had decided to
print it in Bologna. However, there is no text with that date amongst the books
printed in Italy in the fifteenth century. The first confirmed publication of the
Responsiones is dated 9 May 1498, that is, four days after the incarceration of
Girolamo Savonarola and fourteen days before his execution. This suggests
that Bellanti’s Responsiones was primarily directed against Savonarola and his
influence upon Florentine intellectuals, such as Giovanni Pico, rather than
against Giovanni Pico himself. Such a coincidence points to the political rather
than intellectual motivation of Bellanti’s treatise, as well as to its relation to the
persecution of Savonarola. In 1502, four years after Savonarola’s execution and
three years after Bellanti’s mysterious death in Venice, the Responsiones was
re-published alongside Bellanti’s other pro-astrological treatise, the De astro-
logica veritate. Several decades later, both treatises were published for the third
time in Basel under the following titles: De astrologica veritate and In defensio
astrologiae contra Ioannem Picum Mirandulam.3
  Both texts are significant within the astrological polemics of the late fifteenth
and early sixteenth centuries inasmuch as they are devoted to the legitimisa-
tion of astrology. At the same time, only the second treatise, the Responsiones
in disputationes Ioannis Pici adversus astrologos, explicitly tackles Giovanni
Pico’s attack on astrology. In contrast, in the Liber de astrologica veritate,
Bellanti deals with the general and theoretical questions concerning astrol-
ogy. Although the essence of Bellanti’s pro-astrological arguments in the latter
book is not to be ignored, it should be regarded as a supplementary source for
a study of his anti-Piconian views. The structure of both treatises proves that
Bellanti was not a fervent supporter of new humanist intellectual trends. His
Latin is far from being elegant and refined, particularly when measured by the
standards established in the writings of some of his contemporaries. For in-
stance, the Liber de astrologica veritate consists of twenty ‘quaestiones’, which
is a clear reference to the genre of medieval ‘disputationes’, although with-
out the structure of ‘propositiones’ and ‘oppositiones’ typical of both medi-
eval scholastics and many Renaissance writers, including Pietro Pomponazzi.
The Responsiones in disputationes Ioannis Pici adversus astrologos, which will
be the focus of this chapter, is divided into twelve parts, which correspond
to the books of Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes. This approach allows Bellanti


3  I refer to the edition of 1502: Lucio Bellanti, Defensio astrologiae contra Ioannem Picum
   Mirandulam. Lucii Bellantii Senensis mathematici ac physici liber de astrologica veritate. Et
   in disputationes Ioannis Pici adversus astrologos responsiones (Venice: per Bernardinum
   Venetum de Vitalibus, 1502).





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 193

Lucio Bellanti and the Return to ‘Christian Astrology’             179

to consider Giovanni Pico’s main anti-astrological arguments in succession,
showing his readers which of his particular arguments were wrong and to what
extent. In contrast to the Liber de astrologica veritate, the Responsiones seems
to be modelled after the medieval scholastic practice of ‘oppositiones’. Judging
by the structure of both writings, Bellanti stood closer to the medieval philo-
sophical tradition rather than to humanism, Neoplatonism or other ‘innova-
tive’ movements of that period. Such preliminary observations about the form
and style of Bellanti’s treatises and his medieval background receive further
confirmation in what constituted Bellanti’s pro-astrological arguments.
  To understand why Bellanti devotes so much time to medieval astrologers,
it might be helpful to look back at the elements that formed the understand-
ing of ‘Christian astrology’ and its late medieval developments. Grounded in
the Aristotelian teaching on natural philosophy interpreted astrologically,
‘Christian astrology’ implied a distinction between two crucial ideas of an-
cient philosophy: fate or predestination (severely criticised due to its incom-
patibility with Christianity) and the chain of physical, natural causes.4 The
latter concept, which was originally coined in Stoicism, went through many
transformations, first in ancient Greece and later amongst Islamic Peripatetic
philosophers before reaching the Latin West. Bellanti’s principal argument in
favour of astrology was centred on reconciling the doctrine in question with
the critique thereof formulated by Alexander of Aphrodisias in the De fato.
Bellanti does so by insisting that the Stoic causal chain may be compatible
with the Christian notion of human free will. To legitimise the use of astrol-
ogy and achieve his goal of incorporating the elements of Stoic teaching into
the notion of ‘Christian astrology’ without affecting the integrity of Christian
theology in essence, Bellanti makes wide use of medieval scholastics. This ex-
plains Bellanti’s constant references to Thomas Aquinas and John Duns Scotus
throughout the Responsiones, aimed at stressing the similarities between the
concept of predestination and the medieval Aristotelianised astrology.
  Thus, these features determine Bellanti’s polemical strategy, so that his
response focuses on reconsidering two main points of Giovanni Pico’s argu-
ments. First, Bellanti admits that the ‘historiographical’ sketch of astrology and
its sources in the Disputationes has nothing to do with the actual interpretation
of astrological texts. Second, Bellanti strives to re-establish the agreement be-
tween astrology and theology that Pico had sought to destroy. In order to do so,
the Sienese scholar works through the same philosophical categories—such
as light, natural and particular causes, and some others—which are central in


4  Pompeo Faracovi, ‘In difesa dell’astrologia: risposte a Pico in Bellanti e Pontano’, pp. 52–55.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 194

180                                                  Chapter 10

Book III of the Disputationes. Finally, Bellanti pays particular attention to the
problem of the alleged textual distortion of the Disputationes.5
   Bellanti starts his response to Giovanni Pico by addressing the question of
astrological authorities. His aim is to disprove Giovanni Pico’s arguments one
after another. He begins with Homer and Caecilius, whose names are men-
tioned at the beginning of the introduction to the Disputationes. He even uses
the same quotation from the Bible, pointing at the ‘wolves in sheep’s cloth-
ing’ who seduce people under the guise of close friends, specifically with el-
egant speeches.6 Although the citations from ancient poets and the Bible
were used in the Disputationes to demonstrate the corrupt nature of astrology,
Bellanti turns Giovanni Pico’s argumentation against Pico himself. Having rad-
ically changed the direction of Pico’s rhetoric, Bellanti admits that those who
pass themselves off as virtuous and caring people whilst criticising astrology
and astrologers are actually guilty of confusing everyone.7 Bellanti insists
that their attempts are inappropriate and can only be made by those who are
unfamiliar with the subject. Astrology, he adds, was never in violation of civil
or ecclesiastical law and over the centuries proved to be useful and almost
necessary in various fields of human activity, including medicine, travel and
agriculture,8 with all three being traditionally listed in this context.
   Bellanti then points to the transformation of Giovanni Pico’s views of astrol-
ogy. For him, discrepancies and contradictions within Giovanni Pico’s works
lead to serious doubts about the authenticity of the Disputationes. According
to him, Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola’s assertion that his uncle did re-
turn to the fold of the Church is a significant proof that the text of Giovanni
Pico’s treatise had been distorted to serve the interests of Savonarola and
his disciples. Bellanti reproduces several figures of speech taken from the
Disputationes to show his reader that he generally follows the structure of

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 195

Lucio Bellanti and the Return to ‘Christian Astrology’              181

Giovanni Pico’s treatise. Thus, he opens the paragraph on the development
of Pico’s astrological views with the phrase primum omnium scire lectorem
volo, which introduces the reader to the history of astrology and its sources in
Book I of the Disputationes. Bellanti refers to Pico’s earlier works and identi-
fies several contradictions between them and the Disputationes. He mentions
the Apologia addressed to Lorenzo de’ Medici and the ‘speech to the cardinals’
(that is, the Oratio de hominis dignitate), who would later condemn the 900
Conclusiones. In Bellanti’s opinion, in these treatises, Giovanni Pico supports
astrology, whereas his radical volte-face can only be caused by Savonarola’s in-
fluence on Giovanni Pico, which resulted in an intervention into the content
of the Disputationes.9 Savonarola’s further work against astrology also attests
to it. Obviously, the account of Bellanti, whose treatise was published right at
the time when Savonarola had been thrown in prison, was conditioned by the
political context in which it was written. The main goal of Bellanti’s text was
to put forward new charges against Savonarola, who, in Bellanti’s view, had
distorted the text of the Disputationes or at least appropriated Giovanni Pico’s
arguments to serve his own ideological agenda. Despite the controversial char-
acter of this testimony, it seems to have gained popularity in the late fifteenth
century onwards and to have given birth to the notion that Giovanni Pico’s last
treatise might be spurious.
  Because Bellanti’s task is to reconsider, step by step, Giovanni Pico’s argu-
ments against astrology, he begins by analysing the legacy of those authors
who, according to his opponent, rejected astrological speculation. Without
paying much attention to chronology, he shows that Giovanni Pico’s attribu-
tion of most prominent thinkers to the camp of anti-astrologers was false. First
of all, he discusses the two greatest thinkers of antiquity: Plato and Aristotle.
In the Disputationes, as we have seen, Pico argues that none of them took the
question of astrology into consideration. For him, the silence is the best evi-
dence of their opposition to astrology. According to Bellanti, this argument is
far from being convincing, even if it was widely accepted over the centuries.
Bellanti does not deny that in their writings, Plato and Aristotle do not touch

  mortem in Apologia ad Laurentium Medicem et in oratione ad Cardinales diligenter inspex-
    erit. Ibi quidem comperiet quantum virtuti caelorum tribuat, multa quoque cognoscet quae

## Page 196

182                                                  Chapter 10

upon the problem of how the stars influence the terrestrial world, but he looks
at this fact from another standpoint. He recognises that Plato does not men-
tion astrology, along with many other topics, anywhere in his dialogues. In his
opinion, though, this proves that both Aristotle and Plato, who were largely
involved in the study of nature and natural effects, equated astrology with as-
tronomy. This allowed astrology to obtain mathematical apparatus, as well as
an honourable place amongst other sciences in the Ancient world.10 The ar-
gument used by Bellanti echoes the medieval and Renaissance traditions that
Pico had tried to dismantle, which tended to equate astrology and astronomy.
  As regards other authors mentioned in the Disputationes, Bellanti uses the
same polemical strategy. He recognises that Pythagoras and Democritus did
not approve of astrological speculation, as it was shown in Giovanni Pico’s
treatise. However, he adds that they were believed to be the first to incorporate
occult studies into ancient philosophy, though Bellanti does not go in depth
to explain this pioneering contribution.11 It is likely that here Bellanti refers
to the works falsely attributed to Pythagoras, such as the Golden Verses, which
were central to the prisca theologia. He also assumes that Seneca, though an
opponent to astrology, could not leave aside the problem of fate and fortune,
as he generally belonged to the Stoic school.12 According to Bellanti, another
famous opponent of astrology, Cicero, did not criticise astrology as such, but
rather unprofessional and ungifted astrologers who were apparently unable to
provide accurate predictions.13 In a similar manner, Bellanti insists on recon-
sidering Giovanni Pico’s words about Plotinus’ criticism of astrology. He states
that Firmicus Maternus and Porphyry are more competent and more objective
in exploring Plotinus’ astrological views than Giovanni Pico, who proposed
a radical anti-astrological interpretation.14 Besides, Bellanti acknowledges
the legacy of the Aristotelian tradition, assuming that both Alexander of
Aphrodisias and Averroes were involved in philosophical debates on fate and

## Page 197

Lucio Bellanti and the Return to ‘Christian Astrology’            183

necessity strongly tied with the problem of astrology and predestination. It
is worth noting that his phrase about Averroes’ doctrine of the unity of the
intellect, officially prohibited in the Middle Ages, is obscure. From Bellanti’s
own words, it is not clear whether he sought to re-establish the doctrine as
valid or simply criticised Giovanni Pico for referring to the philosopher whose
false theory had been completely rejected.15 Bellanti dismisses the opinion
that Ptolemy’s philosophical interpretation of Aristotle was rather superficial
and essentially poor. Without having any particular doubts in regard to the
credibility and accuracy of Ptolemy’s astronomical/philosophical studies, the
Sienese physician affirms that Ptolemy fairly gained the highest status amongst
his fellow astrologers.16 Thus, whilst working through the same sources and
authorities to which Giovanni Pico referred in the Disputationes and eventu-
ally deconstructing Giovanni Pico’s deconstruction of auctoritates, Bellanti
intends to restore the position of astrology. He aims at achieving this by ana-
lysing all these texts in a completely opposite way.
   Bellanti then goes on to mention the Florentine humanists and scientists
who, he believes, wrote in support of astrology, and the way Bellanti reconsid-
ers their contribution to the astrology/anti-astrology dichotomy is revealing in
terms of the manner in which he constructs his argument. Aside from Giovanni
Marliani and a certain Luchinus, both mentioned in the Disputationes,
Bellanti refers to Paolo Toscanelli. Toscanelli, whose calculations influenced
Christopher Columbus’ decision to explore a new route to the East, was listed
amongst anti-astrologers in the Disputationes. Bellanti, in contrast, recalls that
Toscanelli obtained the reputation of a professional astrologer and even made
horoscopes for some members of the Medici family. He supposes that even
Leon Battista Alberti supported astrology and maintains in the De architec-
tura that its impact might potentially be positive.17 Finally, Bellanti mentions
Marsilio Ficino’s De vita libri tres, in which Ficino endorses applying astrology
and magic for medical purposes, as well as Niccolò Leoniceno, whose writ-
ings, according to Bellanti, do not testify against astrological knowledge. The
most complicated case Bellanti has to face is Angelo Poliziano. In order to
reject Poliziano’s criticism of astrology, or at least to show his lack of astro-
logical knowledge, Bellanti claims that Poliziano was not familiar with astro-
logical/astronomical techniques and that his expertise in astrology was rather



15    Ibid.
16    Ibid., pp. 180–81.
17    Ibid., p. 180.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 198

184                                                  Chapter 10

superficial, notwithstanding his attempts to improve it.18 Bellanti argues that
this assessment of Poliziano’s engagement with astrology demonstrates that
Giovanni Pico’s attempt to assign his close friend to the team of anti-astrologers
is erroneous and groundless.
  Recognising ancient and medieval astrologers, Manilius, Guido Bonatti and
Pierre d’Ailly, and demonstrating that their writings do not contradict but rather
complement Christian teaching are Bellanti’s following steps. Contrary to what
Pico argued, Bellanti claims that ecclesiastical decrees were not at variance
with astrological speculation.19 Bellanti goes on to state that the Church only
prohibited those forms of magical practices that contradicted the essence of
the Catholic doctrine. Astrology at its highest level could, however, be used to
prove and confirm Christian dogmas. Following the ‘theological’ apology of as-
trology, Bellanti establishes a list of early Christian and medieval thinkers who
supported this notion. His aim is to challenge and invert the ‘historiographical’
component of Pico’s argument by giving a new status to the authors who were
previously declared opponents of astrology in the Disputationes. Generally re-
maining within a traditional Aristotelian framework, Bellanti seeks to restore
astrology’s status as a legitimate discipline compatible with both natural phi-
losophy and the Christian religion. Strengthening his argument by constant
references to Aristotle and medieval doctores, namely Thomas Aquinas and
John Duns Scotus, he reiterates that the ‘Christian’ form of astrology is worth
being accepted, mostly for the sake of Christianity itself. Thus, unlike Giovanni
Pico, who dedicates the bulk of the Disputationes to the controversies on as-
trological calculations, Bellanti avoids practical matters and focuses on main-
taining a balance between theological and natural philosophical arguments in
favour of astrology.
  To challenge Pico’s arguments in Book III of his treatise, Bellanti refutes his
central thesis, according to which the heavens cannot simultaneously be the
universal cause and determine particular terrestrial events. This led Giovanni
Pico to deny all possible causal connections between celestial and terrestrial
