                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 199

Lucio Bellanti and the Return to ‘Christian Astrology’            185

elements on which astrological predictions are based. By adopting his main
polemical strategy, Bellanti eventually uses Pico’s weapon against Pico. In
the Responsiones, he refers back to Aristotle, on whose authority he relies in
advancing two assumptions radically opposed to Giovanni Pico’s naturalis-
tic views.
   First, the Sienese physician claims that the heavenly bodies contain in se
specific qualities through which they may produce a large variety of effects.
Therefore, he assumes that each planet affects the world in a characteristic
manner of its own. Such influences of celestial bodies are caused by the prime
mover, which defines the movement of the heavenly spheres. The correspond-
ence between the celestial spheres and the action of the prime mover was taken
from Thomas Aquinas and other medieval scholastics.20 At the same time,
Bellanti admits that some characteristics are common to all heavenly bodies.
As an example, he recalls that the Moon and the Sun are not the only bodies
capable of producing rays and celestial heat; according to Bellanti, these vir-
tues are typical of other planets and stars as well. To make his argument more
solid, Bellanti refers to the Aristotelian tradition and even names Aristotle
and Ptolemy amongst those who expressed similar opinions on the nature
of celestial influence. His intention is clear; in Book III of the Disputationes,
Giovanni argues the opposite: that the sole celestial bodies to have an influ-
ence on earthly events are the Moon and the Sun. Bellanti aims to denounce
Giovanni Pico’s words with the use of the same textual evidence, in this case
taken from Aristotle and Ptolemy.21 Against Giovanni Pico, Bellanti insists that
other planets and stars significantly influence the terrestrial world. Following
the arguments developed in the Disputationes, Bellanti compares the sizes of
various celestial bodies to their potential impacts. Bellanti wonders why the
author of the Disputationes ascribes a significant impact to the Moon and the
Sun if their sizes differ with respect to Saturn or Jupiter by hundreds of folds. It
should be noted here that Bellanti refrains from addressing the more complex
philosophical considerations Pico develops in the corresponding passages in

       tior sit. Sed re vera caeli non parum deficiunt ab angelica profectionem. Ideo exemplum

                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 200

186                                                  Chapter 10

the Disputationes, focusing instead on what he sees as the weakest point in
Pico’s doctrine available for an immediate critique.22
  The second point Bellanti investigates is strongly related to the first one.
As it has been pointed out, one of the main points of Giovanni Pico’s argu-
ment against astrology concerns the mutability of celestial influences when
they come into contact with matter. Thus, according to Pico and his followers,
the real impact of celestial bodies penetrating matter cannot not be clearly
explained. To refute this claim, Bellanti tries to reconcile the belief that heaven
is both a universal and a particular cause (rejected by Pico) and the need to ac-
knowledge that no element, including matter, could distort or modify the pow-
erful and divine impulses of the heavens.23 To give his claims some credibility,
he refers, again, to the works of Aristotle and specifically to the Meteorologica,
which he interprets through the lens of a person well familiar with the medi-
eval astrological tradition. Thus, Bellanti insists that the universal influence
is above all obstacles and cannot be reduced to anything accidental or par-
ticular. Heavenly bodies, which are at the heart of the universal order of the
world, are full of ‘friendly forces’. This term Bellanti borrows from Averroes,
which also reveals that Bellanti did not limit himself exclusively to Aristotle
and the Stagirite’s Greek commentators; his arguments lie in the tradition of
Aristotelianised astrology, broadly conceived by and comprising Aristotle’s in-
terpreters across the whole of the Mediterranean, and its subsequent medieval
religious adaptation.
   Bellanti tries to prove the compatibility of astrology and religion, referring
to Thomas Aquinas and John Duns Scotus.24 It is worth mentioning that he
does not find any contradictions between Scotism and Thomism regarding
this specific doctrine. In light of the philosophical debates between these
two schools in the fourteenth century, Bellanti’s conclusion seems rather con-
troversial. As we remember, in the 900 Conclusiones—along with Plato and
Aristotle, Averroes and Avicenna—Giovanni Pico della Mirandola intended to
reconcile these two prominent scholastic doctores. After the failed dispute in
Rome, Giovanni Pico focused exclusively on Plato and Aristotle, completely
omitting the two other pairs of thinkers. Bellanti undoubtedly knew about
Giovanni Pico’s attempt to reconcile the views of Thomas Aquinas and John
Duns Scotus; thus, the way he portrays Thomas Aquinas and Duns Scotus as
having been unanimous in acknowledging the significance of astrology for the
benefit of Christian religion might reflect Pico’s failed reconciliation project.

22    Ibid., p. 191.
23    Ibid., p. 195.
24    Ibid., p. 196.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 201

Lucio Bellanti and the Return to ‘Christian Astrology’             187

   Bellanti starts by exploring the theological arguments in favour of astrology
with a quotation from Thomas Aquinas. He admits that the heavens must be
considered the natural cause for people to reach God.25 According to him, if it
is so, a human being, ‘in accordance with his body’, corresponds to the celestial
spheres and is therefore subject to their influences on corresponding parts of
his or her body. God, in turn, governs the positions of these celestial bodies and
their movement through angels to promote His will in the terrestrial world.26
Actions produced by human beings are determined due to their correspond-
ence with the highest, celestial spheres.27 This allows Bellanti to come to the
conclusion that such a link between the two worlds, terrestrial and celestial, is
similar to the relationship between man and heaven, that is, between micro-
cosm and macrocosm.28 Thus, the idea of microcosm is extended to an astro-
logical level; this opens the door for a combination of the traditional medieval
vision of man, its theological aspects and further astrological interpretation.
   Similar arguments are present in Bellanti’s second major pro-astrological
treatise, the De astrologica veritate. Here, Bellanti first of all calls astrology
a science, which recognises the laws of natural effects and their celestial
causes.29 According to him, the main task of astrologers is to observe, using
mathematical methods, the depth of the divine will. He argues that the use of
mathematics allows astrologers to provide accurate and verified predictions
and to avoid potential problems and mistakes.30 In the De veritate astrologica,
the liaison of the celestial spheres and their terrestrial analogies is similarly
described with the use of an image of microcosm.31
  Lucio Bellanti’s response to the Disputationes is an interesting example of
the reception of medieval Christianised astrology. Although Bellanti tries not
to limit himself to medieval scholasticism and to draw upon a large range of
pro-astrological arguments, the basis of his response is deeply rooted in the

## Page 202

188                                                  Chapter 10

Aristotelian tradition and its medieval followers. After having proposed his
version of the creation of Pico’s Disputationes, Bellanti sought to refute one
after another all the main notions of his opponent and his followers, especially
Girolamo Savonarola. Finally, Bellanti concluded that astrology was not at var-
iance with theology and might become an important field of knowledge able
to confirm Christian teachings. In this respect, Bellanti remained loyal to the
medieval view of astrology.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 203

Chapter 11
Poet, Astrologer, Courtier: Giovanni Gioviano
Pontano versus Giovanni Pico della Mirandola


Unlike Lucio Bellanti, another opponent of Giovanni Pico della Mirandola,
Giovanni Gioviano Pontano needs no introduction.1 His contribution to hu-
manist culture was largely recognised during his lifetime. As the head and the
most famous member of Porticus Antonianus, also known as the Accademia
Pontaniana, he was responsible for both cultural enterprises and political de-
velopments at the court of the Neapolitan kings of the Aragonese dynasty.
Although Pontano was born in Umbria, he was never associated with the hu-
manist movement north of Naples. In 1447, Alfonso I of Naples appeared in
Tuscany to attack Florence in the papal cause. His favourite humanist, the
Sicilian Panormita (whose real name was Antonio Beccadelli), accompanied
him. When Pontano presented himself at Alfonso’s court, Panormita recog-
nised the young man’s promise and took him under his wing. They began a
close friendship, which lasted until Panormita’s death in 1471. Panormita cre-
ated the first Neapolitan academy, the Porticus Antonianus, which was later
renamed after Pontano.2 This underlines the status of Pontano at the court of
Neapolitan rulers; he was considered to be Panormita’s successor as the lead-
ing ‘court’ humanist. Pontano was also actively involved in the real political
life of Naples. In 1487, he was appointed first secretary to the king, which cor-
responded to today’s position of prime minister. Pontano’s duties at his new
post included diplomatic negotiations with Italian states and foreign policy, as
well as the administration of justice, finance and army. Pontano’s fame as an
astrologer also contributed to his promotion to the position of first secretary; it
was quite common at that time for the political decisions of Renaissance rulers
to be guided by astrological predictions.


1  On Pontano’s general biography, see first of all Carol Kidwell, Pontano. Poet & Prime Minister
   (London: Duckworth, 1991). On how Pontano and his circle constructed their literary iden-
    tity, see Matteo Soranzo, Poetry and Identity in Quattrocento Naples (Farnham; Burlington,
   VT: Ashgate, 2014). On Pontano’s moral and political philosophy conforming to his political
    career, see Matthias Roick, Pontano’s Virtues: Aristotelian Moral and Political Thought in the
   Renaissance (London; New York: Bloomsbury, 2017).
2  On the ‘Accademia Pontaniana’ as a humanist institution and intellectual hub, see Shulamit
   Furstenberg-Levi, The Accademia Pontaniana: A Model of a Humanist Network (Leiden;
   Boston: Brill, 2016).


© Koninklijke Brill NV, Leiden, 2021 | doi:10.1163/9789004442276_013   Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 204

190                                                  Chapter 11

  The list of Pontano’s writings is impressive. It consists of several treatises on
moral and political philosophy, literary writings and poems, and philosophical
dialogues. Astrology played a crucial role in Pontano’s intellectual interests.
He did not limit himself to interpreting astrology and its relationship to phi-
losophy but also served as a translator and commentator of numerous ancient
astrological texts, such as pseudo-Ptolemy’s Centiloquium, mentioned above.3
His interest in Ptolemy’s Tetrabiblos is also worth mentioning. Pontano’s as-
trological poems, the Urania and the De meteoris, which abound in mytho-
logical details, were clearly influenced by Aratus’ Phaenomena and Manilius’
Astronomica.4
  The latter text, as well as Firmicus Maternus’ Mathesis, attracted Pontano’s
particular attention and owed a great deal of their success in the Renaissance
to his work. They shaped the essence of Pontano’s astrological views to a con-
siderable extent; that is why some introduction to their destiny in the Middle
Ages and the Renaissance might be required. The Mathesis, written by Julius
Firmicus Maternus, a fourth-century AC Roman astrologer, was perhaps
amongst the most successful ancient astrological sources. As the Italian scholar
Michele Rinaldi suggests, the first evidence of the dissemination of the treatise
could be found in the eleventh and twelfth centuries in France and England.5
Thus, medieval English historian William of Malmesbury mentioned that
Gerbert of Aurillac, who would later become Pope under the name of Silvester
II, had studied the Mathesis of Firmicus.6 Though William of Malmesbury
is often referred to as one of the most accurate and trustworthy historians
in medieval England, his comments raise some questions. As is well known,
Gerbert of Aurillac had a controversial reputation and was accused of prac-
tising black magic. Such an image of the magician was not only widespread
in medieval literature; it remained in force until the twentieth century and is

3  On Pontano’s place amongst ‘astrological humanists’, see Michele Rinaldi, ‘L’astrologia degli
   umanisti’, in Il linguaggio dei cieli. Astri e simboli nel Rinascimento, pp. 73–89.
4  Mauro De Nichilo, I poemi astrologici di Giovanni Pontano: storia del testo (Bari: Dedalo,
    1975). Book I of the Urania was recently published in a good German edition: Giovanni
   Pontanos Urania Buch 1: Einleitung, Edition und Kommentar, ed. by Dennis Weh (Wiesbaden:
   Harrassowitz, 2017). There is hope that the other books of the poem will follow. On Pontano in
   a broader context of Renaissance Latin astrological poetry, see Yasmin Haskell, ‘Renaissance
   Latin Didactic Poetry on the Stars: Wonder, Myth, and Science’, Renaissance Studies, 12, 4
    (1998), 495–522.
5  Michele Rinaldi, ‘Sic itur ad astra’: Giovanni Pontano e la sua opera astrologica nel quadro
   della tradizione manoscritta della Mathesis di Giulio Firmico Materno (Naples: Loffredo,
   2002), pp. 31–37.
6  William of Malmesbury, Gesta Regum Anglorum, ed. by Roger A. B. Mynors et al. (Oxford:
   Oxford University Press, 1988), I, p. 280.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 205

Poet, Astrologer, Courtier                                               191

reflected in at least two literary masterpieces.7 One wonders whether William
of Malmesbury’s supposition about Gerbert’s astrological studies was caused
by any firm evidence or merely by the legendary status of the future Pope. Be
that as it may, in the mid-eleventh century, the Mathesis was known in both
England and France. References to Firmicus are found in the writings of the
English astronomer Daniel of Morley, whilst in France, several scholastic the-
ologians from the School of Chartres obviously knew and used the Mathesis.
However, the further diffusion of Islamic astrological treatises in the West
had a negative impact on the popularity of the Mathesis in the thirteenth and
fourteenth centuries. Only four manuscripts of Firmicus from the thirteenth
century and one manuscript from the fourteenth century are extant.8 Hence,
it appears that the Mathesis lost its reputation as an important astrological
‘manual’. On the other hand, it probably influenced Pietro d’Abano, whose as-
trological writings became a source for the iconographic program of frescoes
in the Palazzo della Ragione in Padua.9 What is more, Francesco Petrarca was
familiar with the Mathesis and referred to it in some of his texts.10 In 1429,
the famous humanist and tireless manuscript collector Poggio Bracciolini
found a manuscript of the Mathesis in the library of the monastery of Monte
Cassino. He reports this discovery in a letter to his friend Niccolò Niccoli.11
Unfortunately, the codex found by Poggio and containing the Mathesis was
incomplete; the part including Firmicus’ text is lost. The rest of the manu-
script contains a piece by Sextus Julius Frontinus entitled De aquaeductu, ap-
parently rewritten by Peter the Deacon from the Monte Cassino Abbey. This
led some scholars to suggest that Peter the Deacon might also be a copyist of
the Mathesis. An additional argument in favour of this supposition is that in

## Page 206

192                                                  Chapter 11

his autobiography, Peter the Deacon mentions a text on astronomy amongst
the list of books he had copied. Contemporary scholars, however, are scepti-
cal of this hypothesis.12 Though the destiny of the Monte Cassino manuscript
is uncertain, other manuscripts of the Mathesis are extant and were known
to a number of prominent Renaissance humanists and scientists, including
Guarino da Verona, Vittorino da Feltre, Regiomontanus and Panormita. Finally,
the text of the Mathesis became one of the main sources for the astrological
speculation of Pontano, who annotated a manuscript of Firmicus’ work he
kept in his personal library. This manuscript is now preserved in the National
Library of Naples (Ms. V A 17). Pontano refers to Firmicus Maternus in his sub-
sequent astrological works, including the Commentationes in Ptolemaeum and
the De rebus coelestibus.13 In 1499, Aldus Manutius finally printed the Mathesis,
together with other texts about the stars; the collection was widely known as
the Aldina.14 This publication consequently resulted in the growth of the pop-
ularity of the Mathesis amongst European intellectuals.
  The destiny of another important astrological text of antiquity, Manilius’
Astronomica, was different. Unknown in the Middle Ages, it was only discov-
ered in 1416, in the library of the monastery of St. Gallen in Switzerland, by the
same Poggio Bracciolini. The Astronomica became one of the best-known as-
trology textbooks in the Renaissance; for instance, it inspired the iconographic
program of the cycle of frescoes in Ferrara’s Palazzo Schifanoia.15 The dissemi-
nation of the Astronomica, specifically after the invention of the printing press,
contributed to the rise of astrological literature in various humanist and intel-
lectual circles in Italy. The Italian poet and astrologer Lorenzo Bonincontri from
San Miniato played an important role in popularising Manilius and his poem.
Though Benedetto Soldati qualifies Bonincontri as ‘a humanist of the second
level’,16 Bonincontri was an acclaimed author who was on friendly terms with
many prominent thinkers of his time, including Pontano, Ficino and Giovanni
Pico della Mirandola. Whilst in Naples, he was involved in numerous cul-
tural endeavours sponsored by the court of Neapolitan kings. After moving to
Florence, Bonincontri, already recognised as an expert in ancient poetry, litera-
ture and philosophy, met Marsilio Ficino and Angelo Poliziano.17 In the second

12    Paul Meyvaert, ‘The Autographs of Peter the Deacon’, Bulletin of the John Rylands Library,
      38 (1955), 114–38 (125); Rinaldi, ‘Sic itur ad astra’, pp. 60–62.

## Page 207

Poet, Astrologer, Courtier                                          193

half of the 1470s, Bonincontri taught the Astronomica at the Studio Fiorentino
and left a commentary on Manilius’ astrological masterpiece.18 It is worth not-
ing that, apart from the Astronomica, the other source from which Bonincontri
extensively borrowed and which he thoroughly studied was Firmicus Maternus’
Mathesis. It implies that in reconstructing an ancient version of astrology, he
aimed to take into consideration as many authentic ancient writings as possi-
ble. It is also essential that, although astrology was integrated into the univer-
sity curriculum beginning in the Middle Ages, Bonincontri brought it to the
next level; through his successful course on the Astronomica, ancient astrology
appeared to be a part of humanist teaching practices. Thereafter, Bonincontri
wrote his masterpiece De rebus coelestibus, an astrological poem that stylis-
tically and linguistically follows the ancient prototypes.19 Several years later,
Bonincontri’s friend and colleague Pontano would write a text with the same
title, though this time in prose.
  Given the fascination for astrology, and its classicising elements in particu-
lar, that Pontano expressed throughout his long and prolific career, it comes as
no surprise that Pontano went to the effort of responding to Giovanni Pico’s
anti-astrological attack. Besides, Pico had sharply criticised Pontano’s Latin
translation of pseudo-Ptolemy’s Centiloquium, something the ‘astrological
humanist’ Pontano should have taken rather personally. However, Giovanni
Pontano did not devote a specific treatise to opposing Pico’s anti-astrological
polemics. As Benedetto Soldati fairly pointed out, in its entirety, Pontano’s
legacy represents an alternative to what interested Giovanni Pico and his
Florentine entourage. Pontano stayed aloof from the Platonic, Hermetic and
Kabbalistic sources that dominated the intellectual landscape of the Medicean
court in the late fifteenth century.20 Similarly, holding a different political po-
sition, he resisted the calls for a religious and social change that prevailed
in the north of Italy on the eve of the Italian wars and were exemplified in
Savonarola’s teaching. Therefore, the aim of this chapter is to examine and
contextualise Pontano’s response to the Disputationes adversus astrologiam
divinatricem in two of his major pro-astrological writings: the De rebus coeles-
tibus and the De fortuna. I argue that Pontano attempted to reconcile newly
rediscovered ancient astrological texts and ideas with medieval scholasticism
and thus to restore the Christianised tradition of astrology. In this respect, both


18   Arthur Field, ‘Lorenzo Bonincontri and the First Public Lectures on Manilius (Florence,
       ca. 1475–1478)’, Rinascimento, 36 (1996), 207–25.

       logica nel Quattrocento, pp. 154–98.
20    Ibid., p. 231.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 208

194                                                  Chapter 11

Pontano and Bellanti sought to pursue the same goal, but with the use of dif-
ferent sources.
  Pontano started working on the De rebus coelestibus in the 1470s.21 This pe-
riod was marked for him by a number of other astrological projects. It appears
that the De rebus coelestibus was designed to become a systematic presenta-
tion of astrology in prose, whilst Pontano was simultaneously composing a
corresponding astrological poem, the Urania. After the Disputationes adversus
astrologiam divinatricem spread across Italy, Pontano added a specific chapter
to the main body of the De rebus coelestibus devoted to refuting his opponent’s
position concerning astrology.22 It seems that Pontano read the Disputationes
in a manuscript; he finalised his response to Pico’s anti-astrological arguments
some time in 1495, that is, a year prior to the Benedictus Hectoris publication
of Giovanni Pico’s Opera omnia. The twelfth book of the De rebus coelestibus, in
which Pontano refutes the Disputationes, is not long, especially in comparison
to other parts of this enormous treatise, nor does it straightforwardly inves-
tigate his opponent’s flaws. It mostly deals with Pico’s natural philosophical
arguments against astrology; all other issues, including practical astrology and
astrological techniques, are left out. No particular attention is paid to Pico’s re-
consideration of the prisca theologia concept, although it should be noted that
Pontano had never favoured the prisca theologia in its Ficinian version. This
strategy of responding specifically to Pico’s philosophical position on astrology
conforms with the rest of the treatise; by rejecting the essential argument of
Book III of the Disputationes, Pontano avoids the need to go through other
aspects of Pico’s attack and concentrates instead on justifying astrological
knowledge in the main body of the De rebus coelestibus. Thus, he supposedly
refrains from reacting to all Pico’s claims. However, the way this response is
structured and fulfilled leaves much to be desired. As is shown below, Pontano
did bear responsibility for this failure, albeit only in part.
  At the beginning of this chapter, Pontano addresses Paolo Cortesi, a friend
of Giovanni Pico and Marsilio Ficino, who was in charge of Pico’s acquittal
after the failed Roman dispute of 1486. Cortesi saw Pico as a humanist model
for his handbook on cardinals and acknowledges him as ‘the most learned
of the Latins’ and a ‘source of knowledge’.23 The close relationship between
Pico and Paolo Cortesi might be the reason why Pontano remained cautious in


21    I refer to this edition: Ioannis Ioviani Pontani de rebus coelestibus libri XIIII (Basle: apud
     Andream Cratandrum, 1530).
22    Ibid., pp. 337–46.

Poet, Astrologer, Courtier                                          195

commemorating Pico’s life and achievements; he praises Pico as an extremely
gifted scholar, but states that he will, nonetheless, oppose some of his views,
even if Pico is already dead.24 He compares Pico with other prominent think-
ers of the past, including Priscian and Lorenzo Valla. For them, Pontano says,
it was common to criticise those with whom they shared the intellectual ter-
ritory. In this context, he also recalls the debates between the Platonic and
Aristotelian philosophical schools. Pontano’s point is that the controversies on
the status of astrology should not be set aside after Pico’s death, and they de-
serve further discussion.25 However, the character of the short chapter does
not correspond to such an ambitious intention. Its anti-Piconian motivations
also remain unclear.
   Pontano’s vigilant remarks about Giovanni Pico and his Disputationes, of-
ficially presented in the late, published version of the De rebus coelestibus,
contradict the autograph copy of the treatise.26 The full text of the De rebus
coelestibus, together with a number of Pontano’s other writings, was published
posthumously in 1512. It seems that the editor Pietro Summonte significantly
modified several controversial passages of the treatise. In the autograph, there
are clear interpolations, which suggest that Summonte added Paolo Cortesi
as the addressee of the twelfth book of the De rebus coelestibus. Moreover,
Summonte simply deleted a large section in which Pontano accused Pico of
heresy in his early writings, as well as of following Savonarola at the late stage
of his career. Summonte did a similar thing with regard to another of Pontano’s
astrological treatises, the De fortuna.27
  The question of what constituted the notions of fate, fortune and necessity
was central for fifteenth- and sixteenth-century Renaissance thought. After

## Page 210

196                                                  Chapter 11

playing a strong role in medieval theology, as well as in The Consolation of
Philosophy and other philosophical treatises, these concepts received a sec-
ond life in the Renaissance. There were several reasons for this: first, there was
a renewed interest in Cicero’s writings; second, the Renaissance witnessed a
revival of atomism; finally, Renaissance scholars obtained access to a large
corpus of Stoic literature and became better familiarised with anti-Stoic ac-
counts, including Alexander of Aphrodisias’ attack on the causal chain and
determinism. Both Petrarch and Coluccio Salutati admired Stoic philosophy
and tried to Christianise many of its aspects, including the fortuna concept.28
The attempts to combine Stoic elements with the Christian religion led to a
new understanding of fortuna in fifteenth-century Italian moral and political
thought.29 This reading of fortuna combines the notion of virtus in its moral
dimension with the Christian teaching on divine providence and free will,
which goes back to Augustine. The acceptance of any effect of fortune, either
positive or negative, seems to be fundamental and represents a human being’s
humility before God, whose omnipotence cannot be called into question. For
those who manifest moral virtutes, fortune as an act of God’s will is more be-
nevolent. Whilst Salutati touches upon the fortune concept in its relation with
astrological predestination (and firmly rejects the latter), Poggio Bracciolini
examines its political application, claiming that virtus moralis has a clear cor-
relation with virtus politicus. This interpretation was widely accepted in Italian

      troduced the term ‘civic humanism’ is still a relevant reading: The Crisis of the Early Italian

## Page 211

Poet, Astrologer, Courtier                                           197

Renaissance political thought.30 Despite some opposition, Christian Stoicism
crossed the Alps to culminate in Justus Lipsius’ De Constantia. It comes as no
surprise that the issue of fate went far beyond humanist discussions and is re-
flected in sixteenth-century religious controversies, which eventually shaped
the intellectual map of Europe for many centuries. Thus, a reconsidered ver-
sion of Augustine’s interpretation of the concepts of divine providence and
free will formed the basis for the Calvinist theological teaching.
   This was the intellectual context in which Pontano operated in crafting his
understanding of fate in its relation to astrology. What is crucial to stress is that
he contrasted this view with theological and philosophical novelties that, in
his opinion, might contravene the integrity of classicised Christianity. Despite
the fact that Marsilio Ficino’s ideas and writings circulated in fifteenth-century
Naples, Platonic philosophy in its Ficinian interpretation remained on the
margins of Neapolitan intellectual interests.31 Pontano was largely responsi-
ble for this. It seems that what he was trying to promote during his lifetime,
and what laid the groundwork for his philosophical and literary projects, was
centred on restoring a set of alternative sources to be incorporated into the tra-
ditional Aristotelian/Ptolemaic intellectual framework. The De fortuna clearly
reveals this strategy. Combining the astrological tradition of interpreting for-
tune and fate with Aristotelian philosophy, Pontano uses Thomas Aquinas and
John Duns Scotus to confirm an anti-deterministic foundation of astrology
and fortuna and justify it through theology. There is no clear evidence whether
Pontano’s reconciliation of Aquinas with Duns Scotus in exploring the fortune
concept was intended as a response to the early Piconian theological project
initiated in the 900 Conclusiones. In any case, the fact of Pontano’s intense
use of John Duns Scotus, noticed by Francesco Tateo, does not seem to be
accidental.32 However, Pontano’s overall idea to restore an ancient notion of
fortuna and reinterpret it in a Christian theological context faced severe criti-
cism on the part of Egidio da Viterbo, who was involved in numerous debates
with his rival humanist and poet on different topics.33

       in detail in James Hankins’ recent magnum opus: Virtue Politics: Soulcraft and Statecraft in
      Renaissance Italy (Cambridge, MA; London: Harvard University Press, 2019).

     Accademia Pontaniana, pp. 107–21.

## Page 212

198                                                  Chapter 11

  Pontano’s De fortuna was not only a philosophical but a political text. As one
of the leading late fifteenth-century political theorists and the author of the
De principe, in the De fortuna, Pontano provides a political dimension to the dis-
cussion of fortuna, which becomes evident from the dedicatory letter. Its des-
tiny is controversial; the first draft is addressed to Antonio Guevara, the Count
of Potenza, who was close to Pontano’s patron, Alfonso the Magnanimous.
However, a radical change in the political situation in Italy, as well as the be-
ginning of the Italian wars, forced Pontano to look at other possible addressees
in search of a new, trustworthy and influential patron. Consalvo of Cordoba
was his final choice.34 Appointed a commander of Spanish troops in southern
Italy by Ferdinand of Aragon and his wife, Isabella of Castile, he became the ef-
fective governor of Naples. Thus, Pontano’s intention to secure Consalvo’s pa-
tronage is central to the text. As Jerry Bentley has pointed out, after Ferrante’s
death and Alfonso II’s abdication in 1494, Pontano briefly served as secretary to
Ferrandino and tried to prevent the French invasion.35 After the French troops
entered Naples, Pontano’s regular political service was ended. He delivered the
keys to the city to Charles VIII and sought guarantees that the French troops
would not demolish the city. Francesco Guicciardini indicated that Pontano
had welcomed Charles VIII and even pronounced an oration proclaiming the
French king. Although there is no firm evidence of such an episode, by the
time the Spanish crown regained Naples, Pontano’s reputation was spoiled.
Through the dedicatory letter to Consalvo, Pontano, already an old man in his
sixties, intended to protect himself from any possible persecution and, in addi-
tion, to confirm his loyalty to the Spanish reigning couple. This explains a num-
ber of passages flattering his patrons in the introduction and the main body of
the treatise, in which Pontano explores the manifestations of fortune in the
deeds of ancient political leaders. Referring to the ancient examples, he clearly
designated them for his contemporaries. The philosophical arguments, with
numerous quotations from Cicero, Aristotle and scholastic doctors, had to jus-
tify Spanish political success. In this case, Summonte’s decision to cut out the
passages on Giovanni Pico seems reasonable; the posthumous publication of
the De fortuna had to restore Pontano’s political and philosophical authority in
his contemporaries’ view, and an attack against one of the leading Renaissance
philosophers might not have been considered the best means for that.
  Thus, the De fortuna represents a mixture of political and philosophical
arguments used to explain the Spanish crown’s success in Southern Italy.

34   Pontano, La fortuna, pp. 76–80.
35    Jerry H. Bentley,  Politics and Culture in Renaissance Naples (Princeton: Princeton
      University Press, 1987), p. 130.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 213

Poet, Astrologer, Courtier                                         199

Remaining completely loyal to the Stoic-Christian reading of fortuna, Pontano,
on the other hand, enriched it with an astrological element. Having his Spanish
patrons in mind as addressees, he claims that fortune is favourably disposed
towards impeccable and good rulers. For Pontano, there is no conflict between
fortune and free will; a human being who has intellect is free and able to act
in accordance with his personal intentions. God’s will determines whether a
person receives a positive or negative influence; if good fortune accidentally
goes to a scoundrel, it does not contradict the concept of divine omnipotence
and omniscience because, Pontano says, everything is in God’s hands.36 He
adds, though, that in most cases, fortune comes to those who earn its favour by
their moral and ethical excellence. To prove this notion, Pontano mentions a
number of political leaders who went from the very bottom to the top.37
  From a philosophical and theological perspective, Pontano accepts the Stoic
concept of the causal chain, supplementing it with standard Augustinian argu-
ments on free will and divine providence.38 To support the notion of necessary
causes, which were established according to God’s order and thus determine
particular terrestrial effects, Pontano generally refers to ancient philosophers
and some medieval scholastics. However, I argue that all the references to
ancient philosophers or poets are nothing but a mere tribute to the tradition
Pontano admired for many years. In general, his arguments about the fortuna
concept follow a very classical Ciceronian—that is, Roman Stoic—tradition;
other sources intended to impress Pontano’s readers with their variety and
richness add almost nothing substantial to this framework. Explaining the
compatibility of fortuna with astrology, Pontano goes a step forward in com-
parison with Salutati, Poggio and other scholars of the first half of the fifteenth
century. Thus, with reference to the medieval scholastic tradition, mainly to
John Duns Scotus, he introduces the medieval notion of impetus (‘impulse’).
Although the impetus theory is originally linked with Aristotelian physics and
dynamics,39 Pontano gives it a theological explanation. Combining it with a
number of ideas taken from Thomas Aquinas, he claims that the influence of
celestial spheres, with the help of impetus, determines natural effects. In this
context, Pontano supposes that fortune pertains to the celestial spheres from
which, through astrological influences, it disperses over the terrestrial world,


36   Pontano, La fortuna, pp. 88–90, 94.
37    Ibid., pp. 96–98.
38    Ibid., pp. 300–2.
39   For some general information on the concept, see Jürgen Sarnowsky, ‘Concepts of Impetus
     and the History of Mechanics’, in Mechanics and Natural Philosophy before the Scientific
       Revolution, ed. by Walter R. Laird and Sophie Roux (Boston: Springer, 2007), pp. 121–45.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 214

200                                                  Chapter 11

and along with a natural impulse, provokes natural effects.40 It is surprising
that despite Pontano’s apparent fascination for the ancient heritage and his
ambitious idea to restore ‘real’ antiquity, he took most of his arguments from
two main sources: from the classical concept of fatum, developed in Cicero’s
writings and already well known by Pontano’s time, and from medieval
scholasticism.
  Even a cursory analysis reveals that Pontano’s views of astrology and fortuna
fundamentally contradict those of Giovanni Pico della Mirandola, which ex-
plains the reasons behind Pontano’s wish to respond to the Disputationes.
A passage preserved in two manuscripts identified by Francesco Tateo (Vat.
Lat. 2841 and Marc. Lat. VI, 233) sheds more light on how Pontano originally
planned on opposing the Disputationes. By that time, he was already famil-
iar with the work of Lucio Bellanti, ‘a man well known for his extraordinary
acuteness in all sciences’, and his Responsiones.41 In fact, Pontano explicitly
states in this passage from the De fortuna manuscripts that he will not address
Pico’s arguments against astrology because Bellanti had already explored the
matter in detail. Instead, he will focus on Pico’s motivation to reject astrology.
Although Pontano acknowledges Pico’s contribution to contemporary philos-
ophy and calls him ‘a man of great fame, talent and knowledge’, he admits that
Pico fell under the influence of three major authorities at the final stage of
his career: Pyrrho, Lorenzo Valla and Girolamo Savonarola.42 The presence of
Lorenzo Valla, whose writings do not seem to have been amongst Pico’s favour-
ites, is unclear. In the meantime, certain similarities in the ways Pico and Valla
treated and deconstructed the traditions of astrology and theology, respec-
tively, are evident. Pyrrho’s name in this list indicates that Pico’s contemporar-
ies might have been aware of his interest in scepticism. Pontano’s mention that
Pyrrho’s writings influenced Pico’s decision to attack astrology could be con-
sidered to be an additional proof of Pico’s close reading of Sextus Empiricus.
Moreover, the passage in question supports the assumption that for Pico’s

## Page 215

Poet, Astrologer, Courtier                                         201

contemporaries, Sextus was one of the sources that caused the transformation
of his philosophical orientation. According to Pontano, the other source was
Savonarola. Pontano blames Pico and Savonarola for their intention to intro-
duce and propagate certain novelties to the Christian religion; in the case of
Pico, he adds, this led to an accusation of heresy, which did not end in execu-
tion exclusively thanks to the clemency of Pope Innocent VIII.43 Savonarola is
pictured as pure evil, a liar who tried to be glorified as a saint; Pontano even
claims that the senate and the Florentine people went as far as to issue a spe-
cial decree on the glorification of Savonarola.44 This ‘liar’ persuaded Giovanni
Pico to compose the Disputationes adversus astrologiam.45 Another, more per-
sonal, reason was that a certain astrologer had predicted that Giovanni Pico’s
life would be short.46 Here, Pontano clearly refers to a well-known episode
from Lucio Bellanti’s biography, however, without naming the author of Pico’s
horoscope. Pontano concludes that, along with his philosophical and theolog-
ical motivation, Pico attacked astrology because he hoped to convince him-
self of the prediction’s falsity and thus flee from his own destiny. For Pontano,
Pico’s sudden and early death proves that the mathematician’s calculations
and predictions were correct.
  The same accusations against Pico are present in the passage from the
De rebus coelestibus that Pietro Summonte likewise cut out. Taking the failed
Roman dispute as a starting point of his polemics with Pico, Pontano insists
that the grounds for Pico’s attack on astrology lie in his heretical theses. He
recalls how his opponent intended to propose a series of theses on every
discipline, including theology; reformulating Christian dogmas led Pico to a

## Page 216

202                                                  Chapter 11

number of philosophical and theological errors. His conclusions were prohib-
ited by Pope Innocent VIII because they ‘had almost nothing in common with
Christian religion’.47 For Pontano, Pico rejected astrology, a discipline useful
to the Christian religion and helpful in various fields, such as medicine or ag-
riculture, because he remained loyal to his heretical beliefs over the last years
of his life. Pontano further argues that Pico’s acquaintance and friendship with
Savonarola reinforced the heterodox views considerably. The Neapolitan hu-
manist recognises Pico’s exceptional talent in philosophy. However, it does not
prevent him from criticising the Disputationes, even though its author could
not have had a chance to respond to these remarks.
   In the passages not modified by his editor Summonte, Pontano develops his
pro-astrological arguments by claiming that there is no need to refute astrol-
ogy because it can be of use to common people. He reiterates that astrology
can be applied to many disciplines, and he insists that no great philosopher
could ever deny its positive and helpful effects.48 To prove that particular
events can be correctly predicted, Pontano refers to the passage from Ptolemy’s
Tetrabiblos that Pico uses in the Disputationes to disprove the same idea.49
Pontano agrees that whilst preparing the horoscopes, it is hard to take into
account every little detail and, therefore, astrologers usually face numerous
issues in their work. But a good and professional astrologer can avoid these
inaccuracies if they are aware not only of the positions of the spheres in any
particular period of time but also of their potential effects, the nature and
qualities of things upon which the stars produce their influence. The astrolo-
ger has two approaches to acquire this knowledge: either through experience
or through inspiration and fortune.
  Regarding the first approach, Pontano points to a number of practical as-
pects, saying that astrologers of previous generations have already carefully
investigated the effects of astrological influences.50 Medicine and agricul-
ture serve as the most definitive examples of how an accurate calculation

## Page 217

Poet, Astrologer, Courtier                                     203

could prevent a bad harvest or heal a patient. Pontano concludes by calling
astrology/astronomy an exact science based on approved calculations and ex-
perience. He also has no objections to the presence of astrologers in a city or
within the court of a ruler. On the contrary, in Pontano’s opinion, an astrolo-
ger’s expertise is helpful in governing the state; they can give advice on a fa-
vourable date to begin a war campaign, construct a ship or building or propose
new legislation. It is clear that in claiming all of this, Pontano refers to his own
experience as a high-ranking courtier.
  Pontano examines the second approach, through  inspiration, in  sev-
eral treatises. In particular, he reveals his position in the commentary on
Ptolemy’s Centiloquium.51 Whilst commenting on the first conclusion of the
Centiloquium,52 on the possibility to predict the future, Pontano states that as-
trology has the same value as other important and recognised disciplines like
medicine and philosophy. Pontano distinguishes two approaches to astrology.
The first, which deals with demonic powers, he calls insane and mad.53 The
second, however, in accordance with Ptolemy’s notion, operates by means of
mathematical calculations. It belongs to a long tradition of exploring celestial
configurations that dates back to antiquity. Pontano adds that in the Roman
period, not exclusively mathematicians but also poets were responsible for
providing predictions. Hence, he concludes that the prediction of future
events is open only to those who acquire divine inspiration. In this regard, po-
etic art, which is hardly possible or imaginable without divine influence, and

## Page 218

204                                                  Chapter 11

astrology become closely and fundamentally related.54 In the De rebus coelesti-
bus, Pontano develops his argument by saying that poetry is a substantial part
of theology, moral philosophy and astrology, and a poet must investigate the
positions of the stars in order to succeed in his art.55 As Matteo Soranzo has
perfectly shown, Pontano’s notion of ‘divine frenzy’, generally based on ancient
poetry, might be regarded as an elaborate response to Ficino’s Neoplatonised
doctrine on the same subject.56 Alongside the controversy on the legitimacy
of astrology, this case summarises the points about which Pontano opposed
Ficino, Pico and their Florentine milieu as a whole. Intentionally drawing upon
a completely different set of sources and ideas, he advanced another standard
of ancient knowledge and tradition. Thus, as we have previously established,
throughout the whole corpus of his writings, Pontano pretends to ignore—or
at least resists mentioning—all the knowledge that had been revived in con-
temporary Florence. When mentioning Plato and the Platonists, he usually
turns to the texts that had been known since the Middle Ages, for instance, to
Calcidius’ commentary on the Timaeus. Another taboo for Pontano was refer-
encing the Islamic astrological tradition. What Pontano proposed—instead of
the Florentine passion for Platonism, Savonarola’s religious reform and Pico’s
anti-astrological radicalism—was the idea to which the Neapolitan scholar
devoted many years of his work. It was centred on a meticulous humanist res-
toration of an ancient heritage, a virtual return ad fontes of Roman and Greek
origin and its combination with Christian teaching in its traditional, mostly
scholastic form. Pontano’s response to the Disputationes in the De fortuna and
the De rebus coelestibus is very much in line with these central premises of his
thought.

       interpres sit, multo etiam magis quam in poeta necesse est, uti natura ipsa vires suas ex-
       erceat, quando medicum quoque et imperatorem asseverent fortunatum esse oportere.’

## Page 219

Chapter 12
Astrology in Francesco Zorzi’s De harmonia mundi:
A Response to Giovanni Pico della Mirandola’s
Disputationes adversus astrologiam?

Francesco Zorzi (also known as Francesco Giorgio Veneto) is recognised as
a leading thinker in early sixteenth-century Venice. Although he composed
only three treatises, one of which remained in manuscript form and was never
published during its author’s lifetime,1 whereas the two other texts were sub-
jected to posthumous censorship,2 Zorzi’s oeuvre was at the forefront of con-
temporary intellectual trends.3 His interests in biblical humanism, along with
Neoplatonic and Kabbalistic philosophy, resulted in two major writings: the
De harmonia mundi (1525) and the In Scripturam Sacram Problemata (1536),
both of enormous length.4 Whilst in the latter Zorzi aims at interpreting the
Scriptures through the lens of a Hebraist scholar, the former provides a me-
ticulous interpretation of a harmonised world profoundly influenced by
Neoplatonic and Kabbalistic teachings. This chapter will focus on Zorzi’s views
of astrology, the principles of which he considers to be an essential part of the

   Maillard (Milan: Arché, 1991).

   Olschki, 2001), pp. 55–78; Leen Spruit, ‘Francesco Giorgi on the Harmony of Creation and
   the Catholic Censorship of His Views’, in Sing Aloud Harmonious Spheres. Renaissance
   Conceptions of Cosmic Harmony, ed. by Jacomien Prins and Maude Vanhaelen (New York;
   London: Routledge, 2018), pp. 123–59 (130–33).
3  Cesare Vasoli, ‘Marsilio Ficino e Francesco Giorgio Veneto,’ in Marsilio Ficino e il ritorno di
   Platone. Studi e documenti, vol. 2, pp. 533–54; idem, ‘La tradizione cabbalistica e l’esperienza
    religiosa del Rinascimento’, Italia, 9 (1994), 11–35; idem, ‘L’ermetismo a Venezia. Da Francesco
   Giorgio Veneto ad Agostino Steuco’, in L’Ermetismo nell’Antichità e nel Rinascimento (Milan:
   Nuovi Orizzonti, 1998), pp. 127–62 (later reprinted as: ‘Hermetism in Venice. From Francesco
   Giorgio Veneto to Agostino Steuco’, in Magic, Alchemy and Science, 15th–18th Centuries,
   2 vols, ed. by Carlos Gilly and Cis van Heertum (Florence: Centro Di, 2002), I, pp. 50–67);
   Schmidt-Biggemann, Philosophia perennis. Historical Outlines of Western Spirituality in
   Ancient, Medieval and Early Modern Thought, pp. 305–15.

   was reprinted several times in the sixteenth and seventeenth centuries. For the modern edi-
   tion of the De Harmonia mundi, see Francesco Zorzi, L’armonia del mondo, ed. by Saverio
   Campanini (Milan: Bompiani, 2010).


© Koninklijke Brill NV, Leiden, 2021 | doi:10.1163/9789004442276_014   Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 220

206                                                 Chapter 12

world’s harmony. At the same time, as we shall see, the astrological discourse
in the De harmonia mundi is a response to the Disputationes adversus astrolo-
giam. Whilst Zorzi allegedly doubted the authenticity of the Disputationes, by
inverting Giovanni Pico’s arguments against Pico himself, in the meantime, he
sought to restore the concept of ‘Christian astrology’.
  As we have already seen in the previous chapters, all major late fifteenth-
and early sixteenth-century opponents of Pico’s attack against astrology, such
as Giovanni Gioviano Pontano, Lucio Bellanti and Gabriele Pirovano, unan-
imously claimed that the Disputationes, the authenticity of which some of
them openly doubted, had been a product of malicious Savonarolan influence.
Pico’s radical shift from being a ‘Christian Kabbalist’ to a severe critic of as-
trology opened the door for the debates regarding the authorship of the trea-
tise and, consequently, caused opposition to the Disputationes, including from
those who had previously praised Pico. One such author was Francesco Zorzi.
  As Chaim Wirszubski has shown, Zorzi had studied and commented on
Pico’s main Kabbalistic writing, the 900 Conclusiones, whilst later the most fa-
mous Italian Renaissance interpreter of Pico’s Kabbalistic theses, Arcangelo
da Borgonovo, plagiarised a significant portion of Zorzi’s work.5 As a part
of the textually confirmed reliance on Pico’s legacy, in his writings, mainly in
the De harmonia mundi, Zorzi clearly adopts the prisca theologia doctrine.
This, in turn, caused most of the studies on Zorzi either to shed light on his
use of Jewish, mostly Kabbalistic, sources or to focus particularly on the ap-
propriation of the prisca theologia in his works.6 At the same time, Zorzi’s
astrological views have never been studied, even though in his philosophical
and theological system, astrology is seen as an essential element that unifies
heaven and the terrestrial world and thus contributes to universal harmony.
To understand the place of astrology in Zorzi’s system and why it essentially
contradicts what Pico claims in the Disputationes, it is worth re-evaluating the
structure of the universe as described in the De harmonia mundi.
  The whole treatise is divided into three cantica, each consisting of eight
tones in accordance with harmonic rules and thus corresponding to the idea
of octoechos, which dates back to the early medieval Gregorian chant and has



5  Chaim Wirszubski, ‘Francesco Giorgio’s Commentary on Giovanni Pico’s Kabbalistic Theses,’
   Journal of the Warburg and Courtauld Institutes, 37 (1974), 145–56. On some other examples of
   the reception of Pico’s Kabbalistic theses in the sixteenth century, see Saverio Campanini, ‘Il
  commento alle Conclusiones cabalisticae nel Cinquecento,’ in Giovanni Pico e la cabbalà, ed.
   by Fabrizio Lelli (Florence: Olschki, 2014), pp. 167–230.
6  See n. 3 in this chapter above.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 221

Astrology in Francesco Zorzi’s De harmonia mundi               207

its remote source in the Psalms.7 The structure symbolically coincides with the
three worlds represented by the cantica: the first one is immovable, pure and
indefinite; the second one deals with the Logos and its manifestations; whilst
the third is the world of man. Zorzi explains the significance of the octave,
calling it the highest part of the diatonic system. In the first canticum, whilst
describing the laws and musical proportions of the universe, Zorzi works
through the musical structure of the diapason. Claiming that every numerical
system has its beginning in the simplest of all forms, that is, in the number
one, Zorzi clearly follows Plato and differentiates between two progressions.
The first one contains two, four and eight, the number that corresponds to the
first large group of tones, that is, to the octave. The second progression consists
of the cubes with the number three, that is, of three, nine and twenty-seven.
Asserting that the number twenty-seven contains all the multitude of things
in se, Zorzi divides the whole diapason into twenty-seven tones. He goes on
to explain the sacred meaning of the twenty-seven tones and aligns it with
the Kabbalistic notion of the Metatron or Princeps facierum, which in the
Christian Kabbalah is considered God. Zorzi claims that the Metatron has at
its core the number nine, consisting of the three nines, and by this forms the
number twenty-seven. From this Kabbalistically interpreted correspondence
with the number that he associates with God, Zorzi infers the harmony of the
whole universe.
   After reaching the ultimate tone, one stands in a contemplative admiration,
observing the beauty of the universe. The resulting visions are enumerated
and form twenty revelations, from the lowest one (matter) to the highest one
(total silence). Zorzi labelled these revelations as ‘motets’ (modulus, motelus),
referring to an honourable musical genre, which in the Renaissance was often
considered a sacred madrigal and sometimes incorporated into the liturgy.
This leads to a conclusion that in the De harmonia mundi, the idea of motets,
probably inspired by actual mystical visions of the Franciscan Clarist Chiara
Bugni, is associated with a prayerful standing in front of God.8 The image of
the Celestial Jerusalem crowns the plenitude of revelation, thus making the
whole treatise represent the degrees of spiritual ascension, through which the
reader reaches the highest sphere of contemplation.

7  On the structure of the treatise and Zorzi’s musical universe: Jean-François Maillard, ‘Le De
   harmonia mundi de Georges de Venise. Aperçus sur la genèse et la structure de l’œuvre’, Revue
   de l’histoire des religions, 180 (1971), 181–203; Cesare Vasoli, ‘Il tema musicale e architettonico
   della «Harmonia mundi» da Francesco Giorgio Veneto all’Accademia degli Uranici e Gioseffo
    Zarlino’, Musica e Storia, 6 (1998), 193–210.
8  Cesare Vasoli, Filosofia e religione nella cultura del Rinascimento (Naples: Guida, 1988),
   pp. 257–77.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 222

208                                                 Chapter 12

   It is evident that the structure of the De harmonia mundi was influenced
not only by a reading of Pseudo-Dionysius’ Mystical Theology and Jewish
Kabbalistic sources but also by Pico’s Heptaplus, the forty-nine chapters of
which correspond to the forty-nine levels of knowledge revealed to Moses
on Mount Sinai.9 However, in contrast to what Pico does in the Heptaplus,
Zorzi does not limit himself to the description of how the soul ascends to the
heavens; instead, he reconstructs the macrocosm/microcosm correlation be-
tween the mundane and celestial worlds. He insists that the union of the ma-
terial Jerusalem with its spiritual prototype is possible through the progressive
enrichment of the lower world with spirituality obtained through learning,
spiritual practice and contemplation. The rediscovery of the perfect harmony,
planned by the Creator in the beginning but subsequently lost, is the only way
to reach the Celestial Jerusalem. In this context, astrology is considered not as
a purely predictive, judicial tool but as one of the numerological and occult
principles upon which the universe is based. It has a significant role in unifying
the two worlds; Zorzi states that even the number of eight celestial spheres,
which, in his opinion, harmoniously correlates with the octave, clearly illus-
trates that at its heart, astrology is a fundamental element of philosophical and
theological discourses.10 At the same time, in order to restore the concept of
‘Christian astrology’ as a suitable ‘handmaid’ to theology and widely accepted
in the Latin West since the Middle Ages, Zorzi first rejects the attack on astrol-
ogy expressed in the Disputationes and the works of Pico’s followers, mainly
Savonarola and Gianfrancesco Pico.
  Throughout the treatise, neither Giovanni Pico nor Savonarola is men-
tioned, but Chapter XI of the third tone of Canticum I reveals with whom
Zorzi had actually polemicised. Thus, he states that ‘some’ thinkers sought to
denounce the idea that the stars produced any influence upon the terrestrial
world. These thinkers claimed that no great philosopher had ever supported
the idea of astrological predictions and that astrological beliefs were absurd
from a philosophical point of view. To prove that medicine, mathematics and
other disciplines have nothing to do with astrology, they refer to ‘a member of
the Academy Favorinus, a Stoic philosopher Panaetius, Carneades, Posidonius,
Timaeus, Pythagoras, Aristotle, Plato, Plotinus, Avicenna and Averroes’, none



9    See n. 23 in Chapter 4 above.
10   The harmony of celestial spheres and its correspondence with the diapason, on the one
      hand, and the signs of the Zodiac, on the other, are discussed in much detail in Zorzi,
      L’armonia del mondo, I, VIII, 14–21, pp. 1004–34. For the universal structure based on
      Platonic and Kabbalistic teachings, see also ibid., I. VIII, 1–10, pp. 508–40.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 223

Astrology in Francesco Zorzi’s De harmonia mundi               209

of whom had ever explained natural effects through astrological teaching.11
Interestingly, almost the same list of philosophical authorities appears in Book
I of the Disputationes adversus astrologiam, in which Giovanni Pico introduces
the notion of an anti-astrological tradition to demonstrate that astrological pre-
dictions had no validity and intends to prove that all prominent philosophers or
theologians of the past had rejected the very idea of judicial astrology.12 There
are two changes, though: first, in the Disputationes, Pythagoras is listed as the
first major philosopher to criticise astrology, whereas the name of Posidonius
is never mentioned.13 In all other cases, Zorzi simply replicates Pico’s list. He
even pairs up Plato and Aristotle, on the one hand, and Avicenna and Averroes,
on the other. Apart from repeating the sequence taken from the Disputationes,
Zorzi refers to the 900 Conclusiones, in which Pico attempts to reconcile
opposing views, including the philosophical and theological positions of
Thomas Aquinas and John Duns Scotus, Plato and Aristotle, and Avicenna
and Averroes.14 Zorzi goes on to say that unfortunately, ‘these’ authors, that
is, Giovanni Pico, did not work through the medical tradition; otherwise, they
would have come to the opposite conclusions.15 Thus, by claiming that the sim-
ple enumeration of authorities cannot be considered a solid argument, Zorzi
inverts Pico’s idea about the existence of an anti-astrological tradition against
him. He applies the same polemical strategy when stating that the fact that
‘Augustine, Ambrose, Jerome, Origen, John of Damascus, rabbi Salomon, both
rabbis Moses and other Jewish, Greek and Latin teachers’, who had carefully
studied the Scriptures, had never touched upon astrological doctrines does not

      Esculapium et Apollinem, quia facile eis respondebitur.’





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 224

210                                                  Chapter 12

mean that astrology is false as a discipline.16 The passage in question reflects
the corresponding lists of theological authorities from the Disputationes and,
since Jewish authors are included, allegedly from the Heptaplus.17
   Zorzi opposes one of the central arguments expressed in Book I of the
Disputationes. As we remember, to demonstrate that neither Plato nor Aristotle
ever supported astrological predictions, Giovanni Pico claims that their total
silence on astrology is to be regarded as a valid argument against it. Later
reproduced in all post-Piconian texts directed against astrology, including
Savonarola’s Contro gli astrologi, Gianfrancesco Pico’s De rerum praenotione
and even Maximus’ Epistles, this idea was also subjected to severe criticism,
the most remarkable and detailed of which goes back to Lucio Bellanti’s
Responsiones in disputationes Ioannis Pici adversus astrologos. The passage in
question from the De harmonia mundi shows that Zorzi was, without a doubt,
familiar with the debate. He argues that the argument on philosophical silence
through which ‘the authors’ intended to prove the falsity of astrology is not
valid and does not lead to any decent solution. Moreover, Zorzi claims that ref-
erences to silence are an outdated polemical method typical of the scholastic
tradition. Thus, by downgrading his opponents’ position and labelling them
as ‘scholastics’, Zorzi denounces Pico’s and his followers’ argument, which, in
turn, allows him to put forward his own theory.18
   Zorzi’s response is a gradual reversal of what he considers the core of Pico’s
attack. First, he claims that none of the most prominent philosophers denied
the correlation between superior and inferior effects, the central element of
astrological speculation that Pico criticises in the Disputationes.19 He adds that
even if the ‘most secret philosophers’ did not mention that the position, mo-
tion or radiation of celestial bodies determined terrestrial effects, this does not
illustrate their negative attitude towards astrology. In Zorzi’s opinion, the si-
lence on astrological matters was caused only by the limits of the genre of nat-
ural philosophy. He states that in the Meteorologica, Aristotle avoids exploring

## Page 225

Astrology in Francesco Zorzi’s De harmonia mundi                    211

the hidden causes and natural virtues of things and focuses instead on basic
natural philosophical categories, such as motion and distance.20 However,
this is in full conformity with the genre of natural philosophy; on the other
hand, in his other writings of more theoretical and elevated function, Aristotle
support astrology. In order to demonstrate this, Zorzi refers to the Secretum
Secretorum that was falsely attributed to Aristotle in the Middle Ages and, as a
result, significantly contributed to the legitimisation of astrology. Not surpris-
ingly, this foundational element of Pico’s attack on astrology and his quest for
‘pure’ Aristotelianism, devoid of any astrological elements, caused Zorzi’s criti-
cal reaction. Apart from quoting from the Secretum Secretorum, Zorzi backs his
conclusion on the astrological essence of Aristotle’s natural philosophy with
reference to the peripatetic tradition, namely to Arab scholar al-Kindi.21 As pre-
viously demonstrated, al-Kindi, praised as one of the greatest magicians of the
past in Pico della Mirandola’s Oratio de hominis dignitate, later became Pico’s
bête noire in the Disputationes.22 Thus, the corresponding passage of the De
harmonia mundi reflects Zorzi’s strategy of reversing Pico’s arguments.
  With the method in question, Zorzi tries to re-establish the high position of
the prisca theologia concept, the foundation of which had been significantly
shaken by Pico’s Disputationes. By enumerating those amongst the secretiores
philosophi, quos laudatis (‘the most secret philosophers whom you praise’)
who, in his opinion, accepted astrology, Zorzi restores the prisca theologia as
a valid theological doctrine, which justifies astrological studies.23 He states
that Hermes Trismegistus taught others to observe the stars, Hippocrates used
astrology for medical purposes, whilst Democritus, who at first glance down-
graded astrology, did so not because he underestimated it but only because he
acknowledged the difficulty of providing exact mathematical and astrological

212                                                  Chapter 12

calculations.24 Zorzi extends the chain of prisci theologi to Aristotle, Plato and
‘numerous members of the Academy and other secret philosophers’, who,
when exploring the occult, relied primarily on the observation of the stars. He
goes on to claim that according to Plotinus, one’s soul returns to God thanks
to the ascension through Mercury, Venus and the Sun.25 This allows Zorzi to
accomplish his twofold task: by responding to Pico’s critique of the prisca theo-
logia doctrine and his argument that no great philosopher of the past had ever
been engaged in astrological studies, Zorzi rebuilds the very foundations on
which he subsequently restores the image of ‘Christian astrology’ as part of a
harmonised universe.
   Similarly, Zorzi defends Ptolemy’s teaching, which is examined in much
detail in the Disputationes. Given that in the Disputationes, Ptolemy and his
‘ungifted’ followers who misunderstood and misinterpreted Ptolemy’s ideas
and calculations attract Pico’s particular critical attention, with two thirds
of the treatise devoted to a careful treatment of inconsistencies within the
astrological tradition, Zorzi’s intention to advocate for the Alexandrian as-
trologer seems understandable. To demonstrate what legitimate astrological
knowledge is, he reproduces a famous passage from the Tetrabiblos, in which
Ptolemy states that studying the position of celestial bodies is not enough to
produce decent astrological predictions; a good astrologer needs to explore the
time and place of one’s birth as well as customs, food and other features con-
cerned with the region in which a person was born.26 As discussed earlier, in
the Disputationes, Giovanni Pico uses and inverts the same argument to prove
that judicial astrology has no mathematical grounds and, therefore, should
be firmly rejected.27 In contrast, with reference to the passage in question,
Zorzi’s contemporaries, including Marsilio Ficino and another of Pico’s oppo-
nents, Giovanni Pontano, differentiated an astrology of unqualified astrologers

Astrology in Francesco Zorzi’s De harmonia mundi                 213

and an astrology of science based on mathematical calculations and able to
provide reliable horoscopes.28 Thus, quoting from the Tetrabiblos, Zorzi re-
flects a well-known issue, which was hotly debated at the time. In his opinion,
for the benefit of his art, an astrologer must consider every little detail, but
most importantly one’s free will. He again refers to Ptolemy, more precisely
this time to pseudo-Ptolemy’s Centiloquium, which is under particular and
fierce attack in Pico’s Disputationes. Zorzi declares that in his writings, Ptolemy
managed to solve the central problem concerned with astrology, that is, the
opposition between predestination and free will, by introducing an idea that,
due to his proficiency in astrological art, an outstanding astrologer could trans-
form negative celestial influences into positive ones.29 In addition, after re-
storing the leading position of Ptolemy within the astrological tradition, Zorzi
refuses to reject the validity of astrologers who had adopted Ptolemy’s teach-
ing in their commentaries and who are denounced in the Disputationes. The
names of al-Kindi, Haly ibn Riḍwān and other major astrological authorities,
with the use of which Zorzi confirms numerous astrological techniques, ap-
pear throughout the De harmonia mundi.30 As a result, Zorzi achieves his main

## Page 228

214                                                  Chapter 12

goal: by examining step by step Pico’s major polemical arguments, namely on
the unanimous rejection of astrology amongst philosophers and theologians,
the prisca theologia doctrine and the contradictions within a corrupt and dis-
torted astrological tradition, Zorzi eventually reverses these arguments against
Pico and thus advocates for astrology.
  A form of astrology that Zorzi considers reliable presents a mixture of medi-
eval and Neoplatonic elements supplemented, considering Zorzi’s fascination
with the Kabbalah, with magical combinatorics. Following the Neoplatonic tra-
dition, he regards the celestial spheres as animated substances, thus ascribing
to each planet a character of its own and claiming that each planet produces
particular influences on the terrestrial world in accordance with its nature.31
Zorzi argues that even though some planets, such as Saturn and Mars, are tra-
ditionally associated with destructive and bad influences, this has nothing to
do with their allegedly malign nature.32 In Zorzi’s opinion, because the world
was created as universally good, the negative effects usually attributed to the
power of Mars and Saturn occur exclusively due to their conjunctions with
other celestial bodies in particular signs of the Zodiac.33 In this case, Zorzi’s
conclusion is reminiscent of a famous theological doctrine according to which
evil is to be considered not as a universal principle but as a lack of primordial
good. Zorzi goes on to say that the effects produced by the planets are bal-
anced, and the correspondence between the number of celestial spheres and
the octave, both of which consist of eight entities, reveals this universal bal-
ance or harmony planned by God. The pro-astrological argument developed in
the De harmonia mundi aims at reviving what Giovanni Pico and his followers
challenge in their writings: a Christianised version of astrology, whose basic
practices could have been used to prove the essence of Christian teaching. The
analysis of the writings of two of Pico’s other opponents, Giovanni Pontano
and Lucio Bellanti, shows that despite certain differences in polemical strate-
gies, all major respondents to the Disputationes were unanimous in pursuing
the same goal, but with the use of different sources.
  To summarise, in the astrological sections, mostly in Chapter XI of the third
tone of Canticum I of his enormous De harmonia mundi, the Venetian thinker
