                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 229

Astrology in Francesco Zorzi’s De harmonia mundi                  215

Francesco Zorzi provides an explicit response to the anti-astrological claims
expressed in Giovanni Pico’s Disputationes adversus astrologiam divinatricem.
Zorzi’s argument structurally replicates the corresponding passages of Pico’s
treatise and addresses three major polemical points with which Pico della
Mirandola sought to undermine astrology as a legitimate discipline. By reject-
ing the idea that all major philosophers and theologians of the past unani-
mously condemned astrology, and that astrological speculation is based on a
corrupt and distorted tradition, Zorzi reverses these arguments against Pico.
Together with Lucio Bellanti and Giovanni Pontano, he insists on the resto-
ration of the Christianised version of astrology that was severely criticised by
Giovanni Pico and those who supported his anti-astrological attack after the
posthumous publication of the Disputationes in 1496. We do not have enough
evidence to claim without a doubt that Zorzi did not consider the Disputationes
an original work by Pico. At the same time, the analysis has shown that Zorzi
was aware of the debates regarding the authorship of Pico’s last treatise, and
by addressing ‘these authors’, he probably responded not only to Pico but
also to Savonarola and Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola, who, at the time,
were often seen as having appropriated Pico’s arguments into their ideological
agendas. Thus, given the context of the astrological debates, which arose in
Renaissance Italy after the 1496 edition of Giovanni Pico’s last treatise, it is
highly likely that Zorzi, in fact, doubted the authenticity of the Disputationes
adversus astrologiam divinatricem.





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 230

Conclusion

In the biography of Giovanni Pico della Mirandola, Fernand Roulier claims
that the failed Roman dispute of 1486 and the following persecution, which
could have ended badly had Lorenzo de’ Medici not offered his protection, left
a significant mark on the rest of Pico’s legacy. He allegedly lost his fire and
motivation to develop the earlier ideas that were coined in the Conclusiones
and related texts, and eventually, by the time of composing the Disputationes
adversus astrologiam divinatricem, turned into a religious radical deeply in-
fluenced by Girolamo Savonarola.1 Another volte-face, from a Neoplatonic
and Kabbalistic exegete to a philosopher relying on a traditional Aristotelian
framework, was likewise interpreted as a clear sign of Savonarola’s impact at
the late stage of Pico’s intellectual career. This vision of the developments
through which Pico’s itinéraire philosophique was thought to pass originated
soon after his sudden death in 1494. Although in most cases, the tendency to
portray Giovanni Pico as a philosophical youngster, who by the end of his life
got in with the wrong people, had a political and anti-Savonarolan motivation,
it has remained in force for centuries. Both scholarly studies and popular ac-
counts devoted to the life and deeds of the Count of Mirandola continue to
reproduce this image.
   In contrast, as this book argues, considering the lack of historical and textual
evidence to confirm that his late works were written under Savonarola’s spell,
a closer look at their content might allow for a more nuanced understanding
of the evolution of Pico’s thought, and the issue of astrology is particularly
revealing in this regard. This study aimed to reconstruct the development of
Pico’s astrological views from the early Neoplatonic writings and ambitious
theological projects (the Commento and the Trilogy) through the biblical com-
mentaries (the Heptaplus and the Expositiones in Psalmos) to the Disputationes
adversus astrologiam divinatricem. My analysis suggests that regardless of his
interactions with Savonarola, and with evident changes and shifting priori-
ties, Pico’s reasoning was still centred on the same set of topics. In this con-
text, the Disputationes appears to symbolise a continuation of rather than
a drastic rupture with the rest of his legacy. At first, Pico was strongly fasci-
nated with newly discovered sources, namely Kabbalistic literature, Plato and
Neoplatonic writings, and, therefore, relied on them in interpreting astrolog-
ical symbolism, though without explicitly admitting the dependence of one’s
life on the position of celestial bodies. In tracing his views on astrology prior to

1  Roulier, Jean Pic de la Mirandole, pp. 113–14.


© Koninklijke Brill NV, Leiden, 2021 | doi:10.1163/9789004442276_015   Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 231

Conclusion                                                            217

the Disputationes, I explore the reasons behind his shift from earlier strong at-
tachments to the Neoplatonic and Kabbalistic doctrines to the ‘natural science’
mainly understood in Aristotelian and scholastic terms. Between 1489 and
1491, Pico posed for the first time the question of the communication between
two essential philosophical concepts—light and matter—both of which were
essential for astrological speculation. For Pico, the problem in question was
a part of a major philosophical project focused on the reconciliation of Plato
and Aristotle. The competition between those who favoured Platonic philos-
ophy over Aristotelianism, and vice versa, was central to fifteenth-century
Renaissance thought, especially after the revival of the Platonic corpus in the
second half of the fifteenth century.2 What is important to note is that Pico’s
main contribution to the discussion—the De ente et uno, in which he insisted
that Plato and Aristotle had unanimously agreed on God being both One and
Being—was composed in the same period when Pico started investigating the
nature of light and matter. However, Pico failed to combine the notion of ce-
lestial light interpreted within a Neoplatonic framework with Aristotelian and
scholastic physics. The failure ended his reconciliation attempts, whilst the
De ente et uno also marked the reconsideration of Pico’s philosophical method,
which, from that moment on, addressed his intention to purify major figures,
such as Plato and Aristotle, from subsequent interpretations and go back
ad fontes. Thus, in response to Marsilio Ficino, who sought to unify a variety
of doctrines under the banner of prisca theologia, and in contrast to the Pico
of the Conclusiones often nicknamed the princeps concordiae, the Pico of the
De ente et uno scrutinised and challenged the concept of tradition. This means
that Pico’s deviation from Neoplatonic and Kabbalistic sources and his return
to Aristotle and other ‘classical authors’ in the Disputationes should not be re-
garded as surprising or unexpected. On the contrary, it had clear philosophical
and humanist premises and adhered to the idea formulated for the first time in
the De ente et uno of 1491, that is, without any involvement from Savonarola. As
a result, the development of Pico’s astrological views over the period from 1486
through 1493 reflects a more general change in his philosophical positions and
consequently provides a key to reading the Disputationes.
   In his last anti-astrological treatise, Pico develops the new method and
deconstructs the notion of tradition in its astrological, theological and philo-
sophical dimensions. Built on the hypothesis previously tested in the De ente
et uno, this approach also resembles the one applied in Sextus Empiricus’
Adversus mathematicos, some excepts from which, including the chapter

2  On the revival of Plato in fifteenth-century Italy, see James Hankins’ foundational Plato in the
    Italian Renaissance, 2 vols (Leiden; New York; Copenhagen; Cologne: Brill, 1990).





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 232

218                                                 Conclusion

against astrologers, Pico possessed in his private library. The method perme-
ates the whole of the Disputationes and, although at first glance it differs from
what Pico endorses in his earlier writings, the narrative echoes the trends that
dominated Renaissance intellectual life at the turn of the sixteenth century,
meaning that the Count of Mirandola undoubtedly remained at the forefront
of contemporary thought. For instance, working through Ptolemy’s writings
and questioning the existence of a reliable and coherent astrological tradition,
Pico addresses two fundamental issues: the opposition between the two tra-
ditions of scientific knowledge, Greek and Arabic, and the numerous discrep-
ancies in astrological/astronomical data that required corrections. Whilst the
former had already been widely debated in that period but received additional
attention after Pico’s intervention, the latter corresponded to a growing un-
derstanding that the Aristotelian/Ptolemaic cosmology was based on outdated
theoretical grounds. As is well known, numerous fifteenth-century astrono-
mers, including Georg von Peuerbach and Regiomontanus, sought to modify
the mathematical calculations in their theoricae novae planetarum.3 Pico’s
Disputationes once again pointed to this problem, though mostly concerning
astrology, that is, a practical and predictive application of ‘mathematical as-
trology’. Therefore, given the context, it comes as no surprise that the sixteenth
century witnessed an array of efforts to reform astrology in light of new scien-
tific and intellectual developments, and Pico’s attack was one of the texts that
provoked such attempts.
  The Disputationes also firmly denies that Christian teaching could in any
way benefit from astrology. In a manner similar to the one with which he
denounces the validity of the astrological tradition, Pico rejects the prisca
theologia, claiming that superstitious beliefs generated in the East subse-
quently spoiled philosophy and natural knowledge. Besides, he states that no
true theologian had ever seen any value in astrological speculation. In doing
so, he aims to vindicate leading medieval scholastics who, in fact, accepted
astrology or whose anti-astrological stance was not believed to be so clear;
amongst these were Thomas Aquinas and Albert the Great. In the meantime,
some other doctores, including Roger Bacon and Pierre d’Ailly, are subjected
to severe criticism. Finally, in Book III of the Disputationes, Pico consistently
cleanses Aristotelian teaching on natural philosophy of pro-astrological inter-
pretations. He intends to restore what he thought was a ‘purified’ Aristotle,
completely separated from the Aristotelian  ‘turba’, to demonstrate that

3  For an introduction to the pre-Copernican debates, see Before Copernicus: The Cultures and
   Contexts of Scientific Learning in the Fifteenth Century, ed. by Rivka Feldhay and F. Jamil Ragep
   (Montreal & Kingston; London; Chicago: McGill-Queen’s University Press, 2017).





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 233

Conclusion                                                          219

astrology contradicted the very principles of natural philosophy. This purpose
of reconstructing an author’s ‘original’ legacy, truly humanist at its core and
characteristic of the era, determined the way Pico structures his argument
on the Stagirite. Later, in the sixteenth century, the questions of what consti-
tuted Aristotle’s actual views and corpus received a second life, which reveals
that Pico was responsive to the issues that defined the intellectual landscape
of his time. Another point that deserves particular attention with respect to
Book III of the Disputationes is a natural philosophical compromise between
the Platonic and Aristotelian theories of celestial light and matter, a problem
that was important for Pico before the Disputationes and to which he at last
found an elegant and unusual solution.
   In the end, Pico’s thorough examination allowed him to formulate three
primary claims against astrology. First, judicial astrology, which deals with pre-
dictions, is a false form of knowledge. To it, Pico opposes ‘mathematical astrol-
ogy’, which observes natural effects without pretending to predict the future.
Second, astrological techniques are also false because of the erroneous nature
of astrology itself and due to numerous contradictions and errors in astrologi-
cal calculations. Pico insists that most astrological authorities were at variance
with each other regarding the usage of astrological practices. According to
him, a lot of such cases are associated with Ptolemaic terminology, which was
misunderstood by his successors. Third, astrology has no natural philosophical
grounds, whilst all terrestrial effects are of accidental nature and do not de-
pend on the position of stars and planets. On the basis of this assumption, Pico
denies that a human’s life is predestined.
   All these details show that although he changed his mind on a number
of things in the course of almost ten years between the Commento and the
Disputationes, Giovanni Pico della Mirandola remained as intellectually en-
gaged in his last treatise as he had been during the Roman dispute of 1486.
Together with the mysterious circumstances of the author’s death and the
complex socio-political context in which the Disputationes was written and
published, the ambitious character of Pico’s work attracted a wide reception.
Thus, apart from providing an in-depth analysis of Pico’s astrological views,
this book investigates the responses to the Disputationes aroused in the late
fifteenth and early sixteenth centuries. These reactions were mostly centred
around the question of the treatise’s authenticity. On the one hand, Savonarola
and his circle—which included Gianfrancesco Pico della Mirandola, Giovanni
Pico’s nephew, who was responsible for the posthumous publication of the
Disputationes—appropriated Pico’s ideas in order to represent them in a dif-
ferent ideological way and serve their own polemical agenda. On the other,
those advocating for the legitimacy of astrology maintained that the writing





                                                                          Ovanes Akopyan - 978-90-04-44227-6

## Page 234

220                                                 Conclusion

was either composed under Savonarola’s ‘malign’ influence or was significantly
distorted prior to publication. Just as the first group of authors rejected astro-
logical speculation as incompatible with Christian teachings, so the second
stood for reviving a ‘Christianised’ version of astrology, albeit with the use of
diverse sources. However, despite all the doubts concerning the Disputationes’
authorship, as this study argues, there is no evidence at our disposal to accuse
Gianfrancesco Pico, Savonarola or their associates of altering the text of the
Disputationes. At the same time, they did contribute to the dissemination of
anti-astrological ideas in Italy and abroad. The case of Maximus the Greek,
Savonarola’s admirer and Gianfrancesco Pico’s assistant, reveals that the con-
troversies on astrology initiated by the Disputationes reached even the most
distant areas of the European intellectual oikumene.
